Divine Decree

The First Opinion: God Alone Creates Actions, but What of Human Power?
The author presents the first opinion only God's power affects the action, and the Ash'ari-Baqillani sub-disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the shared Ash'ari stance that human power has no effect on the existence of an action, and the internal disagreement between al-Ash'ari and al-Baqillani on whether human power affects the action's moral attribute (kasb).

Core Idea
Human power affects not the action's existence but perhaps its moral character—that is the Ash'ari-Baqillani debate.

This passage sets out the first major position in the debate over human agency: God alone creates the action. The Ash'aris unanimously deny that human power contributes to the action's being, but they disagree on whether it affects the action's attribute of obedience or disobedience. We will unpack this nuanced disagreement and see how al-Baqillani introduces the concept of kasb.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the first opinion
Read the Arabic passage once to see the structure: first the shared opinion, then the sub-disagreement. Note the key figures mentioned.
Pay attention to the terms 'المؤثر' and 'قدرة العبد'.
2
Distinguish the shared premise
Both al-Ash'ari and al-Baqillani agree that human power has no effect on the existence (وجود) of the action. This is the common ground.
The difference lies only in attributes.
3
Understand al-Ash'ari's position on attributes
Al-Ash'ari argues that since human power does not affect existence, it also does not affect any attribute of the action. This is a strict denial.
4
Analyze al-Baqillani's position and his argument
Al-Baqillani says human power does affect one attribute: the moral quality (kasb). He uses the example of movement being shared between obedience and disobedience to argue that the distinguishing attribute comes from human power.
Follow his reasoning: 'ما به المشاركة غير ما به الممايزة'.
5
Reflect on the significance of kasb
Kasb is the term for the human contribution—an attribute, not the essence. This allows human responsibility without compromising God's sole creative power.
This prepares for later discussions on free will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول الأول : إن المؤثر في حصول هذا الفعل ، هو قدرة الله تعالى وليس لقدرة العبد في وجوده أثر . وهذا قول أبي الحسن الأشعري وأكثر أتباعه . كالقاضي أبي بكر الباقلاني وه ابن فورك ، ثم حصل ههنا بين الأشعري ، وبين القاضي ، اختلاف من وجه آخر . فقال الأشعري : قدرة العبد كما لم تؤثر في وجود الفعل البتة، لم تؤثر أيضاً في شيء من صفات ذلك الفعل وقال القاضي : قدرة العبد وإن لم تؤثر في وجود ذلك الفعل ، إلا أنها أثرت في صفة من صفات ذلك الفعل ، وتلك الصفة هي المسماة بالكسب قال : وذلك لأن الحركة التي هي طاعة والحركة التي هي معصية قد اشتركا في كون كل منهما حركة ، وامتازت إحداهما عن الأخرى بكونها طاعة أو معصية . وما به المشاركة غير ما به الممايزة. فثبت : ان كونها حركة غير ، وكونها طاعة أو معصية فذات الحركة ووجودها واقع بقدرة الله تعالى . أما كونها طاعة أو معصية فهو صفة واقعة بقدرة العبد
•
هذا تلخيص مذهب القاضي : على أحسن الوجوه
The first statement: The one who brings about this action is God's power, and the servant's power has no effect in its existence. This is the view of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and most of his followers, such as al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani and Ibn Furak. Then there occurred here between al-Ash'ari and the Judge a disagreement from another aspect. Al-Ash'ari said: Since the servant's power did not affect the existence of the action at all, it also did not affect any of the attributes of that action. And the Judge said: Even if the servant's power did not affect the existence of that action, it did affect an attribute of that action, and that attribute is called acquisition (al-kasb). He said: That is because the movement that is obedience and the movement that is disobedience share in each being a movement, and each is distinguished from the other by being obedience or disobedience. That by which they share is other than that by which they differ. So it is established that its being a movement is other than its being obedience or disobedience. Thus the essence of the movement and its existence are brought about by God's power. As for its being obedience or disobedience, it is an attribute brought about by the servant's power. This is a summary of the view of the Judge in the best manner.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage presents the first opinion among rational people regarding the effect of power in causing an action. It states that the one who brings about the action (المؤثر) is only God's power, and the servant's power has no effect in its existence. This is the view of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and most of his followers, including al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani and Ibn Furak. However, there is a further disagreement between Ash'ari and al-Qadi. Ash'ari holds that since the servant's power does not affect the existence of the action at all, it also does not affect any of its attributes. Al-Qadi, on the other hand, argues that although the servant's power does not affect the existence of the action, it does affect one attribute of it, namely the attribute called acquisition (الكسب). He explains that a movement that is obedience and a movement that is disobedience share in being movements but differ in being obedience or disobedience. That which is shared is distinct from that which differentiates. Therefore, the essence of the movement and its existence come from God’s power, while its being obedience or disobedience is an attribute produced by the servant’s power. The passage concludes by stating that this is a summary of al-Qadi's view in the best manner.

Key Terms
المؤثر
قدرة الله
قدرة العبد
الكسب
طاعة
معصية
المشاركة
الممايزة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that al-Baqillani's view is identical to al-Ash'ari's, both denying any human effect.
Correction: In fact, while both deny human effect on the action's existence, al-Baqillani allows human power to affect the moral attribute (kasb), which al-Ash'ari denies.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the disagreement between al-Ash'ari and al-Qadi al-Baqillani?
A) Whether the servant's power affects the existence of the action.
B) Whether the servant's power affects any attribute of the action, even if not its existence.
C) Whether the action is created by God or by the servant.
D) Whether the acquisition (kasb) is an attribute of the action or something else.

Key Takeaway
The first opinion holds that God alone creates the action, but al-Ash'ari and al-Baqillani differ on whether human power affects the moral attribute of the act, with Baqillani introducing kasb as the human contribution.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that God creates the very existence of your actions, and you only contribute their moral quality, does that make you more or less aware of your responsibility for sin? How does this shift how you think about repentance?
?
If Baqillani is correct that your power affects only the 'obedience' or 'disobedience' tag on an action God already created, then what does that mean for the daily choices you make? Are you truly choosing, or just attributing a label? Let this question press you to examine the sincerity of your intentions.
?
This lesson presents two views: one says you have no effect at all, the other says you have an effect only on the moral attribute. Which view makes you feel more accountable before God? Commit to acting as though your choices really matter, regardless of the theological nuance, because both scholars affirm that you will be judged for obedience and disobedience.

The Second Opinion: Both Powers Are Effective (First Possibility)
Author presents the second opinion and its first probability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second opinion on human action, specifically the view that both God's power and human power are effective, and the first possibility that each is independently effective and their combination is permissible.

Core Idea
Two independent powers can combine on one effect — a permissible possibility in this view.

After presenting the first opinion that only God's power is effective, the author now introduces the second opinion: that both God's power and the servant's power bring about the action. Under this opinion, there are two possibilities. In this lesson, we examine the first possibility: that each power is independently effective, yet they can combine on one effect. This is a key point in the Islamic theology of human agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it first states the second opinion, then mentions two probabilities, and gives only the first probability.
This passage is short but dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two parts of the passage
Divide the passage into: 1) the statement of the second opinion, and 2) the announcement of two probabilities with the first one explained.
3
Understand the first probability
The first probability claims that God's power is independent in effect and the servant's power is also independent, yet their combination on a single effect is permissible. Ask yourself: what does 'independent in effect' mean?
Independent here means each power can produce the effect on its own.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
This view affirms both divine omnipotence and human responsibility. It avoids the problem of two independent causes producing the same effect by deeming it permissible (ja'iz).
This is a sophisticated position in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول الثاني : إن المؤثر في وجود ذلك الفعل هو قدرة الله تعالى ، مع قدرة العبد
·
ثم ههنا احتمالان :
أحدهما : أن يقال : إن قدرة الله تعالى مستقلة بالتأثير وقدرة العبد أيضا مستقلة بالتأثير إلا أن اجتماع المؤثرين المستقلين على الأثر الواحد :
جائز
The second statement: The one who brings about that action is God's power along with the servant's power. Then here there are two probabilities: One of them: that it is said that God's power is independent in effect and the servant's power is also independent in effect, yet the combination of two independent agents on one single effect is permissible.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage presents the second opinion among rational people: that the one who brings about the action (المؤثر) is God's power together with the servant's power. Then it states that there are two probabilities (احتمالان) under this view. The first probability is that God’s power is independent in its effect (مستقلة بالتأثير) and the servant’s power is also independent in its effect, yet the combination of two independent agents on one single effect (اجتماع المؤثرين المستقلين على الأثر الواحد) is permissible (جائز). This touches on the theological question of whether two independent causes can produce the same effect. The passage only gives the first probability; the second probability is not included in this lesson window.

Key Terms
المؤثر
قدرة الله
قدرة العبد
مستقلة بالتأثير
اجتماع المؤثرين المستقلين
جائز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that if two agents are independent, they cannot combine to produce the same effect without conflict.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that their combination is considered permissible (ja'iz) by this position, indicating that independent agents can concur on one effect.

Checkpoint
According to the first possibility of the second opinion, what is said about the power of God and the power of the servant?
A) Both are independent in effect but cannot combine.
B) Both are independent in effect, and their combination is permissible.
C) God's power is the only effective one.
D) The servant's power is a mere acquisition with no real effect.

Key Takeaway
The second opinion holds that both God's power and the servant's power are effective; one possibility is that each is independently effective and their combination on one effect is permissible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you tend to blame it on destiny or your own weakness? How does the teaching that both God's power and your power are independently effective change your sense of personal responsibility? What will you today honestly admit: that you had the power to choose otherwise but chose disobedience?
?
The text says the combination of two independent agents is permissible. In your daily life, do you truly believe that your choices matter even though God's will is sovereign? Identify one decision you have been avoiding because you fear it might conflict with divine decree. How will you now move forward in that matter, trusting that both powers are real?
?
If both powers are independently effective, then you cannot claim 'it was God's will' to excuse your negligence. Think of a recent failure. Write down exactly where you failed to use your God-given power. Now, commit to one concrete action this week that reflects your belief that your power is genuinely effective.

Divine Decree

The Three Opinions All Entail Compulsion (Jabr)
Author declares that three prior opinions share in asserting jabr due to servant's lack of independence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why three different opinions about human action — despite their apparent differences — all lead to determinism (jabr) because they remove independent creative agency from the servant.

Core Idea
Any view denying the servant's independence in creating the act is ultimately a form of jabr.

The author has previously outlined three possible positions on how human actions relate to divine power. In this passage, he drops a bombshell: although they seem distinct, all three share a fatal flaw — none grants the servant true independence in bringing about the act. Let's see why each one collapses into jabr.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal English translation. Notice the author's confident tone as he states his conclusion first.
Pay attention to the phrasing 'مشتركة في القول بالجبر' (share in asserting jabr).
2
Identify the three conditions
The passage lists three scenarios under which the act occurs from the servant. Find each one and note how they differ.
They are: (1) dependence on God's assistance, (2) dependence on power and motive together, (3) necessity when both are present.
3
Analyze why each removes independence
Ask yourself: in each scenario, does the servant have full control over the act? The answer is no — in all three, the act is contingent on external factors.
The key phrase is 'لا يكون العبد مستقلاً بالإيجاد' (the servant is not independent in bringing about).
4
Connect to the conclusion of jabr
Understand that because the servant lacks independence, the act is effectively compelled. The author concludes 'فكان الجبر لازماً' (then compulsion is necessary).
Jabr here means the opposite of free will; the servant is not the true originator.
5
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider why this matters: many theological positions that seem moderate still entail determinism. This is a key insight for the debate on qadar.
The common misunderstanding is that only overtly deterministic views are jabr — but the author shows otherwise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث : إن الأقوال الثلاثة الأول مشتركة في القول بالجبر . لأن بتقدير صحة كل واحد من تلك الأقوال الثلاثة ، لا يكون العبد مستقلاً بالإيجاد . فإن صدور الفعل عن العبد إذا كان موقوفاً على إعانة الله ، أو كان موقوفاً على حصول القدرة مع الداعي ، أو كان بحال متى حصلت القدرة مع الداعية وجب الفعل . ومتى لم يحصلا ، أو لم يحصل واحد منهما امتنع وقوع الفعل فكان الجبر لازماً . فهذا هو القول في تفصيل المذاهب الممكنة في هذه المسألة .
And the third: that the first three opinions share in asserting compulsion (al-jabr). Because assuming the correctness of each one of those three opinions, the servant is not independent in bringing about [the act]. For the occurrence of the act from the servant, if it depends on God's assistance, or depends on the occurrence of power (al-qudra) with the motive (al-dāʿī), or is in a state that when power and motive occur the act becomes necessary (wajaba), and when they do not occur, or one of them does not occur, the occurrence of the act is impossible — then compulsion is necessary. So this is the statement detailing the possible schools in this issue.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage is a classification: the author states that three previous opinions (not fully listed here) are all identical in their implication of jabr (determinism/compulsion). He then explains why: under any of these three opinions, the servant (al-ʿabd) is not independent (mustaqill) in creating (ījād) the act. The three conditions he mentions are: (1) the act depends on God's assistance (iʿānat Allāh), (2) it depends on the combination of power (qudra) and motive (dāʿī) being present, or (3) it is such that when power and motive are present the act is necessary (wajaba) and when one of them is absent the act is impossible (imtanaʿa). In all cases, the servant lacks independent causation, so jabr follows. The author then concludes that this is the detailed account of the possible positions on this issue.

Key Terms
جبر
العبد مستقل بالإيجاد
إعانة الله
القدرة
الداعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only overtly deterministic views (e.g., pure jabr) entail compulsion.
Correction: The author shows that even opinions appealing to divine assistance or conditional necessity also remove true agency from the servant, thus falling under jabr.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do the first three opinions share?
A) They all affirm free will.
B) They all entail compulsion (jabr).
C) They all deny God's assistance.
D) They all require the act to be necessary.

Key Takeaway
Three seemingly distinct opinions on human action all converge on jabr because each denies the servant independent causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If, as the author argues, any dependency on God's assistance negates your independence, do you truly believe you have any real choice in your actions? How does that affect your sense of personal responsibility for your sins? Will you accept that even your sins are compelled by God, or will you resist that conclusion and seek a different account?
?
Consider the statement: 'The servant is not independent in bringing about the act.' Does that thought make you feel grateful (for God's enabling) or frustrated (that you have no control)? Which feeling dominates, and what does that reveal about your heart's attachment to your own autonomy?
?
If you were to fully accept that your every action is ultimately compelled, how would that change your daily prayers? Would you still ask God for help, or would you become passive? What must you do to avoid falling into either despair or fatalism while still affirming God's complete control?

Divine Decree

The Mu'tazili Position Entails Compulsion in Both Cases
Author analyzes the Mu'tazili position and shows it entails jabr whether he says the act is necessary or not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazili doctrine of human action, whether it considers the act necessary or not after power and motive, inevitably leads to compulsion (jabr) and negates human independence.

Core Idea
Whether the act is necessary or not after power and motive, the Mu'tazili ends up affirming compulsion.

This passage is a incisive logical analysis by the author of the Mu'tazili position on human action. He takes the Mu'tazili's acceptance of power and motive as causes and shows that regardless of whether the act is considered necessary or contingent upon those causes, the result is the same: the servant has no real independence. The author then addresses a possible objection and seals the argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two horns of the dilemma
The author sets up a dilemma: the Mu'tazili must either say that the act is necessary after power and motive, or not necessary. Both options are analyzed.
This is a classic dilemma argument.
2
Examine the first horn: if the act is necessary
If the act is necessary, then the chain of motives must end with a God-given motive, leading to jabr. The servant has no independence.
Infinite regress is impossible, so the first motive must come from God.
3
Examine the second horn: if the act is not necessary
If the act is not necessary, then its occurrence is pure coincidence, not within the servant's power. This also results in jabr.
The same conditions can produce different outcomes, so the servant cannot control the act.
4
Consider the objection and final conclusion
An objector might claim to simply affirm occurrence without necessity. The author shows that even then, the occurrence is either with or without necessity, both leading to jabr.
Thus jabr is unavoidable on all assumptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أن ههنا أبحاثاً غامضة في تعيين محل النزاع، لابد من التنبيه عليها . وهي من وجوه : فالبحث الأول : إن المعتزلي إما أن يقول : إن حصول الفعل عقيب مجموع القدرة والداعي : واجب . أو يقول : إنه غير واجب . فإن قال : إنه واجب . فقد بطل الاعتزال بالكلية . لأن تلك الدواعي لا يمكن أن يستند كل واحد منها إلى داعية أخرى ، لامتناع التسلسل والدور . بل لا بد من انتهائها إلى داعية تحصل بفعل الله تعالى . وإذا كان كذلك ، فنقول : عند حصول القدرة والداعية ، لما كان الفعل واجباً ، وعند فقدانها أحدهما لما كان الفعل ممتنعاً : فحينئذ يلزم الجير ، ولم يحصل للعبد استقلال ، لا بالفعل ولا بالترك في شيء . وأما إن قال المعتزلي : إن حصول الفعل عند حصول القدرة مع الداعي : غير واجب . فنقول : فعلى هذا التقدير . عند حصول مجموع القدرة مع الداعي ، يمكن أن يحصل الفعل تارة ، وأن لا يحصل أخرى. لأن كل ما كان ممكناً ، لم يلزم من فرض وقوعه محال . فليفرض أن القدرة مع الداعي كان حاصلا ، واستمرا . ثم تارة حصل الفعل ، وأخرى لم يحصل. فهذا الحصول ما كان من العبد البتة ، بل كان محض الاتفاق من غير سبب . لأن مجموع القدرة والداعي لما حصل بتمامه . عند عدم الفعل تارة . وعند حصوله أخرى ، ولم يتجدد أمر من الأمور عند حدوث الفعل ، حتى يقال : الفعل إنما حدث في ذلك الوقت لأجله . فعلى هذا التقدير يكون حدوث الفعل في ذلك الوقت محض الاتفاق ، ولم يكن ذلك في وسعه ، ولا في اختياره . وهذا أيضاً : محض الجبر . لأنه لا سبيل له البتة إلى إيقاع الفعل ، بل إن اتفق وقوعه مع ذلك القدر من القدرة والداعي ، فقد وقع. وإن لم يتفق وقوعه لم يقع . ولم يكن البتة رجحان أحد الطرفين على الآخر ، بأمر من جهة العبد البتة . فثبت : أن المعتزلي إن قال : إن وقوع الفعل عقيب القدرة والداعي : واجب فقد قال بالجبر ، من حيث لا يشعر به وإن قال : إنه غير واجب . فقد قال أيضاً بالجبر ، من حيث لا يشعر به . فيثبت : أنه لا يمكنه أن يعبر عن نفي الجبر بعبارة معلومة ، إلا وتلك العبارة مشتملة على الجبر . فإن قال قائل : إني أقول : إن عند حصول القدرة والداعي يقع الفعل ، ولا أقول : إن ذلك الوقوع واجب أو غير واجب . وهذا القدر يكفيني في التعبير عن مذهبي . فنقول : هب أنك لا تذكر إلا الوقوع . إلا أنا نقول : ذلك الوقوع . إما أن يكون مع الوجوب، أو لا مع الوجوب . وعلى كل من التقديرين : فالجبر لازم . فكان الجبر لازماً على كل التقديرات .
Know that there are subtle investigations in determining the locus of dispute, which must be pointed out. They are in several aspects. The first investigation: that the Mu'tazili either says that the occurrence of the act after the combination of power (al-qudra) and motive (al-dāʿī) is necessary (wājib), or he says it is not necessary. If he says it is necessary, then Mu'tazilism is entirely invalidated, because those motives cannot each depend on another motive, due to the impossibility of infinite regress (al-tasalsul) and circularity (al-dawr). Rather they must end at a motive that occurs by the act of God Most High. And if it is so, we say: when power and motive are present, since the act is necessary, and when one of them is absent, since the act is impossible, then compulsion (al-jabr) follows, and the servant has no independence, neither in the act nor in the omission in anything. As for if the Mu'tazili says that the occurrence of the act when power and motive are present is not necessary, we say: on this assumption, when the combination of power and motive is present, the act may occur sometimes and not occur at other times. Because everything that is possible (mumkin) does not entail impossibility from assuming its occurrence. So let it be assumed that power with motive was present and continued. Then sometimes the act occurs and sometimes it does not. This occurrence is not from the servant at all, but is pure coincidence (maḥḍ al-ittifāq) without a cause. Because the combination of power and motive occurred completely, with the absence of the act at one time and its occurrence at another, and nothing new occurred at the time of the act such that it could be said that the act occurred at that time because of it. On this assumption, the occurrence of the act at that time is pure coincidence, and it is not within his capacity nor his choice. And this is also pure compulsion (maḥḍ al-jabr), because he has no way at all to bring about the act. Rather, if its occurrence happens to coincide with that measure of power and motive, it occurs; if its occurrence does not coincide, it does not occur. And there is no preference (rujḥān) of one side over the other by anything from the servant at all. So it is established that the Mu'tazili, if he says that the occurrence of the act after power and motive is necessary, he has stated compulsion without realizing it; and if he says it is not necessary, he has also stated compulsion without realizing it. So it is established that he cannot express the negation of compulsion by any known expression without that expression containing compulsion. And if someone says: I say that when power and motive are present the act occurs, and I do not say that that occurrence is necessary or not necessary; this suffices me in expressing my doctrine. We say: Grant that you only mention the occurrence. However we say: that occurrence is either with necessity or without necessity. And on both assumptions, compulsion is necessary. So compulsion was necessary on all assumptions.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage is a detailed objection-and-answer analysis (baḥth) of the Mu'tazili position on human action. The author begins by stating that there are subtle investigations needed to determine the precise locus of dispute. He then presents the first investigation: the Mu'tazili must either say that the act's occurrence after the combination of power (qudra) and motive (dāʿī) is necessary (wājib) or not necessary. 

First case: If he says it is necessary, then Mu'tazilism is invalidated because the chain of motives cannot go back infinitely or circularly; it must end at a motive created directly by God. When power and motive are present the act is necessary, and when absent the act is impossible—thus jabr follows, and the servant has no independence in either doing or refraining.

Second case: If he says it is not necessary, then the act may sometimes occur and sometimes not despite the same conditions. This means the occurrence is pure coincidence (ittifāq) without any cause from the servant. Since nothing new occurs at the moment of action, the act is not within the servant's capacity or choice—again pure jabr.

Thus in both scenarios, jabr is unavoidable. The author then addresses a potential objector who says he simply affirms occurrence without calling it necessary or not. The reply is that the occurrence is still either with necessity or without it, and in either case jabr follows. The conclusion is that compulsion is necessary on all assumptions.

Key Terms
المعتزلي
المجموع
الداعي
التسلسل
الدور
الاتفاق
الوجوب
الاختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the Mu'tazili can avoid jabr by simply describing the act's occurrence without attributing necessity or non-necessity.
Correction: The author shows that even if one only mentions occurrence, it is still either with or without necessity, and both entail jabr.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude about the Mu'tazili's position in both cases?
A) The Mu'tazili affirms free will.
B) The Mu'tazili is consistent with Ash'arism.
C) The Mu'tazili ends up asserting compulsion (jabr) in both cases.
D) The Mu'tazili denies power and motive.

Key Takeaway
No matter what the Mu'tazili says about the necessity of the act after power and motive, he cannot avoid falling into jabr.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you sin, do you ever fool yourself into thinking it was just 'coincidence' or your own doing? How does this passage expose that as self-deception, and what does that mean for how you seek forgiveness?
?
This lesson shows that even the Mu'tazili cannot escape affirming God's control. If you truly internalized that, how would it change your reliance on Allah in your daily choices, especially in moments of temptation?

Author introduces chapter and states thesis of human non-independence.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
You are not the ultimate author of your actions – Allah is.

This lesson begins the first chapter of al-Matalib al-Aliyah, where the author tackles the profound theological question of human agency. He states his central thesis clearly: the servant is not independent in his actions or in creation. But what does 'independent' mean here? And what proofs will he offer? This brief opening sets the stage for a detailed argument that will unfold in the following lessons.

3
Look ahead to the proofs
The author says 'ولنا في هذه المسألة براهين' (And we have proofs in this issue). This signals that the chapter will present multiple proofs. Prepare to follow the arguments.

The First Proof: Inability to Refrain
A logical dichotomy leading to determinism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the dichotomy of ability to refrain leads to a deterministic conclusion.

Core Idea
If a power cannot be withheld, the action is forced.

This lesson introduces the first proof, a deceptively simple logical dichotomy. Before we can argue about free will, we must examine what it means for a power to be truly capable. The author presents a two‑option fork that leads straight to determinism if one prong is accepted.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two‑part structure: either refraining is possible or it is not.
2
Analyze the dichotomy
Identify the two divisions. The first division (possible to refrain) is not pursued here; only the second is explored. Ask: what does it mean for a power to be 'inseparable' from its act?
3
Follow the implication
The author traces the consequence: if the power necessitates the act, then the act is necessary when the power is present and impossible when absent. This is the core of determinism.
4
Consider the conclusion
The passage ends by stating that determinism (al‑jabr) is entailed. Think about whether this is presented as an actual belief or as a premise for further argument.
The common misunderstanding is to treat it as a full endorsement rather than a step in a larger debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الأول القادر على الفعل المخصوص . إما أن يصح منه الترك ، وإما أن لا يصح . والقسم الثاني يقتضي أن تكون تلك القدرة مستلزمة لذلك الفعل . وعند حصول تلك القدرة يجب الفعل ، وعند فقدانها يمتنع الفعل . فكان القول بالجبر لازما
The first proof: The one who is capable of a specific action: either it is possible for him to refrain, or it is not possible. The second division necessitates that that power is inseparable from that action. When that power occurs, the action is necessary, and when it is absent, the action is impossible. So the statement of determinism is necessary.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof. The author sets up a dichotomy: a person capable of a specific action either can refrain from it or cannot. If he cannot refrain (the second division), then the power is inherently connected to the action, meaning the power necessarily produces the action. Thus, when the power is present, the action must occur; when the power is absent, the action cannot occur. The conclusion is that this entails determinism (al-jabr). The proof is a simple logical implication: if the power necessitates the act, then there is no free will. The author is likely using this as a step in a larger argument, possibly a reductio ad absurdum against opponents who deny human independence.

الفعل المخصوص

الجبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is a full endorsement of determinism.
Correction: The proof may be a premise in a larger argument, possibly a reductio ad absurdum against opponents who claim complete human independence.

Checkpoint

B) The power necessitates the action
C) The action is impossible
D) The person is not capable

Key Takeaway
The first proof uses a simple dichotomy to show that if a power cannot be withheld, it forces the action, implying determinism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Commit to examining one habit where you claim 'I can't stop' and ask yourself: is it really impossible, or am I hiding behind a false necessity?
?
This proof shows that if one cannot refrain, determinism follows. Do you live as if your sins are inevitable? When you say 'I couldn't help it,' are you affirming determinism in your own life? Take one sin you often excuse as forced, and today, actively seek the ability to refrain by turning to Allah in du'a and action.
?
The dichotomy in this proof forces a choice: either you can refrain or you cannot. In what area of obedience to Allah do you behave as though you cannot refrain from disobedience? Is that truly the case, or have you surrendered your power to act? Resolve tonight to recite 'La hawla wa la quwwata illa billah' sincerely, reminding yourself that all power is from Allah, and you are never completely unable to choose.

The First Proof: Human Acts Are Not Independent
Al-Razi enumerates a proof using logical divisions of power and determinant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's first proof that human actions cannot be independent of divine causation, based on a logical division of possibility and determinant.

Core Idea
Human acts are ultimately determined by God, not by independent human power.

Al-Razi begins his detailed refutation of human independence in actions with a rigorous logical argument. He systematically divides the possibilities for the agent's power and the determinant of action, showing that all alternatives lead either to predetermination (jabr) or to the denial of God as Maker. The only consistent conclusion is that human acts are determined by a cause external to the agent. This lesson unpacks the steps of this foundational proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof's opening division
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Al-Razi immediately divides the case of the one who has power over an action into two possibilities: either refraining is possible or not. This sets up the logical structure.
The first division is whether refraining is valid (sahha) or not.
2
Examine the first branch: if refraining is impossible
Consider the first branch where refraining is not valid. Al-Razi argues this leads to predetermination (jabr). Ask yourself why that follows because the power entails the action necessarily.
This branch is straightforward: no choice means compulsion.
3
Analyze the second branch: when refraining is possible
This branch is more complex. The preponderance of action over refraining either depends on a determinant (murajjih) or not. Al-Razi shows both sub-branches lead to predetermination or denial of the Maker.
Focus on the logic: if no determinant, preponderance without cause is impossible; if a determinant is required, its source matters.
4
Trace the determinant options
When a determinant is required, it must come from another (God). It cannot come from the servant (leads to infinite regress or predetermination) nor from neither (leads to uncaused event, denying the Maker).
The third option (from another) is the only coherent possibility.
5
Understand the conclusion
Al-Razi concludes that the determinant must be from God, and it necessitates the action. Reflect on how this proof establishes divine determination without denying human responsibility (though not addressed here).
The proof ends with the necessity of the action once the determinant from God is present.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الأول
العلائل الحالة على أن السيد : غير مستقل بنفسه بالفعل والتيك
ولنا في هذه المسألة براهين :
أن نقول :
البرهان الأول
القادر على الفعل المخصوص . إما أن يصح منه الترك ، وإما أن لا يصح . والقسم الثاني يقتضي أن تكون تلك القدرة مستلزمة لذلك الفعل . وعند حصول تلك القدرة يجب الفعل ، وعند فقدانها يمتنع الفعل . فكان القول بالجبر لازما
وأما القسم الأول : فنقول : لما كانت تلك القدرة صالحة للفعل والترك ، كان رجحان أحد الطرفين على الآخر. إما أن يتوقف على مرجح ،
أو لا يتوقف
والقسم الثاني : يقتضي رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن المتساوى على الآخر ، لا لمرجح . وذلك يلزم منه نفي الصانع . وأيضا : فعلى هذا التقدير يكون وقوع الفعل بدلا عن الترك : محض الاتفاق ، ولم يكن مستندا إلى العبد ، فيكون الجبر لازما .
وأما القسم الأول : فنقول : ذلك المرجح . إما أن يكون من العبد ، أومن غيره ، أولا منه ولا من غيره . لا جائز أن يكون من العبد . والا عاد التقسيم الأول فيه ، ويلزم إما التسلسل وإما الجبر
والقسم الثالث : فنقول : أيضا : باطل . لأنه يقتضي جواز حدوث الشيء ، لا لمحدث ، ولا لمؤثر . ويلزم منه نفي الصانع . ويلزم منه أيضا : القول الجبر - على ما بيناه - ولما بطل هذان القسمان ، ثبت : أن ذلك المرجح ، إنما حدث بإحداث الغير. فنقول : ذلك المرجح إنما يكون مرجحا ، إذا اقتضى رجحان جانب الفعل على جانب الترك ، وعند حصول هذا الرجحان يجب الفعل . وذلك لأن طرف الترك ، حال كونه مساويا لطرف الفعل ، كان ممتنع الرجحان . فحال حصوله مرجوحا ، أولى أن يصير ممتنع الرجحان . وإذا
The First Chapter: Evidences (or proofs) that the servant is not independent in action and (the issue?). We have proofs in this issue: we say: The first proof: The one who has power over a specific action: either it is valid for him to refrain, or it is not valid. The second division necessitates that that power entails that action. When that power is attained, the action becomes necessary; when it is absent, the action becomes impossible. So the statement of predetermination (jabr) is necessary. As for the first division: we say: when that power is fit for both action and refraining, then the preponderance of one of the two sides over the other either depends on a determinant (murajjih) or does not depend. The second division necessitates the preponderance of one of the two sides of the equally possible over the other without a determinant. That entails the denial of the Maker. Also: on this assumption, the occurrence of the action instead of refraining would be pure coincidence, and would not be attributed to the servant, so predetermination is necessary. As for the first division: we say: that determinant: either it is from the servant, or from another, or neither from him nor from another. It is not possible that it be from the servant; otherwise the first division would recur in it, leading to either infinite regress or predetermination. The third division: we say: it is also invalid, because it entails the possibility of the occurrence of a thing without an originator or an effecter. It entails the denial of the Maker, and also entails the statement of predetermination—as we have explained. And since these two divisions are invalid, it is established that that determinant only occurs through the causation of another. We say: that determinant is only a determinant if it necessitates the preponderance of the side of action over the side of refraining, and when this preponderance occurs, the action becomes necessary. This is because the side of refraining, when it was equal to the side of action, was impossible of preponderance; so when it becomes outweighed, it is more fitting that it becomes impossible of preponderance. And when...
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof (al-burhan al-awwal) in a series of arguments against human independence in actions. The author begins by dividing the one who has power (al-qadir) over a specific action into two possibilities: either it is valid for him to refrain, or it is not valid. If refraining is not valid, then the power necessarily entails the action, leading to predetermination (jabr). The more interesting case is when refraining is valid. He then subdivides this: the preponderance (rujhan) of one side over the other either depends on a determinant (murajjih) or not. If it does not depend, then the preponderance of one equal possibility over another without a determinant would entail denial of the Maker, and the action would be mere coincidence not attributable to the servant, again leading to jabr. If it does depend on a determinant, the author considers three possibilities: the determinant comes from the servant, from another, or neither. It cannot be from the servant because that would cause infinite regress or jabr. It cannot be neither, as that would allow uncaused events, again denying the Maker and implying jabr. Thus the determinant must come from another (i.e., God). This determinant, when it occurs, necessitates the action because the previously equal side of refraining becomes impossible of preponderance once outweighed. The argument thus concludes that human acts are ultimately determined by a cause external to the agent.

قدرة (power)
جبر (predetermination)
مرجح (determinant)
رجحان (preponderance)
تسلسل (infinite regress)
ممكن (possible)
صانع (Maker)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the proof assumes predetermination from the start, but it actually refutes competing positions to establish that the only coherent alternative is divine determination.
Correction: The proof begins with a neutral division and systematically eliminates alternatives, concluding that divine determination is necessary.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why is it invalid for the determinant to come from neither the servant nor another?
A) It would lead to infinite regress.
B) It would entail the occurrence of something without an originator, implying denial of the Maker.
C) It would imply that the servant is independent.
D) It would make the action necessary.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's first proof shows that human acts cannot be independent because the only coherent alternative is that they are determined by God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your daily choices, do you truly believe that every action you take is ultimately determined by God? How does this affect your sense of personal responsibility?
?
In moments of sin, do you ever attribute your action to your own independent will rather than seeing it as a result of God's decree? How can you reconcile this with Al-Razi's proof?
?
If you accept that your actions are not independent, how might this transform the way you seek to change your habits or repent from sins—knowing that the determinant (murajjih) comes from God alone?

The Inability to Will the Will
The author answers the first question by analyzing human experience of will to show that man is compelled in the form of choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the fact that we cannot create our own will demonstrates that all actions ultimately come from Allah.

Core Idea
We can will actions, but we cannot will the will itself.

The author addresses a common objection: if we feel free in our choices, doesn't that prove independence? In this passage, he dissects human experience to show that while we choose our actions, the very will to choose arises in us without our control. This leads to the conclusion that man is compelled in the form of a free chooser.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author's progression from acknowledging our experience to drawing a crucial distinction.
Pay attention to the words 'مشيئة الفعل' and 'مشيئة الترك'.
2
Identify the key distinction
The author distinguishes between willing an act and willing the will itself. Reflect on why we cannot 'will to will' without an infinite regress.
3
Understand the empirical observation
The author points out that decisive will arises in our hearts spontaneously. Consider how this shows the will is not from us.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The entire chain of will and action is ordered by Allah. Man is compelled while appearing as a free chooser. This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن السؤال الأول : إننا نسلم : أنا نجد من أنفسنا : أنا إن شئنا الفعل : فعلنا . وإن شئنا الترك: تركنا . ولكنا لا نجد من أنفسنا : أنا إن شئنا أن نشأ الفعل : حصلت لنا مشيئة الفعل . وإن شئنا أن نشأ الترك : حصلت لنا مشيئة الترك. وإلا لعاد الكلام في تلك المشيئة . ويلزم افتقارها إلى مشيئة ثالثة . ويلزم التسلسل . بل نجد قطعا ويقينا من أنفسنا : أنه قد يحصل في قلبنا مشيئة الفعل ، لا لأجل مشيئة أخرى سابقة عليها وقد يحصل مرة أخرى مشيئة الترك، لا لأجل مشيئة أخرى سابقة عليها ونجد أيضا : أنه إذا حصلت المشيئة الجازمة للفعل ، في قلوبنا ، فإنه يحصل الفعل لا محالة. وإذا حصلت المشيئة الجازمة للترك في قلوبنا حصل الترك . فإذا اعتبرنا هذه الأمور ، علمنا : أنه ليس حصول المشيئة فينا : بنا . وليس ترتب الفعل على مشيئة الفعل : بنا بل هذه أمور مترتب بعضها على البعض . والمبدأ من خلق الله. فيكون الكل من الله . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن هذا الاعتبار الذي نجده من أنفسنا : من أدل الدلائل على أن الكل من الله ، وأن الانسان مضطر في صورة مختار .
The answer to the first question: We acknowledge that we find from ourselves that if we will the act, we act, and if we will omission, we omit. But we do not find from ourselves that if we will to will the act, the will occurs to us, and if we will to will omission, the will occurs to us. Otherwise, the discourse would return to that will, and it would need a third will, and infinite regress would follow. Rather, we find certainly and with certainty from ourselves that the will for the act occurs in our hearts not because of another preceding will, and again the will for omission occurs not because of another preceding will. And we also find that when the decisive will for the act occurs in our hearts, the act necessarily occurs, and when the decisive will for omission occurs in our hearts, omission occurs. So when we consider these matters, we know that the occurrence of will in us is not from us, and the following of the act upon the will is not from us. Rather, these are matters ordered one after another, and the beginning is from the creation of Allah. So all is from Allah. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned that this consideration which we find from ourselves is among the strongest evidences that all is from Allah, and that man is compelled in the form of a free chooser.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is the author's answer to the first question regarding human free will and responsibility. He concedes that we experience the ability to act or not act according to our will. However, he draws a crucial distinction: we do not experience the ability to create our own will. We cannot 'will to will'. If we could, that would require another will, leading to an infinite regress. Instead, we observe that decisive will for an act arises in our hearts spontaneously, not preceded by another will of ours. And once that decisive will is present, the action follows necessarily. From this empirical observation, the author concludes that the occurrence of will in us is not from our own doing, nor is the consequent action from us. The entire chain of will and action is ordered by Allah's creation. Therefore, this introspection is a strong proof that all is from Allah, and that man is compelled (muḍṭarr) while appearing in the form of a free chooser (ṣūrat mukhtār).

Key Terms
مشيئة
اضطرار
اختيار
تسلسل
مبدأ
خلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that experiencing free will proves total independence from Allah.
Correction: The author shows that the origin of the will itself is beyond our control, thus all is from Allah.

Checkpoint
What is the main evidence the author uses to show that man is compelled?
A) The fact that man cannot create his own will.
B) The fact that man can choose between actions.
C) The fact that motives are created by Allah.
D) The fact that action follows will necessarily.

Key Takeaway
Man is compelled in the form of a free chooser because the will itself is not created by man; all is from Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you genuinely desired to pray or give charity, did you ever stop to think that even that desire was not from you? How does that realization affect your gratitude and humility before Allah?
?
If even your rebellion against Allah occurs by a will He created, does that thought drive you to despair of His mercy or to acknowledge your total dependence on His guidance? What does your reaction reveal about your heart?
?
The author says you are 'compelled in the form of a free chooser.' Does this make you feel less accountable for your sins, or does it magnify the weight of every choice as a gift from Allah for which you must be grateful? Be honest: how does this truth change what you will do today?

6 min
The Self-Awareness of Will and Divine Determinism
The author argues that self-awareness of free will actually proves divine determinism by analyzing the origination of will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the experience of willing an act does not prove free will but rather points to divine determination, and how the infinite regress argument undermines libertarian free will.

Core Idea
You can choose your actions, but you cannot choose your will – that comes from Allah.

A common argument for free will is that we feel we can choose our actions. The author of this text concedes this feeling but then asks a deeper question: can we choose to have a will? By examining the origination of the will itself, the author shows that our experience actually proves determinism. This passage is a masterful use of introspection to defend the Ash'ari position that all acts are from Allah, and humans are 'compelled in the form of a free chooser.'

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once focusing on the overall flow. Then read the English translation to understand the argument.
Pay attention to the logical connectors like 'but', 'otherwise', 'rather'.
2
Follow the initial concession
The author first concedes the empirical observation: we find that if we will an act, we do it. This is the starting point of the argument.
3
Understand the infinite regress objection
The author then points out that we cannot will to have a will; if we could, it would lead to an infinite regress. This is the key undermining move.
Consider why this regress is problematic for free will.
4
Identify the two positive findings
The passage describes two certain findings: (1) the decisive will arises without a preceding will, and (2) when the decisive will occurs, the act follows necessarily. Recognize that these show the will is not under our control.
These findings set up the conclusion.
5
Grasp the conclusion about compulsion
The author concludes that the occurrence of will and its connection to action are not from us but from Allah. Therefore, man is compelled (mudtar) in the form of a free chooser (sura mukhtar).
This is the Ash'ari view of kasb (acquisition).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن السؤال الأول : إننا نسلم : أنا نجد من أنفسنا : أنا إن شتنا الفعل : فعلنا . وإن شئنا الترك: تركنا . ولكنا لا نجد من أنفسنا : أنا إن شئنا أن نشأ الفعل : حصلت لنا مشيئة الفعل . وإن شئنا أن نشأ الترك : حصلت لنا مشيئة الترك. وإلا لعاد الكلام في تلك المشيئة . ويلزم افتقارها إلى مشيئة ثالثة . ويلزم التسلسل . بل نجد قطعا ويقينا من أنفسنا : أنه قد يحصل في قلبنا مشيئة الفعل ، لا لأجل مشيئة أخرى سابقة عليها وقد يحصل مرة أخرى مشيئة الترك، لا لأجل مشيئة أخرى سابقة عليها ونجد أيضا : أنه إذا حصلت المشيئة الجازمة للفعل في قلوبنا ، فإنه يحصل الفعل لا محالة. وإذا حصلت المشيئة الجازمة للترك في قلوبنا حصل الترك . فإذا اعتبرنا هذه الأمور ، علمنا : أنه ليس حصول المشيئة فينا : بنا . وليس ترتب الفعل الحصول على مشيئة الفعل : بنا بل هذه أمور مترتب بعضها على البعض . والمبدأ من خلق الله. فيكون الكل من الله . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن هذا الاعتبار الذي نجده من أنفسنا : من أدل الدلائل على أن الكل من الله ، وأن الانسان مضطر في صورة مختار .
And the answer to the first question: We concede that we find in ourselves that if we will the act, we do it, and if we will omission, we omit it. But we do not find in ourselves that if we will to will the act, the will of the act occurs for us, and if we will to will omission, the will of omission occurs for us. Otherwise the discourse would return to that will, and it would require a third will, and an infinite regress would follow. Rather we find certainly and with certainty in ourselves that the will of the act sometimes occurs in our heart not due to a preceding will, and sometimes the will of omission occurs not due to a preceding will. And we also find that when the decisive will for the act occurs in our hearts, the act necessarily occurs. And when the decisive will for omission occurs in our hearts, omission occurs. So when we consider these matters, we know that the occurrence of will in us is not from us, and the ordering of the act's occurrence upon the will of the act is not from us; rather these are matters ordered one upon another, and the origin is from the creation of God. Thus everything is from God. So it is established by what we mentioned that this consideration we find in ourselves is among the strongest proofs that everything is from God, and that man is compelled in the form of a free chooser.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is an answer to a question about self-awareness of free will. The author first concedes the empirical observation: we find that if we will an act, we do it; if we will omission, we omit. However, a deeper analysis reveals that we do not have control over the origination of the will itself. We cannot will to have a will; that would lead to infinite regress. Experience shows that the decisive will (المشيئة الجازمة) for act or omission arises in our hearts without being preceded by another will, and the act or omission follows necessarily. Thus, the occurrence of the will and its consequent act are not from our own power but are ordered by God. This shows that man is compelled (مضطر) in the form of a free chooser (صورة مختار). The argument uses introspection to argue for determinism.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: The experience of acting according to one's will proves that humans have free will and are the ultimate creators of their actions.
Correction: The author argues that while we do act according to our will, the will itself is not originated by us; it arises without our choice, showing divine determination.

Key Takeaway

Objection and Answer: Human Acts and Divine Power
Objection and answer on human acts and divine power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument that although God creates the servant's power and motivation, the servant is not an independent agent because the act becomes necessary when both are present.

Core Idea
God's creation of power and motive makes human acts necessary, not independent.

This lesson presents an objection to the Ash'ari position on human acts and the author's rebuttal. The objector claims that human agency remains intact despite God creating the tools. The author demonstrates that the necessary connection between power, motive, and the act proves the servant's dependence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the objection and answer unfold naturally.
Notice the structure: objection, then answer with three conditions.
2
Identify the objection's core claim
Find where the objector argues that the act occurs by the servant's power despite being created by God. Understand why he thinks this undermines the author's proof.
The objection hinges on the distinction between causal chain and independence.
3
Follow the author's three-part answer
The author states three conditions: necessity of power, necessity of motivation, and necessity of the combination. Note how each is justified.
These correspond to the three key terms: qudra, da'iya, and the combined state.
4
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that when God creates the combination, the act is necessary; when He does not, it is impossible. Hence the servant is not independent.
The key term 'mustaqill' (independent) is refuted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : عندنا : أن فعل العبد واقع بالقدرة القائمة بالعبد ، وبالداعية القائمة به. وتلك القدرة وتلك الداعية ، إنما حدثت بإيجاد واجب الوجود . فكان فعل العبد مستندا إلى واجب الوجود بهذا الطريق . إلا أن إسناده إلى واجب الوجود ، بهذا الطريق، لا يمنع من وقوعه بقدرة العبد . فثبت : أن الدليل الذي ذكرتم لا يفيد مقصودكم من هذه المسألة الجواب : نحن ندعي أن عند فقدان القدرة والداعي ، يمتنع حصول الفعل . وعند حصولها يجب الفعل . ومتى كان الأمر كذلك ، لم يكن العبد مستقلا بالفعل . أما أنه لا بد من القدرة ، فلأن العاجز عن الفعل ، لا يفعل . وأما أنه لا بد من الداعية ، فلما بينا في البرهان الأول : أن القادر إذا خلا عن الداعية ، امتنع صدور الفعل عنه . وأما عند مجموع القدرة والداعي ، يجب الفعل . فلأن عند حصول هذا المجموع ، يحصل رجحان جانب الفعل . ومتى حصل الرجحان ، فقد حصل الوجوب . للدلائل الجمة التي قررناها في البرهان الأول . فثبت : أن عند فقدان مجموع القدرة مع الداعي ، يمتنع الفعل وثبت : أن عند حصولهما يجب الفعل . فإذا فعل الله ذلك المجموع، وجب الفعل و إذا لم يفعل ذلك المجموع ، امتنع الفعل . فحينئذ لا يكون العبد مستقلا بالتكوين والإيجاد وبالله التوفيق
For it might be said: According to us, the act of the servant occurs by the power residing in the servant and by the motivation residing in him. And that power and that motivation only occur by the creation of the Necessary Being. So the act of the servant depends on the Necessary Being in this way. However, its attribution to the Necessary Being, in this way, does not prevent its occurrence by the servant's power. So it is established that the proof you mentioned does not serve your purpose in this issue. The answer: We claim that when power and motivation are absent, the occurrence of the act is impossible. And when they are present, the act is necessary. And whenever this is the case, the servant is not independent with respect to the act. As for the necessity of power, that is because the one incapable of the act does not act. And as for the necessity of motivation, that is because we have shown in the first proof that when the capable one lacks motivation, the occurrence of the act from him is impossible. And as for when the combination of power and motivation is present, the act is necessary, because when this combination occurs, preponderance of the side of the act occurs. And whenever preponderance occurs, obligation has occurred – according to the abundant proofs we established in the first proof. So it is established that when the combination of power and motivation is absent, the act is impossible, and it is established that when they are present, the act is necessary. So when Allah creates that combination, the act is necessary, and when He does not create that combination, the act is impossible. Then the servant is not independent in bringing into existence and creating. And with Allah is guidance.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This lesson contains an objection and the author's answer. The objector asserts that human acts occur through the servant's own power (qudra) and motivation (da'iya), both created by the Necessary Being. However, this causal chain does not negate the act's occurrence by the servant's power. The objector claims the author's proof fails. The author answers by stating that when power and motivation are absent, the act is impossible; when both are present, the act is necessary. This means the servant is not independent. The author reiterates the first proof: a capable agent without motivation cannot act, and when motivation and power are present, preponderance (rajahan) leads to obligation (wujub). Thus, God's creation of this combination necessitates the act, and its absence prevents it. Therefore, the servant is not an independent agent (mustaqill) in origination (takwin) and creation (ijad).

Correction: The author argues that the necessary connection between the presence of power and motive and the occurrence of the act shows that the servant is not independent; God's creation of that combination determines the act.

The servant is not an independent agent because God's creation of power and motivation makes the act necessary when both are present and impossible when absent.

Human Action: Dependence on Divine Causation
Al-Razi presents an objection and his response regarding human action and divine causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author argues that human action depends on God's creation of ability and motive, thereby negating human independence.

Core Idea
Human action requires God's creation of both ability and motive.

This passage presents a critical objection and response concerning human free will and divine predestination. The objector argues that since ability and motive are created by God, actions are ultimately from God, yet this does not negate human agency. The author counters by showing that without God's creation of these factors, action cannot occur, thus humans are not independent creators.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection in Arabic and translation. Notice the objector's claim that action occurs by the servant's ability and motive, but these are created by God, so the action is ascribed to God without negating the servant's role.
The objector concedes that ability and motive come from God.
2
Identify the author's main claim
Find where the author states the conditional: when ability and motive are absent, action is impossible; when present, action is necessary. This is the core of the response.
3
Analyze the three supporting points
The author explains why ability is necessary, why motive is necessary, and why their combination makes action necessary. Note the reasoning for each.
Ability: an incapable person cannot act. Motive: a capable agent without motive cannot act (from first proof). Combination: preponderance leads to necessity.
4
Understand the conclusion
Read the final sentences: since God creates the combination, He determines action; thus the servant is not independent.
The conclusion directly addresses the objector's attempt to preserve human agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل عندنا أن فعل العبد واقع بالقدرة القائمة بالعبد وبالداعية القائمة به وتلك القدرة وتلك الداعية إنما حدثت بإيجاد واجب الوجود فكان فعل العبد مستندا إلى واجب الوجود بهذا الطريق إلا أن إسناده إلى واجب الوجود بهذا الطريق لا يمنع من وقوعه بقدرة العبد فثبت أن الدليل الذي ذكرتم لا يفيد مقصودكم من هذه المسألة الجواب نحن ندعي أنه عند فقدان القدرة والداعي يمتنع حصول الفعل وعند حصولها يجب الفعل ومتى كان الأمر كذلك لم يكن العبد مستقلا بالفعل أما أنه لا بد من القدرة فلأن العاجز عن الفعل لا يفعل وأما أنه لا بد من الداعية فلما بينا في البرهان الأول أن القادر إذا خلا عن الداعية امتنع صدور الفعل عنه وأما عند مجموع القدرة والداعي يجب الفعل فلأن عند حصول هذا المجموع يحصل رجحان جانب الفعل ومتى حصل الرجحان فقد حصل الوجوب للدلائل الجمة التي قررناها في البرهان الأول فثبت أن عند فقدان مجموع القدرة مع الداعي يمتنع الفعل وثبت أن عند حصولهما يجب الفعل فإذا فعل الله ذلك المجموع وجب الفعل وإذا لم يفعل ذلك المجموع امتنع الفعل فحينئذ لا يكون العبد مستقلا بالتكوين والإيجاد
If it is said: According to us, the action of the servant occurs by the ability (al-qudra) residing in the servant and by the motive (al-dā'iya) residing in him. That ability and that motive only come into existence by the creation of the Necessary Existent (wājib al-wujūd). So the servant's action is ascribed to the Necessary Existent in this way. However, its ascription to the Necessary Existent in this way does not prevent it from occurring by the servant's ability. Thus it is established that the proof you mentioned does not achieve your purpose in this issue. The answer: We claim that when ability and motive are absent, the occurrence of the action is impossible; and when they are present, the action is necessary. Whenever the matter is so, the servant is not independent in the action. As for the necessity of ability, it is because the one incapable of an action does not perform it. As for the necessity of motive, it is because we explained in the first proof that the capable agent, if devoid of motive, the occurrence of the action from him is impossible. As for when ability and motive are combined, the action is necessary, because when this combination occurs, the preponderance of the side of action occurs. And when preponderance occurs, necessity occurs, due to the many evidences we established in the first proof. Thus it is established that when the combination of ability and motive is absent, the action is impossible; and it is established that when they both are present, the action is necessary. So if God creates that combination, the action is necessary; and if He does not create that combination, the action is impossible. Then the servant is not independent in bringing into existence and creating.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the nature of human actions and their relation to divine causation. The objector argues that although the servant's action occurs by his own ability and motive, these two are themselves created by God, so the action ultimately originates from God. Yet this does not negate the action being truly by the servant's power. The author responds by first asserting that when ability and motive are absent, action is impossible, and when they are present, action is necessary. This establishes that the servant is not independent. The author then explains each part: ability is necessary because an incapable person cannot act; motive is necessary because a capable agent without motive cannot act, as shown in the first proof. When both are combined, action becomes necessary because preponderance of action occurs, leading to necessity. Therefore, since God creates the combination, He determines whether the action occurs. Thus the servant is not an independent creator of his actions.

Refutation of the Objection of the Sick Person
Al-Razi refutes the claim that resolve does not necessitate action by giving the example of the sick person.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi refutes the claim that a sick person's hesitation disproves determinism, showing that conflicting motives preserve causal necessity.

Core Idea
The sick person's hesitation proves determinism, not free will, because motives always act by necessity.

This passage presents a classic objection against deterministic views of human action: if a sick person desires food but refrains, doesn't that show freedom? Al-Razi dismantles this objection by revealing the hidden assumption—that the initial motive remains decisive when it actually gets opposed. The lesson will walk through the objection, the misunderstanding, and the true mechanism of conflicting motives.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the objector's claim and al-Razi's refutation.
Notice that al-Razi begins by calling it a 'misunderstanding'.
2
Identify the two motives
Find the two conflicting motives in the sick person: the desire for food and the belief in harm. Understand why the second motive outweighs the first.
The belief in harm is the 'second motive' that opposes the initial desire.
3
Understand the concept of ta'āruḍ
Learn that ta'āruḍ (opposition) weakens the first motive, making it no longer decisive (jāzima) and free from weakness (futūr).
This is the key mechanism that prevents the action, not free will.
4
Recognize the necessity preserved
Realize that al-Razi insists that without the opposing motive, the action would necessarily occur. Thus, causal necessity remains intact.
The objector's example actually supports determinism.
5
Reflect on the refutation
Consider how this explanation refutes the objector's claim that resolve does not necessitate action. The resolve was not decisive due to opposition.
This strengthens the deterministic position by explaining apparent counterexamples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : المريض قد يميل طبعه إلى أكل طعام مخصوص، ومعه يتركه . فعلمنا : أن حصول الفعل عقيب هذا العزم غير واجب . فنقول : هذا سوء فهم قررناه . وذلك لأن المريض إذا دعته شهوته إلى أكل الطعام . فهو إنما يمتنع من أكله ، إذا اعتقد أن له في أكله ضررا ، يزيد على اللذة الحاصلة بأكله . فلولا أن تلك الداعية صارت متعارضة بهذه الداعية الثانية ، وإلا لحصل ذلك الفعل لا محالة . إلا أنه لما حصل هذا التعارض . فعند ذلك لا تبقى الداعية الأولى جازمة خالية عن الفتور . فلا جرم لم يترتب عليه الفعل والأثر .
If they say: The sick person may have his nature inclined to a particular food, yet he leaves it. So we know that the occurrence of the action following this resolve is not necessary. We say: This is a misunderstanding that we have established. This is because the sick person, when his appetite calls him to eat the food, he only refrains from eating it if he believes that in eating it there is a harm that outweighs the pleasure resulting from eating it. Were it not that that motive became opposed by this second motive, the action would necessarily occur. But when this opposition occurs, then the first motive does not remain decisive and free of weakness. Thus, the action and its effect do not follow from it.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The objector argues that a sick person may have a natural inclination toward a specific food yet still refrain from eating it, claiming this proves that resolve (ʿazm) does not necessitate action, thereby challenging determinism. Al-Razi responds by clarifying the misunderstanding (sūʾ fahm). He explains that the sick person only refrains when he believes the harm of eating outweighs the pleasure; thus the initial motive (dāʿiya) is countered by a second, stronger motive. The opposition (taʿāruḍ) weakens the first motive, so it no longer remains decisive (jāzima) and free from weakness (futūr). Therefore, the action does not occur, but this is due to conflicting motives, not to free will. The necessity is preserved because, absent opposition, the action would inevitably follow the decisive motive.

Key Terms
الزعم / resolve
الداعية / motive
التعارض / opposition
الفتور / weakness

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The sick person's hesitation proves that humans have free will to act against their desires, refuting determinism.
Correction: The hesitation actually results from conflicting motives—a stronger belief in harm overrides the initial desire. This supports determinism because a decisive motive (the stronger one) still governs the outcome.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi's response, why does the sick person refrain from eating the food he desires?
A) Because he has free will to choose against his desire.
B) Because a stronger belief in harm opposes the initial motive, weakening it.
C) Because the initial motive disappears completely.
D) Because he has no control over his actions.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi shows that action always follows a decisive motive; hesitation reveals opposing motives, not free will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you resist a temptation, do you truly believe it is your own free will that saves you, or do you see the hand of Allah placing a stronger motive in your heart? Which belief humbles you more in that moment?
?
Think of a sin you committed while knowing its harm. What was the motive that outweighed your fear of Allah? How will you ensure, from this lesson, that the opposing motive (fear of harm) becomes decisive next time?
?
Al-Razi says action necessarily follows the decisive motive. If you find yourself procrastinating in prayer or other obligations, what does that reveal about your motive for Allah's command? Are you willing to strengthen it until it becomes decisive?
