The Necessary Sequence of Action: Al-Razi's Deterministic Proof
Al-Razi resumes his proof that actions are compelled, summarizing the necessary sequence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's argument that human actions follow a necessary chain of stages, leaving no room for free choice.

Core Idea
Perception, inclination, consensus, action—each stage necessarily follows the last.

Al-Razi completes his deterministic proof by showing that the stages of action are linked by necessity. From the initial perception of benefit or harm, through inclination and consensus, the final action is compelled. This passage lays out the entire chain, concluding that the ordering is necessary. Let's trace each step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Try to grasp the overall argument without overanalyzing.
2
Identify the key stages
Look for the sequence: perception (tasawwur), inclination/aversion, decisive seeking/complete inclination, decisive consensus (ijma' jazim), and finally action. Note how each stage is defined.
3
Examine the conditional logic
Notice that Al-Razi mentions opposing perceptions that can cancel the process. Understand when the chain is interrupted.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Al-Razi concludes that each stage necessarily follows its predecessor. This is the core of his determinist argument.
The phrase 'tartub daruri' (necessary ordering) is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلنرجع إلى تقرير البرهان على أن أفعال العباد واقعة على سبيل الاضطرار . فنقول : قد بينا أنه إذا حصل التصور ، فإن كان التصور نافعا ، مال الطبع إليه . شاء الإنسان أم أبى . إلا أن يحصل هناك معارض . وهو أن يتصور تصورا آخر يوجب ضد مقتضى التصور الأول . إلا أن على هذا التقدير لا يبقى التصور الأول تصورا لكون ذلك الشيء نافعا . وأما إن كان ذلك التصور ضارا ، حصلت النفرة . شاء الإنسان ، أو أبي . إلا إذا حصل التعارض المذكور. وحينئذ لا يبقى اعتقاد كونه ضارا . و إن لم يحصل ، لا اعتقاد كونه نافعا ، ولا اعتقاد كونه ضارا . فحينئذ لا يحصل الطلب ولا الحرب ثم بينا : أنه إذا حصل الطلب الجازم ، والميل التام ، ترتب عليه إجماع جازم. على أن لابد من إيجاده. و إذا حصلت النفرة التامة ، ترتب عليه إجماع جازم. على أنه لا بد من تركه. وإذا حصل هذا الإجماع التام الجازم، حصل الفعل لا محالة . فثبت : أن ترتب كل واحد من هذه المراتب على ما قبله : ترتب ضروري .
Let us return to the formulation of the proof that the actions of servants occur in a compelled manner. We say: We have shown that when perception occurs, if the perception is of something beneficial, nature inclines to it, whether the human wills or not, unless there is an opposing perception that requires the opposite of what the first perception demands. But in that case, the first perception does not remain a perception of that thing being beneficial. If the perception is of something harmful, aversion occurs, whether the human wills or not, unless the mentioned opposition occurs. Then the belief that it is harmful does not remain. And if neither belief (beneficial nor harmful) occurs, then no seeking and no fleeing occurs. Then we showed: when decisive seeking and complete inclination occur, a decisive consensus (or resolved will) follows necessitating its existence. And when complete aversion occurs, a decisive consensus follows necessitating its abandonment. And when this complete decisive consensus occurs, the action necessarily occurs. So it is established that the ordering of each of these ranks upon what precedes it is a necessary ordering.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is the resumption and completion of the proof (burhān) that human actions are compelled (iḍṭirārī). Al-Razi summarizes the earlier stages: once perception (taṣawwur) occurs, if the perceived object is beneficial, the nature (ṭabʿ) inclines inevitably unless an opposing perception cancels it; similarly for harm. If neither belief holds, no action follows. Then he adds a key step: from decisive seeking (ṭalab jāzim) and complete inclination (mayl tāmm) proceeds a decisive consensus (ijmāʿ jāzim) that the action must be done; similarly for aversion, a consensus to abandon. Once that consensus is complete and decisive, the action necessarily follows. Therefore the entire sequence is necessary (ḍarūrī), leaving no room for free will. This is the core of the determinist argument.

Key Terms
الاضطرار
التصور
الطلب الجازم
الميل التام
الإجماع الجازم
الترتب الضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof denies that humans experience any sense of choice.
Correction: Al-Razi's argument does not deny the subjective experience of choice, but argues that the choice itself is determined by prior causes (perception, inclination, etc.). The experience of deciding is part of the necessary sequence.

Checkpoint
What is the immediate result of complete inclination and decisive seeking, according to al-Razi?
A) The action occurs without any further condition.
B) A decisive consensus (ijmāʿ jāzim) that the action must be done.
C) The person may still choose not to act.
D) The perception changes.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's proof shows that every step from perception to action follows necessarily, making human actions ultimately compelled.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your every action—even your desire to repent or draw closer to Allah—is compelled by a necessary chain, how does that change the way you approach tawbah? Do you still feel the weight of your sins, or does it feel like you are just a passive observer of your own choices?
?
Al-Razi says that when you perceive something as harmful, aversion occurs 'whether you will or not.' Think of a deep-seated habit you have struggled to break. Did you ever sense that your desire to change was itself being overridden by something within you? After this lesson, can you honestly say you believe you could have chosen otherwise at that moment?
?
The proof culminates in action necessarily following a decisive consensus. When you stand in prayer and your mind wanders, whose fault is that? If the wandering is caused by prior perceptions and inclinations beyond your immediate will, can you truly be held accountable? What does that mean for your relationship with the One who commands your heart?

Al-Razi's Refutation of the Servant's Role in First Perception
Al-Razi refutes the claim that the servant can acquire the first perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that the servant produces the first perception leads to infinite regress or circularity, and how this refutes the objection.

Core Idea
If the servant causes any perception, the chain never starts—it loops or stretches forever.

Al-Razi anticipates an objection: perhaps the servant only produces the initial perception, which then triggers voluntary actions. He dismantles this with two arguments, the second being a devastating regress argument. Let's see how he shows that the very idea of a servant-initiated first perception is self-defeating.

Guided Path
1
Context: The objection being refuted
Read the opening sentence to understand the claim Al-Razi is responding to: that the servant's power produces the first perception.
Notice the phrase 'الواقع بقدرة العبد هو تحصيل التصور'.
2
First argument: A promissory proof
Al-Razi mentions that he will later prove no perception can occur by the servant's power. This is a forward reference.
This argument is not detailed here; it's a placeholder.
3
Second argument: The regress/circularity
Focus on the logical chain: if the servant produces a perception, that perception itself involves four ranks and ends in another perception. If the servant produces that too, endless regress or a circle results.
The four ranks refer to a previous discussion about the elements of action.
4
Conclusion: The first perception is compelled
Al-Razi concludes that the first perception must be involuntary (ḍarūrī) in the heart, not by the servant's choice.
This establishes a deterministic foundation for all voluntary actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس لأحد أن يقول : الواقع بقدرة العبد هو تحصيل التصور ، الذي هو المبدأ الأول . لأنا نقول : هذا باطل من وجهين : الأول : إنا سنقيم الدلالة على أنه لا يمكن أن يقع شيء من المعلومات والتصورات بقدرة العبد . الثاني : إن ذلك التصور الذي حصل بفعل العبد ، حصل في تكوينه تلك المراتب الأربعة المذكورة فانتهت إلى تصور آخر . فإن كان ذلك أيضا بفعله ، لزم إما التسلسل وإما الدور . وهما باطلان . ولما بطل ذلك ، ثبت انتهاء هذه التصورات إلى تصور أول ، هو المبدأ للأفعال الاختيارية لذلك الحيوان . وثبت : أن حصول ذلك التصور ليس باختيار العبد ، بل هو حاصل في قلبه ، على سبيل الاضطرار .
And no one may say: what occurs by the power of the servant is the acquisition (taḥṣīl) of the perception, which is the first principle. For we say: This is false in two ways: First: We will establish the proof that no piece of knowledge or perception can occur by the servant's power. Second: That perception which occurred by the servant's action—its formation itself involves the aforementioned four ranks, ending in another perception. If that too is by his action, then either infinite regress or circularity necessarily follows, both of which are false. When this is invalidated, it is established that these perceptions terminate at a first perception which is the principle of the voluntary actions of that animal. And it is established that the occurrence of that perception is not by the servant's choice, but rather occurs in his heart in a compelled manner.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage contains a refutation of a potential objection: that the servant's power (qudra) only extends to producing the initial perception (al-taṣawwur al-awwal), which then starts the causal chain. Al-Razi gives two rebuttals. First, he promises to prove later that no perception can be produced by the servant's power (a separate argument). Second, he argues that even if the servant produces a perception, its own formation would involve the same four ranks (tasawwur, attraction, etc.), leading to another perception, and so on. This forces either an infinite regress (tasalsul) or a circle (dawr), both impossible. Therefore, the chain must terminate at a first perception that occurs necessarily (iḍṭirārī) in the heart, not by the servant's choice. This refutation reinforces the deterministic stance by removing any role for the servant in initiating action.

Key Terms
قدرة العبد / servant's power
التصور الأول / first perception
التسلسل / infinite regress
الدور / circularity
الاضطرار / compulsion

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if the servant only produces the very first perception, the chain of voluntary actions can start without involving God's direct creation.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that even that first perception, if produced by the servant, would itself involve further perceptions, leading to an impossible infinite regress or circularity, so the first perception must be compelled and not servant-produced.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Razi's second argument, what logical problem arises if the servant produces a perception?
A) The perception is not real.
B) It leads to infinite regress or circularity.
C) It contradicts God's power.
D) The four ranks would not apply.

Key Takeaway
The first perception cannot be produced by the servant; it occurs by compulsion, establishing a deterministic foundation for all voluntary actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own beliefs and initial thoughts, can you identify any that you genuinely chose to have? Or are they forced upon you by your nature, environment, or God? How does this lesson challenge your sense of ownership over your most basic perceptions?
?
Al-Razi argues that if you could produce the first perception, it would lead to an infinite regress. Does this logical proof unsettle your everyday feeling of being the originator of your own thoughts? Are you willing to admit that even your first glance at this text was not fully under your control?
?
The lesson concludes that the first perception occurs in the heart by compulsion. How does this make you feel about your responsibility for your beliefs? Will you now examine whether your faith is something you actively chose or something that was placed in your heart? What would it mean to submit to that reality?

The Necessary Ordering of Conception to Action
Describes the psychological chain from conception to action as necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the necessary causal chain of psychological states that leads from conception to action, as argued in the context of qadar.

Core Idea
From conception to action, every stage necessarily follows the previous one.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah explains how human actions are deterministically linked to prior conceptions of benefit and harm. Understanding this chain is crucial for the qadar discussion on whether actions are compelled. The author presents a psychological process that leaves no room for free choice once the initial conception is formed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First read the Arabic and translation to get the flow of the argument.
Note the terms for each stage.
2
Identify the stages
Map out the chain: conception of benefit/harm → desire/aversion → resolve → action.
Notice how the text says 'when decisive seeking occurs, a decisive consensus is entailed...'
3
Understand the necessary ordering
The text emphasizes that each stage is necessarily entailed by the previous one, leaving no room for optional choice.
This is a deterministic view.
4
Reflect on the qadar context
Consider why this argument matters for the debate on predestination: it suggests human actions are compelled by prior cognitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يتصور تصورا آخر يوجب ضد مقتضى التصور الأول. إلا أن على هذا التقدير لا يبقى التصور الأول تصورا لكون ذلك الشيء نافعا. وأما إن كان ذلك التصور ضارا، حصلت النفرة. شاء الإنسان أو أبي. إلا إذا حصل التعارض المذكور. وحينئذ لا يبقى اعتقاد كونه ضارا. وإن لم يحصل، لا اعتقاد كونه نافعا، ولا اعتقاد كونه ضارا. فحينئذ لا يحصل الطلب ولا الحرب. ثم بينا: أنه إذا حصل الطلب الجازم، والميل التام، ترتب عليه إجماع جازم على أن لابد من إيجاده. وإذا حصلت النفرة التامة، ترتب عليه إجماع جازم على أنه لا بد من تركه. وإذا حصل هذا الإجماع التام الجازم، حصل الفعل لا محالة. فثبت: أن ترتب كل واحد من هذه المراتب على ما قبله: ترتب ضروري.
He conceives another conception that necessitates the opposite of the requirement of the first conception. Except that on this assumption, the first conception no longer remains a conception of that thing being beneficial. And if that conception is harmful, aversion occurs, whether the human wills or refuses, except if the mentioned conflict occurs. Then the belief that it is harmful does not remain. And if it does not occur, there is no belief that it is beneficial nor a belief that it is harmful. Then neither seeking nor war occurs. Then we explained: when decisive seeking and complete inclination occur, a decisive consensus is entailed upon it that it must be brought about. And when complete aversion occurs, a decisive consensus is entailed upon it that it must be left. And when this complete decisive consensus occurs, the action necessarily occurs. So it is established: that the ordering of each of these stages upon what precedes it is a necessary ordering.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage describes the psychological process leading to action. It begins with a conception (تصور) that either presents something as beneficial or harmful. If beneficial, desire (طلب) arises; if harmful, aversion (نفرة) occurs, unless there is a conflicting conception that negates the initial belief. Once desire or aversion is decisive and complete, it entails a firm resolve (إجماع جازم) that the action must be done or avoided. This resolve necessarily results in the action. The author emphasizes that each stage follows the previous one necessarily (ترتب ضروري), establishing a deterministic chain.

Key Terms
تصور (conception)
نفع (benefit)
ضرر (harm)
طلب (seeking)
نفرة (aversion)
إجماع (resolve/jazm)
ترتب (ordering)
ضروري (necessary)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that humans freely choose between desire and aversion, but this passage shows that the chain is mechanically determined by the initial conception and subsequent beliefs.
Correction: The passage asserts that once a conception of benefit or harm is established without conflict, desire or aversion necessarily follows, and from that, resolve and action are entailed. There is no role for independent choice.

Checkpoint

A) A decisive desire to seek it
B) A complete aversion and then resolve to avoid it
C) No change, since the person may still choose
D) Immediate action without resolve

Key Takeaway
The psychological journey from conception to action is a necessary, deterministic chain in which each stage inevitably leads to the next, leaving no room for free choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a moment when you felt a strong aversion to praying or doing good. Did you experience that aversion as something you chose, or as something that came over you involuntarily? If it was involuntary, where does your responsibility lie?

Initial Conception Not by the Servant's Power
Refutes the claim that the initial conception is within the servant's power using two proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the initial conception cannot be within the servant's power, based on the impossibility of infinite regress and circularity.

Core Idea
The first conception is compelled, not chosen — otherwise an infinite chain.

The author now addresses a counterargument: maybe the servant produces the initial conception. He gives two rebuttals. The second is a logical demonstration that if the initial conception were by the servant, we would have either an infinite regress or circularity, both impossible. Therefore, the initial conception must be necessary and not voluntary.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation.
Notice the concise style.
2
Identify the two proofs
The passage presents two proofs against the objection. The first is promised, the second is given in detail.
Look for 'الأول' and 'الثاني'.
3
Trace the regress argument
Focus on the second proof: if each conception were by the servant, the chain would regress infinitely or be circular.
Think of the four mentioned stages.
4
Understand the conclusion
The initial conception is necessary, subsequent stages follow necessarily, so humans are compelled.
This supports the Ash'ari position on qadar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس لأحد أن يقول: الواقع بقدرة العبد هو تحصيل التصور، الذي هو المبدأ الأول. لأنا نقول: هذا باطل من وجهين: الأول: إنا سنقيم الدلالة على أنه لا يمكن أن يقع شيء من المعلوم والتَّصوُّرات بقدرة العبد. الثاني: إن ذلك التصور الذي حصل بفعل العبد، حصل في تكوينه تلك المراتب الأربعة المذكورة فانتهت إلى تصور آخر. فإن كان ذلك أيضا بفعله، لزم إما التسلسل وإما الدور. وهما باطلان. ولما بطل ذلك، ثبت انتهاء هذه التصورات إلى تصور أول، هو المبدأ للأفعال الاختيارية، لذلك الحيوان. وثبت: أن حصول ذلك التصور ليس باختيار العبد، بل هو حاصل في قلبه، على سبيل الاضطرار. ثم إذا حصل ذلك التصور، ترتبت تلك المراتب بعضها على بعض، بالغة ما بلغت، ترتبا واجبا لازما اضطراريا. وعند هذا يظهر أن الإنسان مضطر في اختياره، وان جميع أفعال العباد: إما أفعال الله تعالى، أو موجبات أفعال الله تعالى. وعلى التقديرين فالمطلوب حاصل.
And no one may say: that which occurs by the power of the servant is the acquisition of the conception, which is the first principle. For we say: This is false in two respects: First: we will establish the proof that nothing from the known things and conceptions can occur by the power of the servant. Second: that conception which occurred by the action of the servant, those four mentioned stages occurred in its formation, and it ended at another conception. If that also is by his action, then either infinite regress or circularity is necessary, and both are false. And when that is false, it is established that these conceptions end at a first conception which is the principle for voluntary actions for that living being. And it is established that the occurrence of that conception is not by the servant's choice, but rather it occurs in his heart necessarily. Then when that conception occurs, those stages are ordered upon each other, no matter how many, in a necessary, binding, and inevitable order. And at this it appears that the human is compelled in his choice, and that all actions of the servants are either actions of Allah most high or necessitated by the actions of Allah most high. In both cases, the desired objective is achieved.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage refutes an objection that the initial conception (the first step in the chain) is within the servant's power. The author gives two arguments. First, he promises a separate proof that no conception can be produced by the servant. Second, he shows that if the initial conception were by the servant, then the chain of conceptions would either regress infinitely or become circular—both impossible. Therefore, the initial conception is not voluntary but occurs necessarily. Once that conception occurs, the subsequent stages follow necessarily, proving that humans are compelled (مضطر) in their choices. Thus, all actions are either directly from God or necessitated by His actions.

Key Terms
بقدرة العبد (by the servant's power)
تحصيل التصور (acquisition of the conception)
مبدأ أول (first principle)
تسلسل (infinite regress)
دور (circularity)
اضطرار (necessity)
مضطر (compelled)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The servant freely chooses the initial conception.
Correction: It cannot be freely chosen because that would lead to infinite regress or circularity; it occurs necessarily.

Checkpoint
What is the second proof against the claim that the initial conception is by the servant's power?
A) It would require an infinite regress or circularity of conceptions
B) The servant is unable to conceive anything
C) The four stages cannot be produced by the servant
D) The servant's power is limited to external actions

Key Takeaway
The initial conception is not chosen by the servant but occurs necessarily, and this proves that humans are compelled in their choices.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Empirical Proof of Compulsion and the Example of the Angry Man
Introspective proof and illustrative example of determinism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how introspection and the example of the angry man demonstrate that human actions are compelled by a chain of causes beyond one's control.

Core Idea
Every action is chained to external contingencies beyond your control.

In this passage, the author builds an empirical argument for determinism (al-jabr). He invites you to introspect: every movement you make is caused by a will, which is caused by a belief, which is caused by a perception, which is triggered by an external event. The example of the angry man shows how six innate factors and external circumstances combine to make his anger inevitable. This lesson traces that chain and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how the passage moves from general introspection to a concrete example.
2
Trace the introspective chain
Follow the chain: movement → will → belief → perception → external event. Ask yourself: are any of these steps under your complete control?
The author claims each step is caused by the previous, ending in an external contingency.
3
Identify the six innate factors in the example
In the example of the angry man, list the six factors mentioned: soul, temperament, limbs, upbringing, political necessity, and the contingent external events. See how each is beyond the person's choice.
The example is meant to make the abstract chain concrete.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why the author says that when external contingent causes combine with these six factors, the doctrine of compulsion becomes necessary. Do you agree that no free choice remains?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن كل من رجع نفسه، واعتبر أحوال أفعاله وأعماله: علم قطعا ويقينا أن الأمر كما ذكرناه. وذلك لأنه إنما تحرك إلى جهة كذا لأنه أراد تلك الحركة، وإنما أراد الحركة إلى تلك الجهة لأنه اعتقد أن له في تلك الحركة منفعة. وإنما حصل ذلك الاعتقاد لأنه رأى شيئا فصارت تلك الرؤية سببا لتذكره أن له في ذلك الفعل منفعة. وهلم جرا إلى جميع المراتب. وبالجملة: فأفعال الجوارح مترتبة على أفعال القلوب، وأفعال القلوب يستند بعضها إلى بعض. والأخير يستند إلى أمور خارجية اتفاقية. مثل: إن وقع بصره على شيء فتذكر شيئا، أو سمع صوتا فتذكر شيئا مع العلم الضروري بأن تلك الاتفاقات الخارجية خارجة عن وسعه وطاقته. ثم إنا قد ذكرنا تلك الأشياء الستة التي بسببها تختلف أحوال الإنسان في عقائده وفي أخلاقه. فإذا انضمت تلك الأسباب الخارجية الاتفاقية إلى هذه الأسباب الستة التي ذكرناها، ظهر حينئذ أن القول بالجبر لازم. ومثاله: إذا اتفق أن نفس الإنسان وقعت في أصل خلقتها نفسا شديدة الاستعداد للغضب. ثم اتفق أن كان مزاج بدنه شديد الاستعداد للصفراء. ثم اتفق أن كانت أعضاؤه مواتية للقهر والاستعلاء. ثم اتفق أن كان قد نشأ فيما بين أقوام يستحسنون إمضاء الغضب، ويستحقون ترك تلك الأعمال. ثم اتفق أن صار بقاء دولته ورئاسته معلقا بإمضاء أعمال الغضب.
And know that whoever introspects and examines the states of his actions and deeds knows with certainty and conviction that the matter is as we have mentioned. That is because he only moves to a certain direction because he willed that movement, and he only willed the movement to that direction because he believed that he has a benefit in that movement. And that belief only occurred because he saw something, so that sight became a cause for him to remember that he has a benefit in that action, and so on for all stages. In summary: the actions of the limbs are ordered upon the actions of the hearts, and the actions of the hearts are dependent some on others. And the last depends on external contingent matters, such as: his sight falls upon something, so he remembers something, or he hears a sound, so he remembers something, with the necessary knowledge that those external contingencies are beyond his capacity and ability. Then we have mentioned those six things due to which the states of the human differ in his beliefs and his morals. So when those external contingent causes are added to these six causes which we have mentioned, then it appears that the doctrine of compulsion is necessary. And its example: if it so happens that the human soul in its original creation is a soul intensely predisposed to anger. Then it so happens that the temperament of his body is intensely predisposed to yellow bile. Then it so happens that his limbs are suitable for dominance and superiority. Then it so happens that he grew up among people who approve of executing anger and [deserve/merit?] leaving those actions. Then it so happens that the survival of his state and leadership becomes dependent on executing actions of anger.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage provides an empirical argument for determinism. The author invites introspection: a person moves because he wills, wills because he believes there is benefit, believes because of a perception caused by an external event, and so on. All actions of the limbs depend on internal acts of the heart, which in turn depend on external chance events beyond one's control. Additionally, six innate factors (previously mentioned) influence beliefs and morals. When these contingent external causes combine with the six innate factors, determinism (الجبر) becomes necessary. The example illustrates this: a person's soul may be inherently prone to anger, his temperament to yellow bile, his limbs suited for aggression, and he may be raised in a culture that approves of acting on anger, and his leadership may depend on such actions. All these factors together necessitate that he acts angrily, leaving no room for free choice.

Key Terms
رجع نفسه (introspection)
أفعال الجوارح (actions of limbs)
أفعال القلوب (actions of hearts)
أمور خارجية اتفاقية (external contingent matters)
أسباب ستة (six causes)
جبر (compulsion)
نفس (soul)
مزاج (temperament)
صفراء (yellow bile)
غضب (anger)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: External events and innate factors merely influence a person's actions, but he still has free choice to override them.
Correction: The author argues that when external contingencies combine with innate predispositions, they determine the action necessarily, leaving no room for free choice, as illustrated by the angry man.

Checkpoint
According to the example, which factor is NOT mentioned as contributing to the angry man's compulsion?
A) His soul is predisposed to anger
B) His temperament is predisposed to yellow bile
C) He has free will to overcome his anger
D) His upbringing among people who approve of anger

Key Takeaway
The introspective chain reveals that actions are ultimately caused by external contingencies beyond our control, and when combined with innate factors, compulsion is unavoidable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent decision you made that you considered 'free' — can you trace the chain of factors that led to it? Do any of those factors lie outside your control?
?
If the angry man's actions are compelled by his nature and circumstances, can you truly take pride in your good deeds? Or does the argument force you to reconsider who deserves the credit?
?
The author claims compulsion is unavoidable. Does this belief change how you view your own sins? Are they still your responsibility, or does the chain of causes shift the blame elsewhere?

Proof of Determinism from the Necessary Sequence of Causes
Al-Razi presents a causal chain argument for determinism (jabr).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's argument for determinism (jabr) based on the necessary causal sequence that leaves no room for independent human choice.

Core Idea
Every human action is necessitated by an unbroken chain of causes beyond one's control.

Al-Razi presents a rigorous deterministic argument that traces every voluntary action back to an inevitable chain of internal and external causes. He invites the reader to reflect on their own experience to see that choices are compelled by prior beliefs, perceptions, and coincidences that are not within one's power. This passage lays out the full proof, culminating in a vivid example of an angry man whose action becomes unavoidable given the convergence of multiple factors.

Guided Path
1
Understand the conclusion: man is compelled
Read the opening sentences where al-Razi states that degrees of causes are necessarily arranged, showing that man is compelled in his choice and all actions are either from Allah or necessitated by His acts.
Notice that he calls it 'necessary and inevitable' (واجبا لازما اضطراريا).
2
Trace the internal chain: will, belief, perception
Al-Razi then describes how a specific movement is preceded by will, which is preceded by belief, which is preceded by perception. This chain shows that the action is not independent but caused by prior states.
He uses the phrase 'وهلم جرا' (and so on) to indicate an infinite regress of causes.
3
Recognize external coincidences as beyond control
The final link in the chain depends on external coincidences like seeing or hearing something. These are clearly beyond one's capacity and power.
This is the key point: the causes originate outside the agent's control.
4
Consider the six factors and their role
Al-Razi refers to six previously mentioned factors that affect beliefs and character. When these combine with external coincidences, determinism becomes evident.
These six factors are: innate disposition, temperament, physical makeup, upbringing, social circumstances, etc.
5
Work through the example of anger to see necessity
Study the detailed example of an angry man. Each condition (predisposition, upbringing, social pressure, etc.) is presented as an 'if it so happens' (اتفق), showing that when all align, the person cannot avoid acting angrily.
Al-Razi concludes that whether causes align for or against the action, or conflict, the stronger side prevails, so jabr is necessary in all estimations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التصور، ترتبت تلك المراتب بعضها على بعض، بالغة ما بلغت، ترتبا واجبا لازما اضطراريا. وعند هذا يظهر أن الإنسان مضطر في اختياره، وان جميع أفعال العباد: إما أفعال الله تعالى، أو موجبات أفعال الله تعالى. وعلى التقديرين فالمطلوب حاصل. واعلم أن كل من رجع نفسه، واعتبر أحوال أفعاله وأعماله: علم - قطعا ويقينا -: أن الأمر كما ذكرناه. وذلك لأنه إنما تحرك إلى جهة كذا، لأنه أراد تلك الحركة وإنما أراد الحركة إلى تلك الجهة لأنه اعتقد أن له في تلك الحركة منفعة. وإنما حصل ذلك الاعتقاد، لأنه رأى شيئا، فصارت تلك الرؤية سببا لتذكره: أن له في ذلك الفعل منفعة. وهلم جرا إلى جميع المراتب. وبالجملة: فأفعال الجوارح مترتبة على أفعال القلوب وأفعال القلوب يستند بعضها إلى بعض. والأخير يستند إلى أمور خارجية اتفاقية. مثل: إن وقع بصره على شيء، فتذكر شيئاً. أو سمع صوتاً فتذكر شيئاً مع العلم الضروري بأن تلك الاتفاقات الخارجية: خارجة عن وسعه وطاقته. ثم إنا قد ذكرنا تلك الأشياء الستة التي بسببها تختلف أحوال الإنسان في عقائده وفي أخلاقه. فإذا انضمت تلك الأسباب الخارجية الاتفاقية، إلى هذه الأسباب الستة التي ذكرناها، ظهر حينئذ أن القول بالجبر لازم. ومثاله: إذا اتفق أن نفس الإنسان وقعت في أصل خلقتها: نفسا شديدة الاستعداد للغضب. ثم اتفق ان كان مزاج بدنه: شديد الاستعداد للصفراء ثم اتفق أن كانت أعضاؤه: مواتية للقهر والاستعلاء. ثم اتفق أن كان قد نشأ فيما بين أقوام يستحسنون إمضاء الغضب، ويستحسنون ترك تلك الأعمال. ثم اتفق أن صار بقاء دولته ورئاسته: معلقاً بإمضاء أعمال الغضب. ثم إن اتفق أن رأى شيئا، لا يلائم طبعه. ثم اتفق أن حضر عنده أقوام يعينونه على إمضاء الغضب. ثم اتفق أن حضر عنده أقوام، لو ترك إمضاء ذلك الغضب، لكانوا يذمونه ويلومونه عليه. فعند اجتماع هذه الأمور بأسرها، صار ذلك الإنسان بحيث لا يمكنه ترك تلك الأفعال الغضبية، وأما إن اتفقت أسباب مضادة للأسباب التي ذكرناها، فإنه يكون بحيث لا يمكنه الإتيان بالأفعال الغضبية وإن اختلط البعض بالبعض، وتعارضت وتقاومت فالعبرة بالطرف الراجح فثبت بما ذكرنا: أن الجبر لازم على جميع التقديرات
Thus these degrees are arranged one after another, necessarily and inevitably, no matter how many they are. And from this it appears that man is compelled in his choice, and that all the actions of servants are either acts of Allah Most High or necessitations of acts of Allah Most High. On both suppositions the desired conclusion is established. Know that whoever reflects on himself and considers the states of his actions and deeds knows with certainty and conviction that the matter is as we have described. That is because he only moves towards a certain direction because he willed that movement, and he only willed movement towards that direction because he believed that there is a benefit in that movement for him. And that belief only occurred because he saw something, so that sight became a cause for his remembering that there is a benefit in that action for him. And so on through all the degrees. In summary: the actions of the limbs are dependent on the actions of the hearts, and the actions of the hearts are based on one another, and the last depends on external coincidental matters, such as: his sight fell on something, so he remembered something; or he heard a sound, so he remembered something – with the necessary knowledge that those external coincidences are beyond his capacity and power. Then we have mentioned those six things due to which the states of man differ in his beliefs and his character. When those external coincidental causes join these six causes that we have mentioned, then it becomes clear that the doctrine of compulsion (jabr) is necessary. An example: if it so happens that the soul of man, in its original constitution, is a soul intensely predisposed to anger; then it so happens that his bodily temperament is intensely predisposed to yellow bile; then it so happens that his limbs are suited for domination and supremacy; then it so happens that he grew up among peoples who approve of giving vent to anger and disapprove of refraining from such actions; then it so happens that the continuation of his state and leadership depends on giving vent to anger; then it so happens that he sees something that does not suit his nature; then it so happens that there are present with him people who help him in giving vent to anger; then it so happens that there are present with him people who, if he refrained from giving vent to that anger, would blame and censure him. When all these matters are combined, that man becomes such that he cannot refrain from those angry actions. And if contrary causes to those we mentioned occur, then he becomes such that he cannot perform the angry actions. If some mix with others and they oppose and counteract each other, then the stronger side prevails. So it is established by what we have mentioned that compulsion (jabr) is necessary in all estimations.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a proof for determinism (jabr) based on the necessary causal sequence leading to human actions. Al-Razi begins by stating that the degrees of causes are necessarily arranged, showing that man is compelled in his choice. All actions are either directly from Allah or necessitated by His acts. He then invites self-reflection: every action is preceded by a chain of internal states (will, belief) and external coincidences (sight, sound) that are beyond one's power. He recalls six factors (mentioned earlier) that affect beliefs and character; when these combine with external coincidences, determinism becomes evident. He illustrates with an example of an angry man: a series of accidents (predisposition, upbringing, social pressure, etc.) makes it impossible for him to avoid anger. If opposite causes exist, anger becomes impossible. When causes conflict, the stronger side determines the outcome. He concludes that jabr is necessary in all scenarios.

Key Terms
جبر (jabr - compulsion)
اضطرار (idtirār - necessity)
أسباب (asbāb - causes)
دواع (dawā'ī - motives)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Determinism means humans have no role in their actions.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that actions are still attributed to humans because they are the locus of the causes, even if those causes are necessitated.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi's argument, what makes a person unable to refrain from angry actions?
A) The person's own free choice
B) The combination of internal dispositions and external coincidences that necessitate the action
C) Pure chance
D) The absence of any cause

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's causal chain argument demonstrates that determinism (jabr) is necessary because every action is the inevitable outcome of an unbroken sequence of causes beyond one's control.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a moment when you felt intense anger—raised your voice, said something hurtful. Trace back the chain: the trigger, your mood, your upbringing, the people around you. According to this passage, could you truly have acted otherwise in that moment? Be honest with yourself. What does this mean for how you see your own culpability?
?
If every belief you hold is the result of causes outside your control (upbringing, environment, coincidences), does that shake your confidence in your convictions? Or does it drive you to seek truth more humbly, knowing your mind is not as free as you assume? Commit to one way you will actively challenge your own assumptions this week.
?
Al-Razi concludes that the stronger cause always prevails. Look at your daily life: which 'causes' dominate your actions—your desires, your family's expectations, your fear of what others think, or your love for Allah? If you are honest, which force has the strongest pull on your choices today? What can you do to tip the balance toward Allah's pleasure?

The Decisive Motive and the Objection of Free Choice
Al-Razi answers the objection that humans can still choose despite the causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi defends determinism by analyzing the concept of 'can refrain' and showing that a countervailing thought only changes which motive is decisive.

Core Idea
Choice does not escape necessity; it only replaces one decisive motive with another.

The objector argues that humans can refrain from angry actions despite all causes, proving free choice. Al-Razi calls this the 'last mistake' and offers a careful analysis. He asks: what does 'can refrain' actually mean? In this passage, he dissects the objection and turns it into further evidence for his deterministic position.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: the objection, then al-Razi's response beginning with 'We say: This is the last mistake.'
The objection is short; al-Razi's answer is longer.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Al-Razi clarifies what 'can refrain' could mean. Find the two cases he discusses: (1) a countervailing thought appears; (2) the same motive fails to produce action.
He accepts the first but denies the second.
3
Understand the decisive motive
Notice how al-Razi uses the concept of 'decisive motive' (dā'iyya jāzima). Action is necessary only when a decisive motive is present, and a countervailing thought makes the motive non-decisive.
This is central to his argument.
4
See how the objection backfires
Al-Razi concludes that the objector's first interpretation actually strengthens his case. The second interpretation is impossible. So the objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: الإنسان مع حصول جميع الأمور التي ذكرتم، يمكنه ترك الأفعال الغضبية فعلمنا: أن صدور تلك الأفعال عنه على سبيل الاختيار، لا على سبيل الاضطرار. فنقول: هذا هو الغلط الأخير. وعند ظهور الجواب عنه، لا يبقى البتة في ما ذكرناه إشكال. فنقول: ما الذي تعني بقولك: « إن الإنسان مع الأمور، يمكنه ترك الأفعال الغضبية ؟: إن عنيت به أنه يمكن أن يعرض له في مقابلة تلك الخواطر: خاطر آخر. وهو أن الأولى ترك هذه الأفعال الغضبية. فنحن نسلم: أن عند ظهور هذا الخاطر، قد يترك تلك الأفعال الغضبية. إلا أن على هذا التقدير، صارت تلك الدواعي التي ذكرناها، معارضة بهذه الداعية. فصارت لتلك الدواعي التي ذكرناها، عند حصول هذا المعارض: فائدة غير جازمة. ونحن بينا: أن الفعل إنما يجب عند حصول الداعية الجازمة. فأما عند فتور الداعية، فالفعل ممتنع. فثبت: أن ما ذكرتموه يقوي كلامنا، ولا يوجب ضعفه. وإن أردتم أن تلك الدواعي حال بقائها على قوتها وسلامتها عن المعارض، يمكن أن لا توجد الفعل. فهذا ممنوع. وكيف يمكن
If they say: 'Man, despite the occurrence of all the matters you mentioned, can refrain from angry actions. So we know that those actions proceed from him by choice, not by compulsion.' We say: This is the last mistake. When the answer to it appears, no difficulty remains at all in what we have mentioned. We say: What do you mean by your statement: 'Man, with those matters, can refrain from angry actions'? If you mean that there can occur in him, in opposition to those thoughts, another thought, namely that it is better to refrain from these angry actions, then we concede that when this thought appears, he may refrain from those angry actions. However, on this assumption, those motives we mentioned become opposed by this motive, and so those motives we mentioned, when this opponent occurs, become non-decisive. We have shown that action is only necessary when a decisive motive (dā'iyya jāzima) is present. As for when the motive is weak, action is impossible. So it is established that what you mention strengthens our argument, not weakens it. And if you mean that those motives, while they remain in their strength and free from opposition, can fail to produce the action, this is denied. How could it be possible?
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objector argues that despite all causes, man can still refrain from angry actions, proving free choice. Al-Razi calls this the 'last mistake'. He clarifies the meaning of 'can refrain': if it means a countervailing thought arises, then the motive is no longer decisive, and action is not necessary – this actually supports his view that action requires a decisive motive. If it means that the same strong motives could fail to produce action, that is impossible. Thus, the objection does not refute determinism.

Key Terms
داعية جازمة (dā'iyya jāzima - decisive motive)
اختيار (ikhtiyār - choice)
اضطرار (idtirār - compulsion)
خاطر (khāṭir - thought)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the mere possibility of a countervailing thought proves free choice and refutes determinism.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that a countervailing thought is itself a new decisive motive; action is still necessitated by the strongest motive, so determinism holds.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, when a countervailing thought arises against the motive for anger, what happens?
A) The action still occurs by free choice
B) The original motive becomes non-decisive, and the action is not necessitated
C) The action becomes impossible
D) The person chooses regardless

Key Takeaway
The objection that humans can choose despite causes is refuted by showing that any alternative thought is itself a decisive motive, and the claim that a strong motive can fail to produce action is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you felt a strong urge to react angrily but held back. According to al-Razi, the thought that held you back was itself a decisive motive that determined your action. Does this make you feel less responsible for your patience, or more responsible for cultivating that counter-thought? Commit today to consciously strengthen the motives for good actions in your heart.
?
Al-Razi denies that you can act against a strong, unopposed motive without any reason. Have you ever told yourself 'I could have chosen otherwise' when there was no actual alternative thought? Is that belief sincere, or is it a comforting illusion? Resolve to examine the real motives behind your choices, and take responsibility for the motives you nurture.
?
If every action follows the strongest motive, then your 'free choice' is simply the victory of one motive over others. What does that mean for how you approach your own moral growth? Are you actively seeking to make the right motives decisive in your life, or do you rely on the illusion that you can choose without preparation?

The Proof of Necessity in Action and the Objection Rebutted
The author constructs a scenario proving determinism and rebuts the objection of free choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the deterministic argument that when all motives for an action are present and unopposed, the action is necessary, and how the author rebuts the objection that choice remains.

Core Idea
When all motives align and none oppose, action becomes unavoidable—this is the core of jabr.

This passage presents a detailed scenario to illustrate the deterministic position (jabr) in action. Using the example of anger, the author shows that when all internal and external factors converge, a person cannot refrain from acting. He then anticipates an objection based on the feeling of choice and systematically dismantles it.

Guided Path
1
Read the scenario carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the three conditions that combine to make action necessary.
2
Identify the conditions for necessity
The author describes three specific conditions that together make the angry actions unavoidable. Identify them and see how they lead to the conclusion of determinism.
Also note the converse: opposite conditions make action impossible.
3
Understand the objection
The objector claims that despite all conditions, the person can still refrain, implying free choice. Recognize the phrasing of the objection.
The objector uses the word 'can' — the author will dissect its meaning.
4
Analyze the author's response
The author asks what 'can' means. Follow the two interpretations and how each either concedes the point or is denied as impossible.
Pay attention to the concept of 'decisive motive' (da'iya jazima).
5
Apply the principle broadly
The author concludes that the argument applies to all actions. Reflect on how this deterministic framework explains human behavior across different situations.
Consider how this relates to the broader theological debate on qadar.

ثم إن اتفق أن رأى شيئا ، لا يلائم طبعه . ثم اتفق أن حضر عنده أقوام يعينونه على إمضاء الغضب . ثم اتفق أن حضر عنده أقوام ، لو ترك إمضاء ذلك الغضب، لكانوا يذمونه ويلومونه عليه . فعند اجتماع هذه الأمور بأسرها ، صار ذلك الإنسان بحيث لا يمكنه ترك تلك الأفعال الغضبية ، وأما إن اتفقت أسباب مضادة للأسباب التي ذكرناها ، فإنه يكون بحيث لا يمكنه الإتيان بالأفعال الغضبية وإن اختلط البعض بالبعض ، وتعارضت وتقاومت فالعبرة بالطرف الراجح فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن الجبر لازم على جميع التقديرات · فإن قالوا : الإنسان مع حصول جميع الأمور التي ذكرتم ، يمكنه ترك الأفعال الغضبية فعلمنا : أن صدور تلك الأفعال عنه على سبيل الاختيار ، لا على سبيل الاضطرار . فنقول : هذا هو الغلط الأخير. وعند ظهور الجواب عنه ، لا يبقى البتة فيها ذكرناه إشكال . فنقول : ما الذي تعنى بقولك : « إن الإنسان مع الأمور ، يمكنه ترك الأفعال الغضبية ؟ : إن عنيت به أنه يمكن أن يعرض له في مقابلة تلك الخواطر : خاطر آخر. وهو أن الأولى ترك هذه الأفعال الغضبية . فنحن نسلم : أن عند ظهور هذا الخاطر ، قد يترك تلك الأفعال الغضبية . إلا أن على هذا التقدير ، صارت تلك الدواعي التي ذكرناها ، معارضة بهذه الداعية . فصارت لتلك الدواعي التي ذكرناها ، عند حصول هذا المعارض : فائدة غير جازمة . ونحن بينا : أن الفعل إنما يجب عند حصول الداعية الجازمة . فأما عند فتور الداعية ، فالفعل ممتنع . فثبت : أن ما ذكرتموه يقوي كلامنا ، ولا يوجب ضعفه . وإن أردتم أن تلك الدواعي حال بقائها على قوتها وسلامتها عن المعارض ، يمكن أن لا توجد الفعل . فهذا ممنوع . وكيف يمكن أن يقال : الإنسان حال اجتماع تلك الأسباب الموجبة للأفعال الغضبية ، وبراءتها عن الدواعي المعارضة لها ، فإنه يمكن أن لا يأتي بتلك الأفعال ؟ . فالحاصل : أنكم إن أردتم أن سلامة أعضائه حال انضمام دواعي الترك إليها ، بدلا عن دواعي الفعل . لأمكنه ذلك . فهذا مسلم . ولا يقدح في قولنا . وإن أردتم به أن سلامة أعضائه حال انضمام دواعي الفعل إليها ، وكانت تلك الدواعي خالية عن المعارض، فإن مع التقدير يمكنه الترك. فهذا ممنوع. والعلم بامتناعه ضروري. ولما عرفت الكلام في هذا المثال ، أمكنك اعتبار أحوال جميع الأحوال والأفعال . ولا فائدة في التطويل .
Then if it happens that he sees something not suiting his nature, then it happens that there are people present who help him in executing anger, then it happens that there are people present who, if he refrains from executing that anger, would blame and reproach him. So when all these matters come together, that person becomes such that he cannot abandon those angry actions. And if causes contrary to those we mentioned occur, then he becomes such that he cannot perform the angry actions. And if some are mixed with others, conflicting and resisting, then the consideration is of the stronger side. So it is established by what we mentioned: that determinism (al-jabr) is necessary in all estimations. Then if they say: 'A person, with the occurrence of all the matters you mentioned, can abandon the angry actions, so we know that those actions emanate from him by choice (ikhtiyar), not by compulsion (idtirar).' We say: This is the last error. When the answer appears, no doubt remains in what we mentioned. We say: What do you mean by your statement 'A person, with these matters, can abandon the angry actions?' If you mean that there can occur to him, in counter to those thoughts, another thought, namely that it is better to abandon these angry actions, then we concede that when this thought appears, he may abandon those angry actions. But on this estimation, those motives (dawa'i) we mentioned become opposed by this motive. So those motives we mentioned, with the occurrence of this opponent, become non-decisive. And we have shown that an action is only necessitated when a decisive motive (da'iya jazima) occurs. As for when the motive weakens, the action is impossible. So it is established that what you mentioned strengthens our statement and does not cause its weakness. If you mean that those motives, as long as they remain strong and free from opposition, can not produce the action, this is denied. How can it be said that a person, with the gathering of those causes necessitating angry actions and their being free from opposing motives, can fail to perform those actions? The conclusion: If you mean that [it is possible] with the soundness of his limbs when motives of abandonment join them, instead of motives of action, that he can [abstain], then this is conceded and does not harm our statement. If you mean that with the soundness of his limbs when motives of action join them and those motives are free from opposition, then on this estimation he can abandon, this is denied, and knowledge of its impossibility is necessary. Once you understand the discussion in this example, you can apply it to the states of all conditions and actions. There is no benefit in prolonging.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a proof and an objection-and-answer defending the deterministic (jabr) position. The author sets up a scenario: a person sees something disagreeable, has supporters to help him vent anger, and fears blame if he doesn't. When all these conditions converge, the person becomes unable to refrain from angry actions. Similarly, if opposite conditions exist, he becomes unable to perform them. When conditions mix, the stronger side prevails. Thus, determinism holds in all cases. An objector claims that despite these conditions, the person can still refrain, proving free choice. The author counters: if the objector means a counter-thought arises, that's conceding the point—the original motives become non-decisive, and action only follows a decisive motive. If he means the original motives remain strong yet the action doesn't occur, this is impossible by necessary knowledge. The distinction is between having opposite motives and lacking them. The author concludes by stating the argument applies universally.

Key Terms
جبر (jabr)
داعية جازمة (decisive motive)
دواعي (motives)
اختيار (choice)
اضطرار (compulsion)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if you have motives for an action, you still have free choice to do otherwise regardless of the strength of those motives.
Correction: The author argues that when motives are decisive and unopposed, the action is necessary; apparent choice only occurs when counter-motives arise, which changes the decisive nature of the original motives.

Checkpoint
According to the author, when does a person become unable to refrain from an action?
A) When any motive is present
B) When all motives for action gather without opposing motives
C) When he consciously decides to act
D) When he has free will

Key Takeaway
Determinism (jabr) is proven when all motives are decisive and unopposed; apparent choice is actually due to the presence or absence of opposing motives.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a moment you acted in anger. Looking back, can you identify the three conditions from the source that were present? If any were missing, would you have acted differently? When a counter-motive (like fear of Allah) arises in your heart, do you usually listen, or do you suppress it? What is stopping you from letting that counter-motive become decisive?
?
The author says that when motives are strong and unopposed, the action is impossible to avoid. In your life, are there sins you think you 'cannot' avoid? Does this passage reveal that the real issue is that you allow no counter-motive to truly challenge your desires? What would it take for you to cultivate a powerful counter-motive from your faith?
?
If determinism is true in every estimation, how does that change your view of your own past mistakes? Can you honestly repent while feeling that your actions were necessitated? Or does this passage push you to take more responsibility for cultivating the right motives, knowing that only a decisive motive can save you?

Ali's Sermon on the Heart's Motives as Evidence for Determinism
Ali's sermon enumerating pairs of heart states and their necessary effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ali's description of the heart's conflicting motives supports the deterministic argument that actions are necessitated by dominant motives.

Core Idea
The heart's motives determine its actions necessarily.

The author concludes his deterministic proof by quoting a famous sermon from Ali ibn Abi Talib. In it, Ali describes how each emotional or circumstantial state of the heart leads inexorably to a specific outcome. This passage illustrates the principle that when a motive is decisive and unopposed, the person cannot act otherwise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Notice the repeating pattern: 'if [condition], then [result].' Then read the translation to grasp the full meaning.
2
Identify the cause-effect pairs
List each condition (hope, greed, despair, etc.) and its corresponding outcome (humiliation, destruction, grief, etc.). Observe how each motive leads to a specific necessary effect.
There are ten pairs in the passage.
3
Reflect on the concluding statement
The final sentence says: 'So every deficiency in him is harmful, and every excess in him is damaging.' Consider how this reinforces the idea that the heart's state dictates its response, leaving no room for free choice.
This ties back to the deterministic argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنختم هذا البرهان القاطع ، بفصل منقول عن أمير المؤمنين علي بن أبي طالب - رضي الله عنه ـ يقوي ما ذكرناه روي أنه رضي الله عنه خطب فقال : وأعجب ما في الإنسان قلبه رضي فإن سنح له الرجاء، أذله الطمع. وإن هاج به الطمع، أهلكه الحرص. وإن ملكه اليأس ، قتله الأسف .. وإن عرض له الغضب ، اشتد به الغيظ وإن أسعده الرضى نسى التحفظ . وإن ناله الخوف ، شغله الحذر وإن أصابته مصيبة قصمه الجزع ... وإن وجد مالا ، أطفاء الغني . وإن عضته الفاقة ، شغله البلاء . وإن جهده الجوع ، قعد به الضعف ... فكل تقصير به : مضر ، وكل إفراط له : مفد .
Let us conclude this decisive proof with a section transmitted from the Commander of the Faithful, Ali ibn Abi Talib - may Allah be pleased with him - which strengthens what we have mentioned. It is narrated that he, may Allah be pleased with him, delivered a sermon saying: The most amazing thing in man is his heart, for if hope appears to him, greed humiliates him; if greed stirs in him, covetousness destroys him; if despair masters him, grief kills him; if anger presents itself to him, rage intensifies in him; if pleasure makes him happy, he forgets caution; if fear befalls him, caution preoccupies him; if a calamity strikes him, impatience breaks him; if he acquires wealth, opulence extinguishes him; if poverty afflicts him, tribulation preoccupies him; if hunger exhausts him, weakness brings him down... So every deficiency in him is harmful, and every excess in him is damaging.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is an authoritative citation concluding the earlier deterministic proof. The author quotes Ali ibn Abi Talib's famous sermon on the heart's conflicting states. Each line shows how a specific emotion or condition (hope, greed, despair, anger, pleasure, fear, calamity, wealth, poverty, hunger) leads to a corresponding outcome (humiliation, destruction, grief, rage, forgetfulness, caution, breaking, being extinguished, preoccupation, weakness). The point is that the heart is driven by its dominant motive, and action follows necessarily. The final remark that both deficiency and excess are harmful underscores the determinism: the heart's state dictates its response. This supports the author's argument that when motives are decisive and without opposition, the person cannot act otherwise.

Correction: The author uses the quote to illustrate determinism: each motive necessitates its effect, leaving no room for free choice.

Refraining from Actions Despite Complete Causes: An Objection and Answer
Objection and answer on refraining from angry actions despite complete internal causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how kalam scholars respond to the objection that a person could refrain from an action even when all internal causes (motives) are present, by distinguishing between the presence of opposing motives and their absence.

Core Idea
The possibility of refraining depends on whether opposing motives exist, not on free will independent of motives.

Sanusi now anticipates a common objection: if someone is fully provoked to anger, could they still choose not to act? The answer is subtle — it depends on what 'safety of limbs' means. This passage teaches us how careful distinctions are made in kalam to defend determinism without denying obvious human experiences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objector's question: when causes for anger are fully present and no opposing motives exist, can a person still refrain?
Notice the objector assumes complete causes yet asks about possibility of inaction.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The author presents two ways to interpret 'safety of limbs' (salamat al-a'da'). Find both in the answer.
The first involves motives of abandonment; the second involves motives of action without opposition.
3
See which is conceded and which denied
The author concedes the first interpretation (possible) but denies the second (impossible). Understand why.
The denial is based on necessary knowledge – it is self-evident that if motives are fully present and unopposed, the action must occur.
4
Apply the lesson generally
The author says this example applies to all states and actions. Reflect on how this reasoning works for any action determined by motives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن يقال : الإنسان حال اجتماع تلك الأسباب الموجبة للأفعال الغضبية ، وبراءتها عن الدواعي المعارضة لها ، فإنه يمكن أن لا يأتي بتلك الأفعال ؟ .
فالحاصل : أنكم إن أردتم أن سلامة أعضائه حال انضمام دواعي الترك إليها ، بدلا عن دواعي الفعل . لأمكنه ذلك . فهذا مسلم . ولا يقدح في قولنا . وإن أردتم به أن سلامة أعضائه حال انضمام دواعي الفعل إليها ، وكانت تلك الدواعي خالية عن المعارض، فإن مع التقدير يمكنه الترك. فهذا ممنوع. والعلم بامتناعه ضروري. ولما عرفت الكلام في هذا المثال ، أمكنك اعتبار أحوال جميع الأحوال والأفعال . ولا فائدة في التطويل .
One might say: The person, when those causes that necessitate angry actions are assembled, and they are free from opposing motives, is it possible that he does not perform those actions? So the conclusion: If you mean that the safety of his limbs when motives of abandonment are attached to them, instead of motives of action, then that is possible. This is conceded, and it does not impugn our statement. But if you mean that the safety of his limbs when motives of action are attached to them, and those motives are free from opposition, that even with this assumption he can abandon [the action], then this is denied. And the knowledge of its impossibility is necessary. And once you have understood the discussion in this example, you can apply it to all states and actions. And there is no benefit in prolongation.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer (sual wa jawab). The objector poses a hypothetical: When all the causes that provoke anger are present, and no opposing motives exist, is it possible for a person not to commit the angry action? The author responds by distinguishing two interpretations of 'safety of limbs' (salamat al-a'da'). First, if the objector means that limbs are safe from harm when motives for abstention are present instead of motives for action, then yes, the person can refrain—this is conceded but irrelevant. Second, if the objector means that limbs are safe even when motives for action are present and there is no opposing motive, yet the person could still refrain, then this is denied. The impossibility of such refraining is self-evident. The author then states that once this example is understood, it can be applied to all situations.

Key Terms
دواعي الفعل
دواعي الترك
سلامة الأعضاء
المعارض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection proves that humans have free will to refrain from actions even when all internal causes for action are present and unopposed.
Correction: The author shows that refraining is possible only when motives for abandonment are present, not when motives for action are fully present without opposition. In the latter case, refraining is impossible by necessity.

Checkpoint
According to the author, when is it possible for a person to refrain from an angry action despite the presence of causes?
A) When motives of action are present and free from opposition.
B) When motives of abandonment are present instead of motives of action.
C) Under no circumstances whatsoever.
D) When the person consciously decides to refrain regardless of motives.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of refraining from an action depends on the presence or absence of opposing motives, not on independent free will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When anger surges within you and you feel compelled to lash out, do you honestly examine the motives driving you? Are you aware of whether any opposing motives—like fear of Allah or desire to please Him—are truly present in your heart, or have you allowed them to be absent? What concrete step can you take today to cultivate those opposing motives before your next moment of rage?
?
Have you ever excused your outbursts by saying 'I couldn't help it' or 'It was inevitable'? This lesson teaches that refraining is only impossible when no opposing motive exists. Are you relying on that excuse while ignoring the fact that you could have summoned an opposing motive? What would change if you genuinely believed that you are accountable for failing to generate that motive?
?
This passage forces you to confront whether you truly desire to control your anger, or whether you secretly prefer the ease of claiming powerlessness. If you were to actively seek opposing motives—like remembering Allah's command to forgive—what would that require you to give up? Are you willing to make that sacrifice?

Fourth Proof: Divine Knowledge Necessitates Predestination
Thesis and common ground for the fourth proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the thesis of the fourth proof and how the author establishes common ground with the Mu'tazila to argue that God's knowledge necessitates predestination.

Core Idea
God's perfect knowledge of all events makes their occurrence inevitable.

The author now advances a new argument for predestination, this time based on divine knowledge. He first states the thesis: the servant can only do what Allah has destined. Then he notes that even the Mu'tazila—who champion free will—agree that Allah knows all future particulars. This shared premise becomes the starting point for the proof. Let's see how the argument begins.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author first states the thesis, then mentions the agreement with the Mu'tazila.
The passage is cut off mid-sentence at the end, but the direction of the argument is clear.
2
Identify the thesis and the common ground
Find the sentence that states the thesis (what the author wants to prove) and the sentence that establishes common ground with the Mu'tazila.
The thesis is about the servant's inability to do what Allah has not destined. The common ground is that Allah knows all particulars.
3
Understand the logical implication
Even though the passage is incomplete, try to see the reasoning: if Allah knows that a disbeliever will not believe, then belief cannot occur from him. Why? Because knowledge must correspond to reality. Discuss or write down why this implies predestination.
This is the core of the argument: God's infallible knowledge makes the known event certain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الرابع على أن العبد لا يمكنه أن يأتي إلا بما قدره الله تعالى وأن الذي ما قدره الله تعالى له فانه يمتنع صدوره عنه. إن المعتزلة وافقونا على أنه تعالى عالم بجميع الجزئيات التي ستقع. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: تقريره من وجوه: الأول: إنه تعالى لما علم من الكافر أنه لا يؤمن، كان صدور
The fourth proof that the servant cannot do except what Allah—exalted is He—has destined, and that what Allah—exalted is He—has not destined for him cannot occur from him. The Mu'tazila have agreed with us that He—exalted is He—knows all particulars that will occur. If you understand this, we say: Its exposition is from several aspects: The first: Since He—exalted is He—knew of the disbeliever that he will not believe, then the occurrence [of faith]
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a new proof (al-burhan al-rabi'). The author states the thesis: The servant can only do what Allah has decreed, and what Allah has not decreed for him cannot occur. He then notes that even the Mu'tazila agree that Allah knows all particulars that will happen. Building on this common ground, the author presents the proof in several ways (min wujuh). Only the first way is begun: Since Allah knew that the disbeliever would not believe, the occurrence of belief from him is impossible. The argument is cut off, but the intended logic is clear: God's knowledge that something will not happen makes its occurrence impossible, because knowledge corresponds to reality. If it did occur, God's knowledge would be false, which is impossible. Hence the servant cannot produce an action that God knows will not happen.

Key Terms
البرهان الرابع
عالم بجميع الجزئيات
تقدير الله
الكافر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's knowledge merely passively records actions without affecting their reality, so humans can still act contrary to what God knows.
Correction: The argument shows that God's knowledge is infallible and must correspond to reality. If God knows an event will not occur, it cannot occur, otherwise His knowledge would be false. Thus knowledge necessitates the event's occurrence or non-occurrence.

Checkpoint
What common ground does the author establish before presenting the fourth proof?
A) The Mu'tazila agree that humans create their own actions.
B) The Mu'tazila agree that Allah knows all particulars that will occur.
C) The Mu'tazila agree that Allah's knowledge is acquired.
D) The Mu'tazila agree that the servant has free will.

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof begins by establishing divine knowledge of all particulars as a shared premise; from this, the author argues that what God knows will not happen cannot occur, thereby proving predestination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah knew every sin you would commit before you were born? How does that knowledge change the way you approach repentance? This week, when you are tempted to sin, remind yourself that Allah already knew you would be tempted—and that He knows whether you will resist or fall. Will you use that knowledge to flee from sin rather than excuse it?
?
If Allah's knowledge makes the future inevitable, what genuine responsibility do you have for your actions? The proof says the disbeliever cannot produce faith because Allah knew he wouldn't. How does this affect your view of your own struggles to obey? Are you using predestination as a shield to avoid effort, or as a motivation to strive harder knowing that Allah's knowledge encompasses your choices?
?
Consider the disbeliever in the proof: Allah knew he would never believe. Pause and ask yourself: what does Allah know about your faith tomorrow, next year, at the end of your life? Are you living as if that knowledge is fixed, or are you striving to change your fate through sincere du'a and action? What will you do differently today because you believe Allah's knowledge is both certain and merciful?
