The Common Ground: God's Knowledge of All Particulars
Establishing agreed premise of God's omniscience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the shared premise between Sunni and Mu'tazila that God knows all future particular events, which is the foundation for the subsequent proofs.

Core Idea
Both schools agree: God knows everything that will happen.

Before presenting the three proofs, the author establishes that the Mu'tazila concede a crucial point: God has complete and detailed knowledge of all future particulars. This agreement sets the stage for the logical arguments that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the shared premise
Read the Arabic passage stating that both schools agree on God's knowledge of all particulars. Note how it is presented as a fact before the proofs.
This premise is accepted by both sides to avoid lengthy debate.
2
Understand the function of this premise
Recognize that this common ground allows the author to build proofs that rely on God's foreknowledge without needing to re-argue it.
3
Prepare for the proofs
Notice the phrase 'if you have understood this, then we say: Its exposition is from aspects:' – this signals that the proofs are about to be presented.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الرابع على أن العبد لا يمكنه أن يأتي إلا بما قدره الله تعالى وأن الذي ما قدره الله تعالى له، فانه يمتنع صدوره عنه. إن المعتزلة وافقونا على أنه تعالى: عالم بجميع الجزئيات التي ستقع. إذا عرفت هذا، فنقول: تقريره من وجوه: الأول: إنه تعالى لما علم أنه لا يؤمن، كان صدور الإيمان منه يستلزم انقلاب علم الله جهلاً. وهذا محال. ومستلزم المحال محال. فيلزم: أن يكون صدور الإيمان عنه محالاً. الثاني: إن وجود الإيمان، يستحيل أن يحصل مع العلم بعدم الإيمان لأنه إنما يكون علماً، لو كان مطابقاً للمعلوم. والعلم بعدم الإيمان، إنما يكون مطابقاً لو حصل عدم الإيمان. فلو وجد الإيمان مع العلم بعدم الإيمان، يلزم أن يجتمع في الإيمان كونه موجوداً ومعدوماً معاً. وهو محال. أنه تعالى لما علم منه عدم الإيمان كان وجود الإيمان منه محالاً. ولو علم منه وجود الإيمان، كان عدم الإيمان منه محالاً. والثالث: إنه تعالى أخبر عن أقوام معينين: أنهم لا يؤمنون كقوله: إن الذين كفروا سواء عليهم الأنذرتهم أم لم تنذرهم لا يؤمنون، وقوله: لقد حق القول على أكثرهم فهم لا يؤمنون، فهؤلاء الذين أخبر الله عنهم، أنهم لا يؤمنون. لو آمنوا، لانقلب خبر الله الصدق تكذباً، وهذا محال. والمؤدي إلى المحال محال. فوجب أن يكون صدور الإيمان منهم محالاً.
The Fourth Proof: That the servant cannot come except with what God Almighty has decreed, and that what God Almighty has not decreed for him, then its occurrence from him is impossible. Indeed, the Mu'tazila agreed with us that He, exalted, is Knower of all particular things that will occur. If you have understood this, then we say: Its exposition is from aspects: The First: That He, exalted, when He knew that he will not believe, the occurrence of belief from him would necessitate the transformation of God's knowledge into ignorance. And this is impossible. And that which necessitates the impossible is impossible. So it follows that the occurrence of belief from him is impossible. The Second: The existence of belief is impossible to occur together with knowledge of non-belief, because it (knowledge) is only knowledge if it corresponds to the known. And knowledge of non-belief is only corresponding if non-belief occurs. So if belief exists together with knowledge of non-belief, it would necessitate that in belief there would be its being existent and non-existent together. And that is impossible. And that He, exalted, when He knew from him non-belief, then the existence of belief from him is impossible. And if He knew from him the existence of belief, then non-belief from him would be impossible. The Third: That He, exalted, informed about specific peoples that they will not believe, such as His saying: 'Indeed, those who disbelieve – it is all the same to them whether you warn them or do not warn them – they will not believe' (Quran 2:6), and His saying: 'The word has indeed been fulfilled against most of them, so they will not believe' (Quran 36:7). So those about whom God informed that they will not believe – if they were to believe, the truthful news of God would turn into falsehood, and this is impossible. And that which leads to the impossible is impossible. So it is necessary that the occurrence of belief from them is impossible.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs within the broader fourth argument that a servant can only act according to God's decree. The author first establishes that the Mu'tazila agree with Sunni Muslims that God knows all future particulars. Then he offers three 'aspects' (وجوه) of proof. The first proof is based on God's foreknowledge: if God knows that a particular person will not believe, then the occurrence of belief would transform God's knowledge into ignorance, which is impossible; thus belief is impossible. The second proof appeals to logical contradiction: the existence of belief alongside God's knowledge of non-belief would require belief to be both existent and non-existent simultaneously, which is absurd; therefore belief cannot occur. The third proof relies on divine news (khabar) from the Quran, where God explicitly states that certain groups will not believe; if they were to believe, God's truthful report would become false, which is impossible; hence their belief is impossible. Each proof concludes that the act (e.g., belief) cannot occur from that person, reinforcing the doctrine of qadar (divine decree). The author does not elaborate rebuttals here; the proofs are presented as straightforward logical deductions from agreed premises.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
عالم بجميع الجزئيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the agreed premise between Sunni Muslims and the Mu'tazila according to this passage?
A) That God decrees all actions.
B) That God knows all future particular events.
C) That humans have free will.
D) That the Quran is created.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila conceded God's omniscience over all future events, which serves as the foundation for the proofs that follow.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You now know that even the Mu'tazila, who strongly defended free will, agreed that God knows everything you will do. Does that truth make you feel watched or cared for? How will you live differently today, knowing that your every thought and action is fully known by your Creator?
?
If God knows every sin you will commit before you commit it, does that reduce your sense of responsibility or increase it? What will you do the next time you are tempted to sin, remembering that nothing escapes His knowledge?
?
The author says 'if you have understood this' before moving to proofs. Have you truly internalized God's all-encompassing knowledge, or is it just an idea you acknowledge? What specific change in your daily habits would prove that you really believe He sees everything?

First Proof: Foreknowledge and Impossibility of Belief
First proof: foreknowledge entails impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that God's foreknowledge of non-belief makes belief impossible because it would require changing God's knowledge into ignorance.

Core Idea
Turning knowledge into ignorance is impossible.

The first proof uses the agreed premise of God's foreknowledge. If God knows that a specific person will not believe, then belief cannot occur because it would transform His knowledge into ignorance, which is a contradiction. This argument shows that the act is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof
Read the Arabic text of the first proof. Identify the conditional: if God knows that he will not believe, then belief would necessitate changing God's knowledge into ignorance.
The proof is a simple syllogism.
2
Analyze the logical steps
Break it down: premise – God knows he will not believe. Conclusion – belief is impossible because it would entail a contradiction (knowledge becomes ignorance). The impossibility of the result means the cause is impossible.
3
Connect to the broader argument
Understand that this proof applies to any particular action God knows will not occur. It reinforces that the servant cannot act except by God's decree.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Second Proof: Logical Contradiction of Existence and Non-Existence
Second proof: logical contradiction between knowledge and existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that belief cannot co-exist with God's knowledge of non-belief because it would require belief to be both existent and non-existent simultaneously.

Core Idea
Knowledge must correspond to reality; contradiction is impossible.

The second proof deepens the argument by examining the nature of knowledge. For God's knowledge to be true, it must correspond to the known. Since God knows non-belief, belief cannot actually exist, otherwise the knowledge would not correspond, leading to a logical contradiction where belief is both existent (as an actual event) and non-existent (as required by God's knowledge).

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof
Read the Arabic text. Notice it builds on the concept that knowledge is only knowledge if it matches reality.
2
Understand the correspondence condition
Grasp the idea that knowledge 'عدم الإيمان' (non-belief) only corresponds if non-belief actually occurs. If belief happens, the knowledge would not correspond.
3
Recognize the contradiction
If belief exists, then belief would have to be both existent (as an actual event) and non-existent (because God's knowledge requires non-belief). That is a logical contradiction (محال).
4
Apply the principle
Since contradiction is impossible, the scenario (belief occurring) is impossible. This applies to both belief and non-belief symmetrically.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Third Proof: Divine News and the Impossibility of Falsehood
Third proof: scriptural evidence from Quranic prophecies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof that belief is impossible for certain disbelievers because God's unequivocal news in the Quran declares they will not believe, and God's truthful report cannot become false.

Core Idea
God's truthful news cannot become false; therefore, those He says will not believe cannot believe.

The third proof moves from logical arguments to scriptural authority. The Quran contains explicit statements that specific groups will not believe. Since God's news is always truthful, if they were to believe, His news would become false, which is impossible. Therefore, their belief is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the third proof
Read the Arabic text, noting the Quranic verses quoted as evidence.
2
Identify the Quranic verses
Two verses are mentioned: Quran 2:6 and 36:7. Understand that they explicitly state that certain individuals will not believe.
3
Understand the argument
If those individuals were to believe, God's news would become false. Since God's news is truthful, that cannot happen. Therefore belief is impossible.
4
Apply the principle
The argument uses the same logic as the first proof: leading to the impossible makes the cause impossible.

The first proof is based on God's foreknowledge: if God knows that a particular person will not believe, then the occurrence of belief would transform God's knowledge into ignorance, which is impossible; thus belief is impossible. The second proof appeals to logical contradiction: the existence of belief alongside God's knowledge of non-belief would require belief to be both existent and non-existent simultaneously, which is absurd; therefore belief cannot occur. The third proof relies on divine news (khabar) from the Quran, where God explicitly states that certain groups will not believe; if they were to believe, God's truthful report would become false, which is impossible; hence their belief is impossible. Each proof concludes that the act (e.g., belief) cannot occur from that person, reinforcing the doctrine of qadar (divine decree). The author does not elaborate rebuttals here; the proofs are presented as straightforward logical deductions from agreed premises.

Key Terms
الثالث
أخبر
الخبر الصدق
تكذباً
المؤدي إلى المحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, what would happen if those mentioned in Quran 2:6 were to believe?
A) God's knowledge would be confirmed.
B) God's truthful news would become false.
C) The Quran would be revealed again.
D) The disbelievers would become believers.

Key Takeaway
The third proof uses Quranic declarations that certain individuals will not believe to show that their belief is impossible, as it would contradict God's truthful news.

Examine yourself: do you ever fear that your faith is merely a temporary state rather than a fixed decree?
?
The passage declares that belief is impossible for some. Does that thought humble you or make you complacent? When you read that God has the power to seal hearts, do you respond with gratitude for His mercy or with a sense of entitlement? Repent of any pride in your own faith and ask God to keep you firm.

The Contradictory Obligation to Believe in One's Own Disbelief
A proof from contradictory obligation (تكليف المحال)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how God's foreknowledge of disbelief creates a contradictory obligation, demonstrating the impossibility of faith for those foreordained to disbelieve.

Core Idea
God's command to believe includes believing His report that you will never believe—a logical impossibility.

This proof from the nature of obligation (taklif) reveals a subtle contradiction. Those whom God has informed will never believe are commanded to have faith. But faith requires affirming all of God's reports, including the report that they will never believe. This sets up a logical impossibility, showing that such faith cannot occur.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice how the argument unfolds in a logical sequence.
Pay attention to the key terms: تكليف, تصديق, جمع الضدين.
2
Identify the premise about faith
The argument states that faith (إيمان) requires affirming the truth of God in everything He has informed about. This is a crucial premise that drives the contradiction.
3
Trace the contradiction
Since God informed that they will never believe, they must believe that they will never believe. But believing is itself an act of faith, so they must both believe and believe they cannot believe—a contradiction.
This contradiction is impossible, so faith cannot occur from them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إنه تعالى كلف هؤلاء الذين أخبر عنهم بأنهم لا يؤمنون البتة : بالإيمان، والإيمان يعتبر فيه تصديق الله في كل ما أخبر عنه . ومما أخبر عنهم : أنهم لا يؤمنون قط . فقد صاروا مكلفين بأن يؤمنوا بأنهم لا يؤمنون قط . وهذا تكليف بالجمع بين الضدين. وذلك لا يوجد البتة ، ويمتنع وجوده . فثبت : أن هؤلاء الذين أخبر الله عنهم أنهم لا يؤمنون : يستحيل صدور الإيمان عنهم ، والذين أخبر عنهم أنهم لا يؤمنون : يستحيل عدم الإيمان عنهم
The fourth: He, exalted, obligated those about whom He informed that they will never believe to have faith, and faith requires affirming the truth of God in everything He has informed about. And among what He informed about them is that they will never believe. So they become obligated to believe that they will never believe. This is an obligation to combine two opposites. That does not exist at all, and its existence is impossible. So it is established that those about whom God informed that they will not believe—the occurrence of faith from them is impossible, and those about whom He informed that they will not believe—the non-occurrence of faith from them is impossible.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is a proof from the nature of religious obligation (تكليف). God commands these people to believe, and belief entails affirming all of God's reports. Since God has reported that they will never believe, they must believe that they will never believe. But this is self-contradictory: they are asked to simultaneously believe (which implies they can believe) and believe that they will never believe (which implies they cannot believe). This is an obligation to hold two contradictory states, which is impossible. Hence, faith cannot actually occur from them.

Key Terms
تكليف
تصديق
جمع الضدين
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God directly commands the impossible, i.e., He commands disbelief or something inherently impossible.
Correction: The argument shows that the impossibility arises from the very act of believing, which includes believing in one's own inability to believe—a self-contradictory obligation, not a direct command of impossibility.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The foreknowledge of disbelief leads to a self-contradictory obligation, proving that faith cannot be actualized for those predestined to disbelieve.

The Fifth Rational Argument: God's Knowledge of the Contingent
The author argues that faith being possible does not make it necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that God's knowledge of faith does not necessitate faith, and why claiming otherwise would be contradictory.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of contingent things does not change their nature.

This lesson examines the fifth rational argument presented by the author. The argument tackles a crucial point: does God's act of knowing something transform it from possible to necessary? Using simple logic, the author shows that such a conclusion is impossible because it would force a contradiction. Let's follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage and Translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the English translation. Get a sense of the argument as a whole.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if...then...'.
2
Identify the Key Premise
The argument begins by stating that faith (iman) is contingent (mumkin) in itself. Why is this premise important? If faith were necessary or impossible, the argument would not work.
Contingent means its existence and non-existence are equally possible.
3
Understand the Reductio ad Absurdum
The author argues that if God's knowledge of a contingent thing made it necessary, then the same thing would be both contingent and necessary—a contradiction. Therefore, God's knowledge does not change the contingent nature.
This is a classic kalam argument using the impossibility of contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : إن الإيمان في نفسه من قبيل الممكنات ، نظراً إلى ذاته وعينه فوجب أن يعلمه الله من الممكنات الجائزات . إذ لو لم يكن كذلك ، لكان ذلك العلم جهلاً ، وهو محال . وإذا [ كان (۱) ] علم الله من الممكنات الجائزات ، التي لا يمتنع وجوده ولا عدمه - ولو صار بسبب العلم واجباً - لزم أن يجتمع على الشيء الواحد : كونه من الممكنات ، وكونه ليس من الممكنات . وهو محال
Fifth: Faith in itself is of the category of contingent things (mumkināt), with regard to its essence and itself, so it must be that God knows it as among the permissible contingent things (jāʼizāt). For if it were not so, that knowledge would be ignorance, and that is impossible. And if God’s knowledge [was] of permissible contingent things, whose existence and non-existence are not impossible—and even if it became necessary due to knowledge—then there would be combined in a single thing: its being from the contingent things, and its being not from the contingent things. And that is impossible.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth rational argument. It begins by stating that faith (īmān) in itself is a contingent thing (mumkin), meaning its existence and non-existence are equally possible. Therefore, God’s knowledge of it must be of something permissible and contingent (jāʼiz). If God knew it as necessary, that would be ignorance, which is impossible. The key point is that if God’s knowledge turned a contingent thing into a necessary one, then the same thing would be both contingent (since it is possible in itself) and non-contingent (due to knowledge), a contradiction. Hence, God’s knowledge does not necessitate the existence of faith.

Key Terms
الممكنات
الجائزات
العلم
الجهل
المحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some argue that God's knowledge of something makes it necessary, so faith is compelled by God.
Correction: The argument shows that if God's knowledge made a contingent thing necessary, a contradiction would arise—the same thing would be both contingent and non-contingent. Therefore, God's knowledge does not change the contingent nature of faith.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the fifth rational argument?
A) God's knowledge makes faith necessary.
B) Faith is impossible.
C) God's knowledge of faith does not make it necessary.
D) Faith is necessary in itself.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of faith does not necessitate faith; it remains a contingent act of the servant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your faith is a contingent act you choose, or have you resigned to the idea that God will simply decide it for you? What would change in your daily efforts if you fully embraced this truth?

?

The Sixth Rational Argument: Commanding the Impossible is Vain
The author argues that commanding the impossible would undermine prophethood and scripture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why commanding the impossible would destroy trust in prophethood and scripture, and how this argument refutes the deterministic implication of God's foreknowledge.

Core Idea
If God could command the impossible, no prophet or scripture could be trusted.

This lesson examines the sixth rational argument advanced by Imam al-Razi in al-Matalib al-‘Aliyah. The argument begins with a simple premise: commanding the impossible is futile and foolish. From this, the author draws a devastating hypothetical — if divine law could command the impossible in any instance, it could do so in all instances, thereby undermining the entire edifice of prophethood and revelation. By following this reasoning, you will see how the argument not only defends the coherence of divine commands but also dissolves a deterministic misconception about God's knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the logical flow from premise to conclusion.
Pay attention to the conjunctive phrasing: 'فلو جاز ... لجاز'.
2
Identify the central premise
The argument starts with a self-evident claim: commanding the impossible is عبث وسفه (futile and foolish). Why is this considered obvious in Islamic theology?
This premise is based on the wisdom of God.
3
Trace the hypothetical
The author reasons: if the Law could command the impossible in some cases, it could in all cases. Follow this conditional chain to its logical end.
The word 'الجاز' (lajāza) indicates the consequence.
4
Consider the devastating consequence
If the hypothetical holds, then miracles could be performed by liars and scriptures could contain falsehoods. No trust would remain in any prophet or the Quran.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Since the consequence is false, the premise that commanding the impossible is permissible must be false. This shows that God's knowledge of non-faith does not necessitate that faith becomes impossible.
The conclusion links to the broader Qadar debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : إن الأمر بالمحال : عبث وسفه ، فلو جاز ورود الشرع به في بعض الصور ، الجاز وروده به في بقية الصور. فوجب أن لا يمتنع من الباري تعالى : أن يظهر المعجزات على أيدي الكاذبين ، وأن لا تمتنع أقوال الكتب المشتملة على الكذب والأضاليل . وعلى هذا التقدير لا يبقى وثوق بصحة نبوة الأنبياء عليهم السلام ، ولا بصحة القرآن . بل يجوز أن يكون كله كذباً . ولما بطل ذلك ، علمنا : أن العلم بعدم الإيمان، والخبر عن عدم الإيمان ، لا يقتضي انقلاب الإيمان ممالاً
Sixth: Commanding the impossible is futile and foolish. If it were permissible for the Law to command it in some cases, it would be permissible for it to command it in the rest of the cases. Then it would be necessary that it is not impossible for the Exalted Creator to produce miracles at the hands of liars, and that the statements of scriptures containing lies and delusions are not impossible. On this basis, no trust would remain in the truth of the prophethood of the prophets (peace be upon them), nor in the truth of the Quran. Rather, it would be possible that all of it is false. Since that is false, we know that knowledge of non-faith and the report about non-faith do not necessitate that faith become something else.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth rational argument. It starts with a premise: commanding the impossible (al-muḥāl) is vain (ʻabath) and foolish (safah). If the Law (sharʻ) could command the impossible in any instance, then it could in all instances. This would mean it is not impossible for God to show miracles at the hands of liars, and for scriptures to contain lies and misguidance. Consequently, confidence in the truth of prophethood and the Quran would be lost. Since this conclusion is false, we know that God's knowledge of a person's non-faith and the divine report about it do not require that faith becomes something else (i.e., do not make it impossible).

Key Terms
الأمر بالمحال
عبث وسفه
الشرع
المعجزات
النبوة
القرآن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge of a person's non-faith makes it impossible for them to have faith.
Correction: God's knowledge does not necessitate the occurrence of the impossible; rather, commanding the impossible would be vain, and God's wisdom precludes it.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue would happen if commanding the impossible were allowed?
A) Only some miracles would be false.
B) All prophethood and scripture could be false.
C) Faith would become impossible.
D) God's knowledge would change.

Key Takeaway
Commanding the impossible would undermine the trustworthiness of all revelation, proving that God's wisdom prevents such futility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Commanding the Impossible: A Rational Refutation
The author uses analogies to show commanding the impossible is irrational.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how rational analogies demonstrate that commanding the impossible is not permissible, and that God's foreknowledge of unbelief does not make that command impossible.

Core Idea
If commanding the impossible were allowed, you could command a blind man to dot a Quran—absurd.

In this lesson, we examine the seventh rational argument from the text. The author uses vivid analogies—like commanding a blind man to dot a Quran—to show that commanding the impossible is intrinsically irrational. He then applies this principle to the case of God's command to the unbeliever, clarifying that God's knowledge of non-belief does not render the command impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the logical flow.
Notice the two parts: the analogies and the application to God's command.
2
Identify the analogies
List the three examples of impossible commands: blind man dotting Quran, paralyzed person flying, bound person thrown from cliff blamed for not flying.
These are all clearly impossible by common sense.
3
Understand the logical structure
The author uses reductio ad absurdum: if commanding the impossible were allowed, these absurd commands would be allowed. Since they are not, commanding the impossible is not allowed.
This is a rational argument based on intellectual rejection.
4
Apply to God's command
The author then notes that God commands the one He knows will not believe. This does not contradict the previous conclusion because knowledge of non-existence does not imply impossibility.
The command remains possible even if the outcome is known.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider how this argument refutes the idea that God's foreknowledge makes belief impossible for the predetermined unbeliever.
This is important for the debate on free will and divine knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : لو جاز ورود الأمر بالمحال في هذه الصورة ، لجاز ورود أمر الأعمى بنقط المصحف ، وورود أمر الزمن بالطيران في الهواء ، وأن يقال لمن قيد يداه ورجلاه ، وألقي من شاهق جبل : لم لم يطر إلى الفوق ؟ ولما لم يجز شيء من ذلك في العقول ، علمنا : أنه لا يجوز ورود الأمر بالمحال . ثم إنا أجمعنا على أن الله تعالى أمر من علم منه أنه لا يؤمن . فدل ذلك على أن العلم بالعدم ، لا يدل على الامتناع
Seventh: If commanding the impossible were permissible in this situation, then commanding a blind man to dot a copy of the Quran would be permissible, and commanding a paralyzed person to fly in the air, and it would be said to one whose hands and feet are bound and thrown from a mountain peak: 'Why did you not fly upward?' And since none of that is permissible in the intellects, we know that it is not permissible to command the impossible. Then we are all agreed that God Almighty commanded one whom He knows will not believe. That indicates that knowledge of non-existence does not indicate impossibility.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This is the seventh rational argument, which uses analogical reasoning. It argues by reductio ad absurdum: if commanding the impossible (al-muḥāl) were permissible in the case of commanding the unbeliever to believe when God knows he won't, then it would also be permissible to command a blind man to dot a Quran, a paralyzed person to fly, or to blame a bound person thrown from a cliff for not flying. Since these are clearly absurd, commanding the impossible is not permissible. And we all agree that God did command those whom He knows will not believe. Therefore, God's knowledge of non-existence (al-ʻilm bi-al-ʻadam) does not make the commanded act impossible—it is still possible for the person to choose, even though God knows the outcome.

Key Terms
الأمر بالمحال
الأعمى
الزمن
العلم بالعدم
الامتناع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's foreknowledge that someone will not believe means commanding them to believe is impossible.
Correction: The passage argues that commanding the impossible is not permissible, but God does command those He knows will not believe. Therefore, God's knowledge does not make the command impossible; it remains a possible command even though the person will not comply.

Checkpoint

A) That God can command anything.
B) That commanding the impossible is permissible in some cases.
C) That commanding the impossible is irrational and thus not permissible.
D) That the intellect cannot judge such matters.

Key Takeaway
Rational analogies prove that commanding the impossible is invalid, and God's foreknowledge does not negate the possibility of His commands.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Eighth Rational Argument: Sending Prophets to Inanimates
The author argues that allowing impossible commands would lead to absurdities like sending prophets to stones.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the absurdity of sending prophets to inanimate objects to prove that commanding the impossible is not permissible.

Core Idea
Allowing impossible commands reduces religion to mockery.

This lesson presents the eighth rational argument from the author's discussion on divine commands. The author employs a reductio ad absurdum: if God could command the impossible, then He could also send prophets to stones and books to rocks—a clearly ridiculous conclusion. This shows that the original premise must be false.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the conditional structure: 'if...then...'.
The Arabic uses 'لو...لجاز' to indicate a hypothetical.
2
Identify the premise and the absurd conclusion
The premise is 'commanding the impossible is permissible'. The conclusion drawn is that sending prophets to inanimates would be permissible. Note the list of absurdities: prophets, books, angels being sent to stones.
The author adds 'in every state' to emphasise the absurdity.
3
Understand why this is mockery
The author explicitly states that this is 'sukhriyya' (mockery) and 'talaʻub bi-al-dīn' (playing with religion). Reflect on why addressing obligations to stones is inherently ridiculous.
This judgment is the key: it shows the premise leads to an unacceptable outcome.
4
Reflect on the argument's force
Consider how this argument fits into the broader discussion of qadar. It shows that divine commands must have rational purpose and cannot be arbitrary.
The reductio works because the conclusion is clearly false, so the premise must be false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامن : لو جاز ورود الأمر بالمحال ، لجاز بعثة الأنبياء الى الجمادات ، وإنزال الكتب عليها ، وإنزال الملائكة عليها ، لتبليغ التكاليف إليها ، حالاً حال . ومعلوم أن ذلك سخرية ، وتلاعب بالدين
Eighth: If commanding the impossible were permissible, then sending prophets to inanimate objects would be permissible, and sending down books to them, and sending down angels to them, to convey obligations to them, in every state. And it is known that that is mockery and playing with religion.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This eighth argument continues the reductio. If it were possible to command the impossible (i.e., God could command the unbeliever to do what He knows he will not do), then sending prophets to inanimate objects (jamādāt) and revealing books to them would also be permissible. They would be addressed with obligations. But this is clearly mockery (sukhriyya) and playing with religion (talaʻub bi-al-dīn). Therefore, the original premise—that God commands what He knows to be impossible—is false.

Key Terms
الجمادات
بعثة الأنبياء
التكاليف
سخرية
تلاعب بالدين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God can command the impossible without any consequence.
Correction: The author shows that allowing impossible commands leads to clear absurdities like sending prophets to stones, which is mockery. Therefore, such commands are not possible.

Checkpoint
What does the author say would be the consequence of allowing impossible commands?
A) Prophets would only be sent to humans.
B) Prophets could be sent to inanimate objects.
C) The Quran would be sent to angels.
D) Religion would become more serious.

Key Takeaway
Allowing impossible commands leads to absurdities like sending prophets to stones, proving such commands are not possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Ninth Argument: Knowledge Does Not Necessitate Existence
The author argues that knowledge does not necessitate existence, contra the philosophers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's knowledge of an action does not make that action necessary, and how this preserves the role of divine will and power.

Core Idea
Knowledge alone does not make something happen; will and power are necessary.

This lesson presents the ninth rational argument from the author. The argument refutes the idea that God's knowledge of a thing necessitates its existence. If that were true, God's will and power would be superfluous, contradicting what we know about divine attributes. We will see how the author uses this conditional to distinguish the Sunni position from that of the philosophers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'.
The passage is short but packs a logical punch.
2
Identify the premise and consequence
The premise is: if knowledge of a thing necessitated its existence. The consequence is: then will and power would be unnecessary. See how the author derives that.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: the conclusion is false, so the premise must be false.
3
Recognize the philosophical position
The author links the rejected view to the philosophers (falāsifa) who say God is a necessary cause (al-mūjib). Understanding this helps you see what is at stake.
The Sunni position affirms that God acts with will and power, not by necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التاسع : إن العلم بوجود الشيء ، لو اقتضى وجوبه ، لأغنى هذا العلم عن الإرادة والقدرة ، فوجب أن لا تدل حدوث الأفعال على كونه تعالى قادراً مريدا . وذلك قول الفلاسفة القائلين بالموجب
Ninth: If knowledge of the existence of a thing necessitated its existence, then this knowledge would suffice without will and power. So it would be necessary that the occurrence of acts does not indicate that He, exalted, is powerful and willing. And that is the saying of the philosophers who affirm necessitation (al-mūjib).
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This ninth argument refutes the idea that God's knowledge of something makes it necessary. The author argues: if knowing a thing were enough to make it happen, then God's will (irāda) and power (qudra) would be superfluous. This would mean that the occurrence of actions in the world does not prove that God is powerful and willing—a view held by the philosophers (falāsifa) who say God is a necessary cause (al-mūjib bi-al-dhāt). Since we know that actions do depend on God's will and power, knowledge alone does not necessitate.

Misconception: Some think that God's knowledge of an event forces it to happen, making will and power irrelevant.
Correction: The author shows that if knowledge sufficed, will and power would be meaningless. But we know God acts with will and power, so knowledge does not necessitate.

Checkpoint
What would follow if God's knowledge of something made it necessary?
A) God would still need will and power.
B) God's will and power would be unnecessary.
C) Only some actions would be from will.
D) The philosophers would be refuted.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge does not make actions necessary; will and power are required, otherwise we would be like the philosophers who deny divine will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
This argument proves that God's will and power are real and active. When was the last time you cried out to Allah with the conviction that He can change your situation right now, not just because He knows it? How would your dua change if you truly believed His will is decisive?

Scriptural Evidence Against Impossible Burdens
Quoting verses and arguing against impossible burden.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Quranic verses and rational reasoning together demonstrate that God's foreknowledge does not make His commands impossible.

Core Idea
Quranic verses prove God never imposes impossible burdens.

In this lesson, we examine the tenth rational argument from al-Matalib al-Aliyah, which uses both scriptural and rational evidence to refute the idea that God's foreknowledge prevents action. The author cites four clear Quranic verses that state God does not burden a soul beyond its capacity, then provides a powerful logical deduction. This combined approach shows that divine commands are always possible for the one commanded.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verses
Read the four verses cited by the author. Notice how each emphasizes that God does not impose hardship or impossible burdens.
These verses are directly from the Quran.
2
Understand the rational argument
The author argues that if God's foreknowledge (that the act will not occur) prevented the act, then all obligations would be impossible—contradicting the verses. Therefore, foreknowledge does not prevent action.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Synthesize scriptural and rational proofs
See how the verses provide the premise that no impossible burden exists, and the rational argument uses this premise to deny that foreknowledge creates impossibility.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this argument clarifies the relationship between God's knowledge and human capacity. God's commands are always possible, even if He knows they will not be performed.
This refutes a common misconception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العاشر : الآيات الدالة على أن تكليف ما لا يطاق لم توجد . قال الله تعالى : لا يكلف الله نفساً إلا وسعها (۱) - ( وما جعل عليكم في الدين من حرج (۲) ) - ( ويضع عنهم إصرهم ، والأغلال التي كانت عليهم (۳) ) - ولا يريد بكم العسر (٤) ) ولو كان العلم بالعدم ، مانعاً من ذلك الفعل ، لكانت التكاليف بأسرها : تكليفاً [ بما لا يطاق (٥) ] فثبت بمجموع هذه الوجوه العقلية : أن العلم بالعدم ، لا يمنع من الفعل
Tenth: The verses indicating that obligation of what cannot be endured has not occurred. God Almighty said: 'God does not burden a soul except within its capacity' (2:286) and 'He has not placed upon you in religion any hardship' (22:78) and 'He removes from them their burden and the shackles that were upon them' (7:157) and 'He does not desire hardship for you' (2:185). And if knowledge of non-existence were a preventer of that act, then all obligations would be an obligation of what cannot be endured. So it is established by the total of these rational arguments that knowledge of non-existence does not prevent the act.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This is the tenth rational argument. The author presents Qur'anic verses that explicitly state God does not impose an impossible burden. He quotes: 'God does not burden a soul beyond its capacity' (Sūrat al-Baqara, 2:286), 'He has not placed upon you in religion any hardship' (Sūrat al-Ḥajj, 22:78), 'He removes from them their burden and the shackles that were upon them' (Sūrat al-Aʻrāf, 7:157), and 'He does not desire hardship for you' (Sūrat al-Baqara, 2:185). Then he argues: if God's foreknowledge (ʻilm bi-al-ʻadam) prevented the act, then all obligations would be impossible, contradicting these verses. Therefore, these rational arguments (wujūh ʻaqliyya) prove that God's knowledge of non-existence does not prevent the act itself.

Correction: The verses show God does not impose impossible burdens, and the rational argument shows that foreknowledge does not make an act impossible; God's commands are always within human capacity.

Checkpoint

B) That God does not impose impossible obligations.
C) That knowledge of non-existence prevents action.
D) That only rational arguments are needed.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of non-existence does not make His commands impossible; the Quran explicitly denies impossible burdens.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The First Scriptural Evidence: Quranic Blame Proves No Preventer
The author presents the first of six scriptural evidences against divine prevention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran's rhetorical questions about disbelief serve as evidence that God's foreknowledge does not prevent anyone from believing.

Core Idea
Quranic blame implies belief was possible.

The author begins his survey of scriptural evidences with a powerful observation: the Quran is full of verses that rebuke disbelief. These verses do not say 'God prevented you'; they ask 'What prevented you?' The implication is that no external force stood in the way — not even God's knowledge. In this lesson, we will examine this first evidence and see why the author considers it a strong argument against predestinarian interpretations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the author's claim that the Quran is filled with such verses.
The passage is incomplete due to OCR.
2
Identify the Quranic examples
The author gives four examples: 17:94, 4:39, 38:75, and Moses' question (cut off). Notice how each asks 'what prevented you?' in a rebuking tone.
The fourth example is incomplete.
3
Understand the logical argument
The author argues that if God's foreknowledge were a preventer, then these questions would be false. Therefore, they prove no preventer exists.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this evidence refutes the idea that God's knowledge compels disbelief. Ask yourself: Does this argument hold?
This connects to the broader debate on free will and predestination.

Source Text

وأما الوجوه السمعية فستة
الأول : إن القرآن مملوء من الآيات [ الدالة (۱) ] على أنه لا مانع لأحد من الإيمان . قال تعالى : وما منع الناس أن يؤمنوا إذ جاءهم الهدى (۷) ) ؟ وهو إنكار بلفظ الاستفهام ومعلوم أنه لو كان علم الله بعدم الإيمان ، وإخباره عن عدم الإيمان : مانعاً لصار قوله : ( وما منع الناس أن يؤمنوا ) : كذباً . وكذلك قوله : ( وماذا عليهم لو آمنوا (۸) ) ؟ وقوله لإبليس : ( ما منعك أن تسجد (۹) ) ؟ وقول موسى عليه السلام لأخيه : ( ما منعك إذ رأيتهم
And as for the scriptural evidences, they are six:
First: The Quran is filled with verses indicating that there is no preventer for anyone from faith. He, exalted, said: 'And what prevented the people from believing when guidance came to them?' (17:94) This is a denial in the form of a question. And it is known that if God's knowledge of non-faith and His report about non-faith were a preventer, then His saying 'And what prevented the people from believing' would be a lie. Likewise His saying: 'And what harm would it have done them if they had believed?' (4:39) and His saying to Iblis: 'What prevented you from prostrating?' (38:75) and the saying of Moses (peace be upon him) to his brother: 'What prevented you when you saw them...'
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This is the beginning of the scriptural evidences (al-wujūh al-samʻiyya), which the author introduces as six in number. The first evidence is drawn from the Quran itself. The Quran repeatedly asks, 'What prevented you from believing?' (e.g., Sūrat al-Isrāʼ, 17:94) in a tone of rebuke. The author argues: if God's foreknowledge of disbelief were a real preventer, then these Quranic questions would be false—because they imply the person could have believed. Similarly, verses like 'What harm would it have done them if they had believed?' (Sūrat al-Nisāʼ, 4:39) and God's question to Iblis, 'What prevented you from prostrating?' (Sūrat Ṣād, 38:75) and Moses' question to his brother (incomplete here) all presuppose that belief was possible. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the argument is clear: divine blame proves human ability.

The First Scriptural Proof: No Preventer from Faith
Concludes rational proofs and presents first scriptural proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses Quranic interrogatives to argue that God's foreknowledge does not prevent human actions.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of disbelief does not prevent faith; the Quran assumes no preventer exists.

The author has just concluded that God's knowledge of non-existence does not prevent actions, based on rational proofs. Now he turns to scriptural evidence, beginning with a category of verses that deny any preventer from faith. The first scriptural proof points to the Quran's many rhetorical questions that imply there is no obstacle to belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the transition
The passage begins by concluding the rational proofs and then introduces six scriptural proofs. The first of these is presented in detail.
The transition phrase 'وأما الوجوه السمعية فستة' marks the shift.
2
Identify the key Quranic verses
The author cites several Quranic verses in the form of rhetorical questions, such as 'And what prevented the people from believing?' and others. List them and observe their common feature.
All these verses imply that there is no valid preventer from belief.
3
Understand the logical argument
If God's knowledge and announcement of disbelief were a preventer, then these Quranic questions would be false because they deny any preventer. But the Quran is truthful, so knowledge cannot be a preventer.
This is an argument from the Quran's own consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العاشر : الآيات الدالة على أن تكليف ما لا يطاق لم توجد . قال الله تعالى : لا يكلف الله نفساً إلا وسعها - وما جعل عليكم في الدين من حرج - ويضع عنهم إصرهم، والأغلال التي كانت عليهم - ولا يريد بكم العسر . ولو كان العلم بالعدم، مانعاً من ذلك الفعل، لكانت التكاليف بأسرها : تكليفاً بما لا يطاق . فثبت بمجموع هذه الوجوه العقلية : أن العلم بالعدم، لا يمنع من الفعل . وأما الوجوه السمعية فستة . الأول : إن القرآن مملوء من الآيات الدالة على أنه لا مانع لأحد من الإيمان . قال تعالى : وما منع الناس أن يؤمنوا إذ جاءهم الهدى؟ وهو إنكار بلفظ الاستفهام ومعلوم أنه لو كان علم الله بعدم الإيمان، وإخباره عن عدم الإيمان : مانعاً لصار قوله : ( وما منع الناس أن يؤمنوا ) : كذباً . وكذلك قوله : ( وماذا عليهم لو آمنوا )؟ وقوله لإبليس : ( ما منعك أن تسجد )؟ وقول موسى عليه السلام لأخيه : ( ما منعك إذ رأيتهم ضلوا )؟ وقوله : ( فما لهم لا يؤمنون )؟ - فما لهم عن التذكرة معرضين؟ - عفا الله عنك . لم أذنت لهم؟ - ولم تحرم ما أحل الله لك؟ وهذا باب فيه إطناب . والقدر الذي ذكرناه : كافي .
The tenth: The verses indicating that obligation of what cannot be borne does not exist. Allah Almighty said: 'Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity' (2:286) – 'and He has not placed upon you in the religion any hardship' (22:78) – 'and He will remove from them their burden and the shackles which were upon them' (7:157) – 'and He does not intend for you hardship' (2:185). And if knowledge of non-existence were a preventer of that action, then all obligations would be obligation of what cannot be borne. So it is established by the totality of these rational proofs that knowledge of non-existence does not prevent the action. As for the scriptural proofs, they are six. The first: Indeed the Quran is filled with verses indicating that there is no preventer for anyone from faith. He Almighty said: 'And what prevented the people from believing when guidance came to them?' (17:94) – and it is a denial in the form of a question. It is known that if God's knowledge of non-belief and His informing about non-belief were a preventer, then His statement 'And what prevented the people from believing' would be a lie. Likewise His statement: 'And what [harm would come] upon them if they believed?' (4:39) – and His statement to Iblis: 'What prevented you from prostrating?' (38:75) – and the statement of Moses, peace be upon him, to his brother: 'What prevented you, when you saw them going astray?' (20:92) – and His statement: 'So what is [the matter] with them that they do not believe?' (84:20) – 'Then what is [the matter] with them that they turn away from the reminder?' (74:49) – 'May Allah pardon you, why did you give them permission?' (9:43) – 'And why do you prohibit what Allah has made lawful for you?' (66:1). This is an extensive topic; what we have mentioned is sufficient.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is a combination of a concluding remark on rational proofs and the first of six scriptural proofs. The author begins by stating that the tenth set of verses (from the rational section) shows that the obligation of what cannot be borne does not exist. He cites several Quranic verses that emphasize ease and absence of hardship in religion. He argues that if God's knowledge that an action will not occur were to prevent that action, then all obligations would be impossible because God knows the outcome of everything. This would lead to the absurd conclusion that all divine commands are obligations of what cannot be borne. Hence, it is established by these rational considerations that God's knowledge of non-existence does not prevent the action. Then he transitions to the scriptural proofs, stating there are six of them. He presents the first: The Quran is full of verses that deny any preventer from faith. For example, the verse 'And what prevented the people from believing when guidance came to them?' is a rhetorical question that implies there is no valid preventer. If God's foreknowledge and His announcement of disbelief were a preventer, then that verse would be false because it would imply that something (God's knowledge) is preventing them, contradicting the question. He lists several other similar Quranic interrogatives that all assume the absence of a preventer. The author concludes that the point is clear from these texts.

Third Scriptural Proof: Disbelievers' Claim of Impediment is Blamed
The author shows that God blames the disbelievers for claiming their hearts are veiled, which would be truthful if foreknowledge prevented faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how God's blame of the disbelievers' claim of impediment demonstrates that divine foreknowledge does not compel disbelief.

Core Idea
Foreknowledge does not prevent; blame proves choice.

This lesson presents the third scriptural proof using a Quranic verse. The disbelievers claimed they could not believe due to covered hearts and blocked ears. If God's foreknowledge were a genuine barrier, their claim would be true, yet God blames them for it. This argument shows that foreknowledge is not a coercive cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation to grasp the argument's structure.
Notice how the author presents the disbelievers' claim.
2
Identify the core logical move
The argument uses a conditional: if foreknowledge prevented faith, then the disbelievers would be truthful. Since God blames them, they are not truthful, so foreknowledge does not prevent.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider why this matters for the debate on qadar. It affirms human responsibility despite divine foreknowledge.
This proof is scriptural, not purely rational.

Commentary
This passage gives the third scriptural proof. God quotes the disbelievers as saying their hearts are covered and their ears are blocked from the message. If God's foreknowledge that they would not believe were a genuine preventer, then their claim of being unable to hear or understand would be true—they would have a valid excuse. But God does not accept this excuse; He blames them for saying this. This shows that the real preventer is not God's knowledge but their own rejection. Therefore, foreknowledge is not a barrier.

Fourth Scriptural Proof: Verse as Blame, Not Excuse
The author argues the verse blames disbelievers, proving they are capable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verse 'Indeed, those who disbelieve… they will not believe' serves as a blame that proves human responsibility, not an excuse due to predestination.

Core Idea
Blame proves ability; excuse proves inability.

We now examine the fourth scriptural proof from the Qur'an. The author cites a well-known verse that seems to imply that disbelievers are sealed and cannot believe. However, he argues that the verse is actually a form of blame, which would be unjust if they were genuinely prevented. This lesson unpacks that argument, showing how blame implies free will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Note the author's conclusion that the verse is for blame, not excuse.
The passage uses an analogy with the blind and paralyzed.
2
Understand the verse context
The verse (2:6) states that it is the same for disbelievers whether warned or not; they will not believe. Many interpret this as predestination.
The author argues that this interpretation misses the point.
3
Follow the logical argument
The author argues: if they were prevented from believing, they would be excused and not blamed. Since they are blamed, they must have the ability.
This is a simple conditional logic.
4
Connect to the broader issue of qadar
This proof shows that God's foreknowledge does not remove human responsibility; blame is evidence of free will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إنه تعالى إنما أنزل قوله : ( إن الذين كفروا . سواء عليهم ) إلى قوله : ( لا يؤمنون ) : ذماً لهم، وزجراً لهم، عن الكفر، وتقبيحاً لفعلهم . فلو كانوا ممنوعين عن الإيمان، غير قادرين عليه : ما استحقوا الذم البتة . بل كانوا معذورين فيه . كما يكون الأعمى معذوراً في أن لا يرى، والزمن في أن لا يمشى .
The fourth: He Almighty only revealed His statement: 'Indeed, those who disbelieve – it is all the same for them whether you warn them or do not warn them – they will not believe' (2:6) as blame for them and a deterrent from disbelief and an ugly depiction of their action. For if they were prevented from faith, not able to do it, they would not deserve any blame at all. Rather, they would be excused in it, just as the blind is excused for not seeing, and the paralyzed for not walking.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is the fourth scriptural proof. The author cites the well-known verse from Surah Al-Baqarah (2:6) which states that it is the same for the disbelievers whether they are warned or not; they will not believe. The author argues that this verse is revealed to blame and censure the disbelievers and to show the ugliness of their action. If they were actually prevented from believing—if God's foreknowledge made them incapable—then they would not deserve any blame; they would be excused like a blind person who cannot see or a lame person who cannot walk. Since they are blamed, it shows they have the ability and are not prevented by God's knowledge. Therefore, foreknowledge does not prevent faith.

Key Terms
الذم
العذر
الممنوع
القادر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse proves that disbelievers cannot believe because God has sealed their hearts.
Correction: The verse is a blame, not a statement of inability. Blame only makes sense if they could have done otherwise.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth proof, how does the verse 'Indeed, those who disbelieve – it is all the same for them whether you warn them or not – they will not believe' demonstrate that foreknowledge does not prevent faith?
A) Because the verse blames them for their disbelief, which would be unjust if they were prevented.
B) Because the verse says they will not believe, which means they cannot believe.
C) Because the verse says they are excused.
D) Because the verse is about the Prophet's warning.

Key Takeaway
The verse about disbelievers is a divine blame, which proves they have the ability to believe, thus foreknowledge does not force disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The verse is blame for disbelievers for not having iman. As a believer, you are not blamed for disbelief, but are you ever blamed for neglecting obligations? How does this lesson affect your view of divine decree and your own choices? Commit to one area where you will take full responsibility this week.
?
Reflect on a time you used Allah's knowledge as an excuse for inaction or sin. After this lesson, what will you do differently when faced with a difficult act of obedience? Will you trust that you are able and act, or will you fall back on excuses?

Five Proofs That God's Knowledge Does Not Prevent Belief
Al-Matalib presents five proofs that God's foreknowledge does not prevent belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand five Quranic and rational arguments demonstrating that God's foreknowledge of disbelief does not make belief impossible.

Core Idea
God's knowledge does not compel action; His blame proves human ability.

This passage from al-Matālib al-ʿĀliyya presents five interconnected proofs against the claim that divine foreknowledge prevents faith. Each proof uses Quranic verses and logical reasoning to show that if knowledge were a barrier, it would undermine divine justice and blame. The author systematically dismantles the notion that foreknowledge equals compulsion.

Guided Path
1
Read the Full Arabic Passage
Begin by reading the Arabic passage from 'الثاني : إنه تعالى قال' to the end. Let the author's words sink in before analyzing.
Notice that the passage enumerates five distinct arguments (marked الثاني through السادس) plus a conclusion.
2
Identify Each Argument and Its Quranic Basis
For each of the five arguments, pinpoint the Quranic verse cited and the logical consequence the author draws. Write them down.
The second argument uses verses about messengers removing excuses; the third uses the disbelievers' claim about covered hearts; the fourth uses the verse 'Indeed, those who disbelieve...'; the fifth uses the purpose of the Qur'an; the sixth uses 'What an excellent Protector.'
3
Understand the Logical Structure
Notice that each argument follows a reductio ad absurdum: if God's knowledge prevented belief, then an absurd or unjust result would follow (e.g., no blame, no answer to disbelievers' objection).
This shows that the initial claim leads to contradictions with clear Quranic teachings and common sense.
4
Grasp the Conclusion
Read the concluding sentence: 'Thus it is established by these proofs that Allah's knowledge of the absence of belief and His information about the absence of belief do not indicate that that belief is impossible to obtain.'
The conclusion directly refutes the misconception that foreknowledge entails compulsion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إنه تعالى قال : رسلاً مبشرين ومنذرين لئلا يكون للناس على الله : حجة بعد الرسل (٦) ) وقال : ( ولو أنا أهلكناهم بعذاب من قبله . لقالوا : ربنا لولا أرسلت إلينا رسولاً . فتتبع آياتك من قبل أن نذل ونخزی (۷)) بين تعالى أنه ما أبقى لهم عذراً ، إلا وقد أزاله عنهم . ولو كان علمه بكفرهم ، وخبره عن كفرهم : بياناً لهم عن الإيمان ، لكان ذلك من أعظم الأعذار ، وأقوى الوجوه الدافعة للعقاب عنهم . ولما لم يكن كذلك ، علمنا : أن العلم بعدم الإيمان ، لا يمنع من الإيمان . الثالث : إنه تعالى حكى عن الكفار في ( حم السجدة أنهم قالوا : قلوبنا في أكنة مما تدعونا إليه . وفي آذاننا وقر (۸) ) وإنما ذكر تعالى هذه الحكاية عنهم ، ذماً لهم على هذا القول . ولو كان العلم بعدم الإيمان ، مانعاً من الإيمان . لكانوا صادقين في هذا القول . فلم ذمهم عليه ؟ الرابع : إنه تعالى إنما أنزل قوله : ( إن الذين كفروا . سواء عليهم ) إلى قوله : ( لا يؤمنون ) : ذماً لهم ، وزجراً لهم ، عن الكفر ، وتقبيحاً لفعلهم . فلو كانوا ممنوعين عن الإيمان ، غير قادرين عليه : ما استحقوا الذم البتة . بل كانوا معذورين فيه . كما يكون الأعمى معذوراً في أن لا يرى ، والزمن في أن لا يمشى . الخامس : القرآن إنما أنزل ليكون حجة الله ولرسوله على الكفار ، لا أن يكون حجة للكفار على الله وعلى رسوله . ولو كان العلم والخبر مانعين عن الإيمان ، لكان لهم أن يقولوا : لما اعترفت (۱) بأن الله علم الكفر منا ، وأخبر عن حصول الكفر فينا - وهذا من أعظم الموانع لنا عن الإيمان - فلم يطلب المحال منا ؟ ولم يأمرنا بالمحال ؟ ومعلوم : أن هذا مما لا جواب الله و [ لا(۲) ] لرسوله عنه ، لو (۳) ثبت : أن العلم والخبر يمنع عن الإيمان : السادس : إنه تعالى قال : ( فنعم المولى ونعم النصير (1) ) ولو أنه أمر بالإيمان ، مع حصول المانع منه : لما كان نعم المولى ونعم النصير، بل كان بئس المولى وبئس النصير . ومعلوم : أن ذلك : كفر فثبت بهذه الوجوه : أن علم الله بعدم الإيمان ، وخبره عن عدم الإيمان : لا يدل على كون ذلك الإيمان ممتنع الحصول .
The second: He, exalted, said: 'Messengers as bearers of good news and warners so that mankind will have no argument against Allah after the messengers.' And He said: 'And if We had destroyed them with a punishment before it, they would have said: Our Lord, why did You not send us a messenger so that we might follow Your verses before we are humiliated and disgraced.' He, exalted, made clear that He did not leave any excuse for them except that He removed it from them. And if His knowledge of their disbelief and His information about their disbelief were a deterrent to them from belief, that would be one of the greatest excuses and the strongest grounds for averting punishment from them. And since it is not so, we know that knowledge of the absence of belief does not prevent belief. Third: He, exalted, related from the disbelievers in Surah Fussilat that they said: 'Our hearts are covered against that to which you invite us, and in our ears is deafness.' He only related this account from them to blame them for this statement. And if knowledge of the absence of belief were a preventer of belief, they would have been truthful in this statement, so why blame them for it? Fourth: He, exalted, only sent down His saying: 'Indeed, those who disbelieve, it is the same to them...' to His saying: '...they will not believe' as a blame for them, a deterrent from disbelief, and a condemnation of their action. If they were prevented from belief and unable to do it, they would not deserve any blame at all. Rather, they would be excused in it, just as the blind is excused for not seeing and the paralyzed for not walking. Fifth: The Qur'an was only sent down to be the argument of Allah and His messenger against the disbelievers, not to be an argument for the disbelievers against Allah and His messenger. And if knowledge and information prevented belief, they would have said: 'Since you acknowledge that Allah knew disbelief from us and informed of the occurrence of disbelief in us—and this is one of the greatest preventers for us from belief—why does He demand the impossible from us? And why does He command us to do the impossible?' And it is known that there is no answer from Allah or His messenger to this, if it were established that knowledge and information prevent belief. Sixth: He, exalted, said: 'What an excellent Protector and what an excellent Helper!' If He commanded belief while a preventer existed, He would not be an excellent Protector and an excellent Helper; rather, He would be an evil Protector and an evil Helper. And it is known that that is disbelief. Thus it is established by these proofs that Allah's knowledge of the absence of belief and His information about the absence of belief do not indicate that that belief is impossible to obtain.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage presents five arguments (the second through sixth) against the claim that God's foreknowledge or predestination prevents human beings from having faith. Each argument draws on Quranic verses and rational reasoning to show that if God's knowledge of disbelief were an actual impediment, it would lead to absurdities and contradictions in divine justice and wisdom.

The second argument uses verses about sending messengers. If God's knowledge of disbelief were a deterrent, then sending messengers would not remove excuses; rather, God would have left an excuse for the disbelievers. Since God emphasizes that messengers remove excuses, His knowledge cannot be a barrier.

The third argument cites the disbelievers' claim that their hearts are covered. God blames them for this claim. If their disbelief were truly prevented by God's knowledge, they would be truthful, not blameworthy.

The fourth argument discusses the verse 'Indeed, those who disbelieve...' which blames them. If they were incapable of belief, they would be excused like the blind or paralyzed. Since God blames them, they must have ability.

The fifth argument states that the Qur'an is a proof for God and His messenger against the disbelievers. If God's knowledge prevented belief, the disbelievers could argue that God is demanding the impossible, and there would be no answer. Thus, such prevention cannot be true.

The sixth argument uses the verse 'What an excellent Protector...' If God commanded belief while preventing it, He would not be an excellent Protector. This would be blasphemous, so prevention cannot be true.

The conclusion draws all five together: God's knowledge of disbelief does not imply that belief is impossible. The arguments collectively establish that foreknowledge does not entail compulsion.

Key Terms
علم الله
الإيمان
الكفر
الرسل
الحجة
المانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God knows someone will disbelieve, that person cannot possibly believe because the knowledge predetermines the outcome.
Correction: The passage shows that God's knowledge does not compel action; it simply knows what people will freely choose. Blame and commands only make sense if people have genuine ability.

Checkpoint
In the fifth argument, what would the disbelievers be able to claim if God's knowledge prevented belief?
A) That they are excused because they are blind
B) That God is demanding the impossible from them
C) That they have free will to choose belief
D) That the messengers were sent in vain

Key Takeaway
God's foreknowledge of disbelief does not make belief impossible; it is simply knowledge of what people freely choose, and the Quranic blame confirms human ability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that God's knowledge does not prevent belief. Are there areas in your life where you have used the excuse of 'it is already written' to avoid striving in faith or obedience? What would it mean for you to truly believe that your choices matter despite God's knowledge?
?
The passage argues that if disbelief were caused by God's knowledge, disbelievers would be excused like the blind. When you sin, do you ever think, 'I was destined to make this mistake'? How does this lesson call you to take full responsibility for your actions instead?
?
Consider the sixth proof: if God commanded belief while preventing it, He would not be an excellent Protector. Do you truly believe that God's commands are always coupled with your ability to fulfill them? Trust that He has given you the capacity to believe, even when you feel weak.

First Mu'tazilite Response: The Logical Error in the Conditional
The first Mu'tazilite way: analyzing the conditional on divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first Mu'tazilite argument that the conditional 'If something other than God's knowledge occurs, then God's knowledge becomes ignorance' is logically flawed because it links an impossibility to a possibility.

Core Idea
God's knowledge cannot change; the conditional itself is invalid, not foreknowledge.

The Mu'tazila were not content to simply deny that God's knowledge could change. Instead, they analyzed the logical structure of the conditional 'If something other than what God knows occurs...' and found it to be an invalid linkage of an impossibility (knowledge becoming ignorance) to a possibility (the event occurring). This lesson unpacks their subtle reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the Mu'tazila are responding to an objection by analyzing the conditional statement itself, not by affirming or denying divine knowledge.
2
Identify the key terms 'المحال' and 'الممكن'
These terms are crucial. Understand that 'المحال' means impossible and 'الممكن' means possible. The Mu'tazila argue that the conditional links an impossible consequence (knowledge becoming ignorance) to a possible antecedent (the event occurring).
3
Understand the conclusion: الإمساك عن القولين
The Mu'tazila conclude that one must refrain from both saying that knowledge becomes ignorance and that it does not. Because the entire conditional is invalid; the premise is impossible, so no judgment about its consequent is warranted.
This is a subtle logical point aimed at preserving God's immutability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب على سبيل التفصيل : فاعلم : أن للمعتزلة فيه طريقين : الأول : طريقة ( أبي علي » و « أبي هاشم » و « القاضي عبد الجبار بن ) أن قالوا : إن قول القائل : لو وقع خلاف معلوم الله ، لانقلب علم الله جهلاً . قالوا : خطأ من يقول : إله ينقلب علمه جهلا . لأن هذا تعليق للمحال بالممكن . فإن انقلاب علم الله جهلا محال . ووقوع علم الله أنه لا يقع ممكن . فقولنا : لو وقع ما علم الله أنه لا يقع تعليق المحال بالممكن فكان خطأ . وخطأ أيضاً : قبول من يقول : إنه لا ينقلب جهلا . ولكن يجب الإمساك عن القولين .
As for the detailed answer: Know that the Mu'tazila have two ways regarding this. The first: The way of Abu 'Ali, Abu Hashim, and Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar ibn [...], that they said: The statement of one who says: 'If something other than what Allah knows were to occur, then Allah's knowledge would become ignorance' is mistaken because it links an impossibility to a possibility. Indeed, the transformation of Allah's knowledge into ignorance is impossible. And the occurrence of what Allah knows will not occur is possible. So our statement: 'If what Allah knows will not occur were to occur' links an impossibility to a possibility, so it is mistaken. And it is also mistaken to accept the statement of one who says: 'It does not become ignorance.' But one must refrain from both statements.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is the first of two Mu'tazilite responses to the problem of divine foreknowledge and human free will. The author presents it as part of a 'detailed answer' to an earlier objection. 

The Mu'tazila argue that the statement 'If something other than what Allah knows were to occur, then Allah's knowledge would become ignorance' is logically flawed. They analyze it as a conditional linking an impossibility to a possibility. The impossibility is that God's knowledge would change or become ignorance – that is impossible. The possibility is that something could occur that God knows will not occur – that is possible in itself (since God's knowledge does not compel the action). So the conditional is false: it tries to link an impossible consequent (God's knowledge becoming ignorance) to a possible antecedent (the occurrence of something God knows will not happen). 

They say it is also mistaken to accept the opposite claim that God's knowledge would not become ignorance in such a scenario (perhaps meaning one should not assert either that knowledge changes or that it doesn't, because the scenario itself is impossible). The correct position is to refrain from both statements (الإمساك عن القولين) – that is, one should not say that knowledge would become ignorance, nor that it would not, because the entire conditional is invalid. This is a subtle logical point aimed at preserving God's immutability while not denying human capacity.

Key Terms
المحال
الممكن
انقلاب العلم
تعليق المحال بالممكن
الإمساك عن القولين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila are denying that God knows future events.
Correction: They are not denying foreknowledge; they are analyzing the logical form of a conditional statement about it. They affirm that God's knowledge is immutable and that the antecedent is possible, but the conditional as a whole is invalid.

Checkpoint
According to the first Mu'tazilite way, what is wrong with the statement 'If something other than what Allah knows occurs, then Allah's knowledge becomes ignorance'?
A) It denies divine foreknowledge
B) It links an impossibility (knowledge becoming ignorance) to a possibility (the event occurring)
C) It asserts that God's knowledge can change
D) It is a valid conditional

Key Takeaway
The first Mu'tazilite response shows that the conditional about God's knowledge changing is logically flawed because it ties an impossible outcome to a possible event, so one must refrain from making any claim about it.
