The Second Mu'tazilite Response: Knowledge Follows the Known
Al-Matalib presents the second Mu'tazilite way: knowledge is dependent on the known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite principle that knowledge follows the known and how it is used to argue that God's eternal knowledge is dependent on actual human actions.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is determined by what actually happens, not the other way around.

This lesson introduces the second Mu'tazilite approach to the problem of divine foreknowledge and human free will. The proponents, led by al-Ka'bi and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, argue that knowledge follows the known—meaning that God's eternal knowledge is based on what the servant actually does. This principle aims to preserve human freedom while maintaining God's omniscience. Read the passage carefully, noting how the argument applies to both belief and its absence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the principle
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. The key principle is 'knowledge follows the known' (العلم تبع للمعلوم). Understand that this means God's knowledge is dependent on the actual event.
The passage is incomplete; the second scenario cuts off, but the intended meaning is that if disbelief occurs, God's knowledge would be of disbelief.
2
Identify the proponents
The passage names al-Ka'bi and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri as advocates of this view. Why might this be significant? They represent a major Mu'tazilite school.
3
Apply the principle to both possibilities
The passage gives one full scenario (belief) and begins another (absence of belief). Complete the second scenario in your mind: if the servant does not believe, God's knowledge is of that disbelief. This shows the principle is consistent.
This avoids the problem of God's knowledge forcing action.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this differs from the first Mu'tazilite response (not covered here). What does it say about God's knowledge and human will? The principle makes knowledge dependent on the actual event, preserving free will.
The common misunderstanding is that this implies God's knowledge changes; but the Mu'tazila insist it is eternal and unchanging, simply determined by what occurs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : طريقة ( الكعبي ، واختيار ( أبي الحسين البصري ، وهو أن العلم تبع للمعلوم. فإذا فرضنا أن الصادر من العبد هو الإيمان ، كان الحاصل في أزل الله تعالى هو العلم بوجود الإيمان . وإذا فرضنا أن الواقع منه هو عدم
The second: The way of al-Ka'bi and the choice of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, which is that knowledge follows the known. So if we suppose that what emanates from the servant is belief, then what was obtained in God's pre-eternity would be knowledge of the existence of belief. And if we suppose that what occurs from him is the absence
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage presents the second Mu'tazilite approach to the problem of divine foreknowledge and human freedom. It is given as an alternative to the first way. The core principle is 'knowledge follows the known' (العلم تبع للمعلوم). This means that God's eternal knowledge is determined by what actually happens; it does not predetermine it. 

The Mu'tazila argue from this principle: if we suppose that a person actually does believe, then from eternity God's knowledge would have been that the person believes. If we suppose the person disbelieves, God's knowledge would have been that. The passage cuts off mid-sentence at 'if we suppose that what occurs from him is the absence...' but the intended completion is clear: if disbelief occurs, then God's eternal knowledge is of disbelief. This approach avoids the problem of God's knowledge forcing action by making knowledge dependent on the actual event. The Mu'tazila thus preserve human free will without attributing ignorance or change to God.

Key Terms
العلم تبع للمعلوم
أزل الله
الصادر من العبد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this doctrine implies God's knowledge changes or is contingent on events.
Correction: The Mu'tazila hold that God's knowledge is eternal and unchanging; it is simply dependent on what actually occurs, not that it changes. The principle 'knowledge follows the known' means that the content of God's knowledge corresponds to the actual event, not that God learns new things.

Checkpoint

A) God's will
B) The actual action of the servant
C) The servant's foreknowledge
D) The pre-eternal decree

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite principle 'knowledge follows the known' argues that God's eternal knowledge is determined by actual human actions, preserving free will while maintaining omniscience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
The Mu'tazilites argue that if God forced belief, then belief would be involuntary. In your own heart, do you truly believe that your faith is a free response to truth, or do you sometimes hide behind predestination? Commit to one concrete step to take responsibility for your faith today.

Answer to the Third Objection: God's Knowledge Does Not Negate Human Power
The author answers the third objection by distinguishing between the cause of necessity (power+motive) and God's knowledge (unveiling).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how divine knowledge does not directly necessitate human actions, but rather unveils the necessity created by God through the servant's power and motive.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge unveils necessity, it does not create it.

In this passage, the author responds to the third objection that if an act is known by God to occur, the servant would have no power. He clarifies that the act's necessity arises not from God's knowledge, but from the combination of power and motive that God creates in the servant. This preserves both divine foreknowledge and human agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the third objection as stated: if the act is known to be necessary, the servant has no power. Understand the concern.
2
Examine the answer's structure
The author first refers to a previous proof, then explains the true cause of necessity.
3
Identify the effective factor
Note that the author says the effective factor is the totality of power and motive (مجموع القدرة مع الداعي) created by God in the servant.
4
Understand the role of knowledge
God's knowledge merely unveils (يكشف) this created necessity; it does not cause it. Reflect on how this resolves the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الثالث: وهو قوله: « ولو كان ذلك المعلوم، واجب الوقوع، لوجب أن لا يبقى للعبد قدرة على الفعل أصلاً، فجوابه: ما تقدم في البرهان الأول: أن ذلك الفعل [ما] صار واجب الوقوع بسبب العلم بل لأن القادر المختار، خلق في العبد ما يوجب حصول ذلك الفعل وهو مجموع القدرة مع الداعي. فالمؤثر في وجوب ذلك الفعل: هو هذا المعنى. أما تعلق علم الله فهو يكشف عن حصول هذا. لا أنه هو الموجب الوجوب
As for the third question: which is his statement: 'And if that known [thing] were necessary of occurrence, then it would be necessary that the servant should have no power over the act at all,' its answer: what preceded in the first proof: that that act became necessary of occurrence not because of knowledge, but because the powerful chooser created in the servant what necessitates the occurrence of that act, which is the totality of power together with the motive. So the effective factor in the necessity of that act is this meaning. As for the attachment of God's knowledge, it unveils the occurrence of this. It is not itself the necessitator of necessity.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This answers the third objection: if the act is necessitated by God's knowledge, then the servant has no power. The author reiterates his earlier proof that the act is not necessitated by knowledge but by God's creation in the servant of the combination of power and motive (the causal factors). God's knowledge simply reveals this created necessity; it does not cause it. Thus the servant's power is not negated; rather, God creates it and the motive that leads to the act. The necessity arises from God's creative act, not from His knowledge per se.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Divine knowledge directly necessitates human actions, leaving no room for human power.
Correction: Divine knowledge does not necessitate; it unveils the necessity that arises from the combination of power and motive which God creates in the servant.

Checkpoint

A) God's knowledge alone.

C) The servant's absolute free will.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge does not cause the necessity of an act; rather, it unveils the necessity created by the servant's power and motive, which are themselves created by God.

?

?

?

Divine Knowledge Does Not Negate Human Power
The author refutes that divine knowledge of the act negates the servant's power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's knowledge of an act does not remove the servant's ability to act, and how the combination of power and motivation is the real cause.

Core Idea
Knowledge discloses; power and motivation necessitate.

This lesson addresses a common theological objection: if God knows an act will happen, how can the servant have free power? The author refers back to a previous proof and clarifies that knowledge is merely descriptive, not causative. The real cause of an act's occurrence is the combination of power (qudra) and motivation (dāʿī) that God creates in the servant.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection: the objector argues that if an act is known by God and thus necessary, then the servant can have no power over it at all.
This is a common challenge to human agency under divine foreknowledge.
2
Follow the author's answer
The author refers to an earlier proof: the act's necessity is not because of knowledge, but because God creates in the servant the combination of power (qudra) and motivation (dāʿī). This combination is the true cause of the act.
The root cause is ontological, not epistemic.
3
Grasp the key distinction
Knowledge (ʿilm) is only a 'discloser' (kāshif) of the causal chain; it does not impose necessity. The servant's power remains real and effective.
This preserves human responsibility without compromising divine knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الثالث: وهو قوله: «ولو كان ذلك المعلوم، واجب الوقوع، لوجب أن لا يبقى للعبد قدرة على الفعل أصلاً» فجوابه: ما تقدم في البرهان الأول: أن ذلك الفعل [ما] صار واجب الوقوع بسبب العلم بل لأن القادر المختار، خلق في العبد ما يوجب حصول ذلك الفعل وهو مجموع القدرة مع الداعي. فالمؤثر في وجوب ذلك الفعل: هو هذا المعنى. أما تعلق علم الله فهو يكشف عن حصول هذا. لا أنه هو الموجب الوجوب.
As for the third question: his saying: 'If that known thing were necessary of occurrence, then it would be necessary that the servant have no power over the act at all.' So the answer: What preceded in the first proof: that that act did not become necessary of occurrence because of knowledge, but rather because the free agent created in the servant what necessitates the attainment of that act, which is the combination of power and motivation. So the influencer in the necessity of that act is this meaning. As for the attachment of God's knowledge, it discloses the attainment of this. It is not that it is the necessitator of necessity.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This is an objection-answer passage. The objector claims that if the act is necessary due to God's knowledge, the servant loses all power. The author refers to a previous proof: the act's necessity is not from knowledge but from God creating in the servant a combination of power (qudra) and motivation (dāʿī). This combination is the true cause of the act's necessity. Knowledge (ʿilm) merely discloses (kashf) this causal nexus. Thus the servant's power remains intact.

Key Terms

القدرة
الداعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God knows I will do X, then I have no real power to avoid X.
Correction: God's knowledge discloses what will happen due to the causal factors He creates, including the servant's power and motivation. Knowledge does not force the act.

Checkpoint

A) God's knowledge
B) The combination of power and motivation
C) The servant's free choice alone
D) The essence of the act

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge reveals the necessity of an act, but the necessity itself comes from the power and motivation He creates in the servant.

?

Commit to one small act where you consciously activate both power and motivation.

If God's knowledge only discloses what you will do, not forces it, how does that change your responsibility for your next decision? Before you sleep, recall a moment today when you blamed God's decree for your action. Repent of that evasion and resolve to own your choices.
?
The author says the act's necessity comes from 'power together with motivation.' What is the state of your dāʿī (motivation) right now toward Allah? Be honest: is it weak, divided, or absent? What one step can you take tonight to strengthen your inner drive without waiting for a 'feeling'?

The Command to the Disbeliever Does Not Imply Ignorance
The author answers the objection that commanding faith implies charging God with ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the impossibility of the command itself and the impossibility of the commanded act, and how the author defends the view that commanding the disbeliever is a command of the impossible.

Core Idea
Commanding the impossible does not make God ignorant.

This passage addresses a key objection: if God commands the disbeliever to believe, and the disbeliever will never believe, does that not mean God is commanding something impossible — and therefore implying ignorance? The author clarifies by drawing a subtle but crucial distinction between the command itself and the attainment of the commanded thing. Understanding this nuance is essential for grasping the Ash'ari position on taklīf.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Begin by reading the objection as presented by the questioner: that commanding the disbeliever to faith would be commanding tajhīl and takdhīb of God. Notice the charge: this command would imply God's ignorance.
The objection is the fourth question in a series.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The author responds by splitting the objection into two possible meanings. One concerns the command itself (does the very act of commanding require ignorance?), and the other concerns the attainment of the commanded (does the fulfillment of the command require ignorance?).
This is the key analytical move.
3
Understand the author's response
The author denies the first meaning (the command itself does not require ignorance) but affirms the second meaning (the attainment of the commanded act would indeed require ignorance). Note that affirming the second meaning is exactly the author's original position.
The author does not deny that the commanded act is impossible.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that the command is in reality a command of the impossible (al-muḥāl). This is consistent with the Ash'ari doctrine that God may command what He knows will not happen.
The impossible here is not logically impossible but impossible in terms of actual occurrence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الرابع: [وهو] قوله: (ولو كان الأمر كما قلتم، لكان أمر الكافر بالإيمان: أمراً بتجهيل الله تعالى وبتكذيبه، قلنا: إن عنيتم به: إن أمر الكافر بالإيمان، لا يتأتى إلا مع تجهيل [الله] ومع تكذيبه. فهذا ممنوع. وإن عنيتم أن حصول المأمور به، لا يمكن إلا مع تجهيل [الله] وتكذيبه. فهذا هو الذي قلناه. وزعمنا: أن هذا الأمر في الحقيقة: أمر بالمحال، الذي لا يمكن إيقاعه صار محالاً.
As for the fourth question: his saying: 'If the matter were as you claim, then commanding the disbeliever to faith would be a command to regard God as ignorant and to deny Him.' We say: If you mean that commanding the disbeliever to faith cannot occur except with regarding God as ignorant and denying Him, then this is denied. And if you mean that the attainment of the commanded thing cannot occur except with regarding God as ignorant and denying Him, then this is what we said. And we claimed: that this command, in reality, is a command of the impossible, which cannot be actualized. It became impossible.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer passage. The objector claims that the author's view entails that commanding the disbeliever to believe is a command to regard God as ignorant (tajhīl) and as a liar (takdhīb). The author distinguishes two meanings: (1) If the command itself is impossible without tajhīl and takdhīb, that is denied; (2) If the attainment of the commanded act (i.e., the disbeliever's actual belief) is impossible without tajhīl and takdhīb, then this is exactly what the author maintains. The command is an instance of commanding the impossible (al-muḥāl).

Key Terms
التجهيل
التكذيب
الأمر بالمحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Commanding the disbeliever to faith implies that God is ignorant or a liar.
Correction: The author shows that the command itself does not require ignorance; only the attainment of the commanded act would require ignorance, which is why the command is of the impossible. Thus, God remains omniscient and truthful.

Checkpoint
What does the author say the command to the disbeliever amounts to?
A) A command of the possible
B) A command of the impossible
C) A command of the ignorant
D) A command of the free

Key Takeaway
Commanding the impossible does not entail ignorance of God; it only acknowledges that the commanded act cannot be actualized.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you struggle to obey Allah's commands, do you ever find yourself questioning His wisdom or fairness? How does this lesson's distinction between the command and the attainment challenge you to trust Him even in your failures?
?
The author says the command itself is real even if you cannot fulfill it. How does that reshape your attitude toward a sin you keep repeating? Will you now see the command as a sign of Allah's mercy rather than a burden?
?
If Allah commands something He knows you will not do, what does that say about your responsibility? Are you ready to take full ownership of your disobedience without blaming His decree?

Dismissing Sophisms and the Reality of Taklif Mā Lā Yuṭāq
Author dismisses remaining objections as sophistry and defends taklīf mā lā yuṭāq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author dismisses remaining objections as sophistry and argues that commanding the impossible is a reality, supported by rational proofs that are not open to interpretation.

Core Idea
Scriptural proofs are interpretable; rational proofs are not — so the argument stands.

After presenting his main arguments, the author now addresses remaining objections. He labels them as sophisms (shubahāt) and reiterates his core position: commanding the impossible occurs because God creates motivations for disbelief in the disbeliever and then commands faith. He then contrasts the status of scriptural evidence, which is open to interpretation, with his own rational proofs, which are definitive. This lesson focuses on that dismissal and the foundational claim about taklīf mā lā yuṭāq.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the author's confident tone as he dismisses objections.
The passage begins with 'وأما بقية الوجوه'.
2
Identify the two main claims
The author makes two key points: (1) the remaining objections are sophisms, and (2) commanding the impossible (taklīf mā lā yuṭāq) is a reality. Find the evidence he gives for the second claim.
He says God creates motivations for disbelief and then commands faith.
3
Contrast scriptural and rational proofs
Notice how the author asserts that scriptural proofs are 'qābila li-l-taʾwīl' (open to interpretation) while his rational proofs are not. This is a methodological move to privilege reason over scripture in this debate.
4
Reflect on the outcome
The author concludes that 'al-tarjīḥ min jānibinā' (the preponderance is on our side). Ask yourself: why does he think this follows from what he has said?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بقية الوجوه: فهي شبهات يذكرونها في أن الأمر بالمحال، لا يجوز. لكنا بينا: أن هذا المعنى واقع. لأنه يخلق الدواعي إلى الكفر في حق الكافر، ثم يأمره بالإيمان. وما ذاك إلا تكليف ما لا يطاق. وأما الوجوه السمعية التي عولوا عليها: فهي قابلة للتأويل. وما ذكرناه من الدلائل لا يقبل التأويل. فكان الترجيح من جانبنا. قلنا: جوابهم الأول. وهو قولهم: (خطأ قول من يقول: إن وقوع ما علم الله أنه لا يقع: يدل على انقلاب علم الله جهلا، وخطأ قول من يقول: إنه لا يدل، : فضعيف. وذلك لأنهم إن أرادوا أن كلا النقيضين باطل في نفس الأمر. فهذا لا يقوله عاقل. وإن أرادوا به أن أحدهما حق، ولكن لا ينطقون به [ولا يتلفظون به] فهذا مسلم. إلا أن الزامنا غير مبني على نطقهم، ولا على لفظهم وعبارتهم. فإنا لما بينا أنه تعالى إذا علم أنه لا يؤمن فلو آمن ذلك الشخص. فإن على هذا التقدير، لم يكن علمه مطابقاً للمعلوم ولا معنى للجهل إلا هذا. فثبت منه: أنه يلزم انقلاب علم الله جهلا. ولما كان هذا محالاً، وجب أن يكون ذلك حالاً لأن المؤدي إلى المحال محال.
As for the remaining points: they are sophisms they mention concerning that commanding the impossible is not permissible. But we have shown that this meaning occurs. Because He creates motivations for disbelief in the disbeliever, then commands him to faith. That is nothing but a command of what cannot be borne. And as for the scriptural points on which they relied, they are open to interpretation. And what we have mentioned of proofs does not accept interpretation. So the preponderance is on our side. We say: Their first answer, which is their saying: 'The statement of one who says that the occurrence of what God knew would not occur indicates the conversion of God's knowledge into ignorance, and the statement of one who says it does not indicate is false.' So this is weak. That is because if they mean that both contradictories are false in themselves, then no sane person says this. And if they mean that one of them is true, but they do not utter it and do not pronounce it, then this is admitted. Except that our entailing is not based on their utterance nor on their expression. For when we have shown that when He, exalted, knows that he will not believe, if that person were to believe, then on this estimation, His knowledge would not correspond to the known. And there is no meaning of ignorance except this. So it is established from this: that it necessitates the conversion of God's knowledge into ignorance. And since this is impossible, it must be that that state is impossible, because what leads to the impossible is impossible.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This concluding passage addresses remaining objections and scriptural arguments. The author first dismisses the remaining 'points' as sophisms (shubahāt). He reiterates that commanding the impossible occurs because God creates motivations (dawāʿī) for disbelief in the disbeliever and then commands faith — this is taklīf mā lā yuṭāq. Scriptural evidence (wujūh samʿiyya) is open to interpretation (qābila li-l-taʾwīl), while his rational proofs are not. Then he refutes a specific counter-argument: the objectors claim that both saying 'the occurrence of an act God knew would not happen indicates conversion of knowledge into ignorance' and its negation are false. The author analyzes: if they mean both are false in themselves, that is absurd; if they mean one is true but they don't pronounce it, that is conceded. But his proof is not based on their utterance; it is based on the logical consequence: if God knows someone will not believe, and that person does believe, then God's knowledge does not correspond to reality, which is precisely ignorance (jahl). Since this is impossible, the hypothetical scenario is impossible. The conclusion: whatever leads to the impossible is itself impossible.

Key Terms
الشبهات
الأمر بالمحال
التكليف بما لا يطاق
التأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that scriptural proofs are always definitive and can override rational arguments in theological matters.
Correction: The author argues that scriptural proofs are open to interpretation, whereas rational proofs (like his) are not, so the rational argument takes precedence.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the scriptural points (wujūh samʿiyya) used by opponents?
A) They are decisive and clear
B) They are open to interpretation
C) They are irrational
D) They are stronger than rational proofs

Key Takeaway
The author dismisses remaining objections as sophisms and asserts that commanding the impossible is a reality, supported by rational proofs that are not open to interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Is there a command of Allah that you have avoided because you felt it was too difficult or impossible for you? Will you now submit to it, trusting that Allah’s decree and command may transcend your own reasoning?
?
The author claims his rational proofs are not open to interpretation, while scriptural proofs are. When you face a difficulty in understanding a divine command, do you instinctively resort to rationalizing it away or reinterpret it to suit your desires? This lesson challenges you to accept the reality of taklīf mā lā yuṭāq. Is there an area of your life where you have been using 'interpretation' to escape a difficult obligation?
?
The author dismisses remaining objections as sophisms. Have you ever dismissed a challenging truth by labeling it as sophistry or unreasonable? Reflect on a time you rejected a difficult teaching because it felt 'impossible' to follow. Refuting the Objection on Knowledge Conversion
Author refutes opponents' first answer regarding knowledge conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical refutation of the objection that claiming the occurrence of what God knew would not happen indicates the conversion of God's knowledge into ignorance.

Core Idea
If God's knowledge could fail to match reality, that would be ignorance — which is impossible.

The author now addresses a specific counter-argument from his opponents. They claim that both saying 'the occurrence of what God knew would not happen indicates the conversion of knowledge into ignorance' and its negation are false. The author analyzes this claim, showing that it is either absurd or conceded, and then explains why his proof does not depend on their utterance. This lesson unpacks that logical analysis and the conclusion that the scenario is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic from 'قلنا: جوابهم الأول' to the end, along with the translation. Notice the precise logical argument.
This is a dense refutation; read carefully.
2
Understand the opponents' claim
The opponents present a dilemma: they claim that both the statement that occurrence indicates conversion of knowledge and its negation are false. Identify the two alternatives they offer.
3
Analyze the author's response
The author breaks the claim into two interpretations. First, if they mean both contradictories are false in themselves, that is absurd. Second, if they mean one is true but they do not utter it, that is admitted. Then he explains why his proof does not depend on their utterance.
This is the guided breakdown.
4
Trace the logical consequence
The author restates his proof: if God knows someone will not believe, but that person does believe, then God's knowledge would not correspond to the known. That is the definition of ignorance (jahl). Since ignorance is impossible for God, the scenario is impossible. Follow this chain.
5
Conclude the argument
The final sentence: 'what leads to the impossible is impossible.' Reflect on why this closes the debate.
This is a classic kalam reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Since this is impossible, the hypothetical scenario is impossible. The conclusion: whatever leads to the impossible is itself impossible.

Commanding the Impossible is Real: Answering Remaining Doubts
Sanusi rebuts remaining objections and reaffirms commanding the impossible is real.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi argues that commanding the impossible is actual and how he dismisses scriptural objections as interpretable.

Core Idea
God's command to the disbeliever is a real imposition of the impossible.

Sanusi has already given rational proofs that commanding the impossible is permissible. Now he addresses the remaining objections from his opponents. He declares them mere doubts and reaffirms that commanding the impossible actually occurs—as seen in God creating motives for disbelief and then commanding faith. He then explains that any scriptural proofs against this are open to interpretation, while his rational proofs are not. This lesson unpacks his final argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
This passage is a rebuttal of the remaining objections. Recognize that Sanusi is concluding his argument.
The opponents' remaining arguments are presented as mere 'doubts' (shubuhat).
2
Identify the key claim
Sanusi asserts that commanding the impossible is actual (waqi'). Find the sentence where he states this.
3
Examine the example
He gives the example of God creating motives for disbelief in the disbeliever and then commanding him to believe. This is the concrete case of imposing what cannot be borne.
The phrase 'taklif ma la yutaq' is key.
4
Analyze the response to scriptural proofs
The opponents' scriptural proofs are said to be open to interpretation (ta'wil), while Sanusi's rational proofs are not. Note the contrast.
This shows the priority of reason over transmitted evidence in this context.
5
Conclude with preponderance
The final sentence states that the preponderance (tarjih) is on Sanusi's side because his proofs are unequivocal.
This is a decisive conclusion.

But we have shown that this meaning is actual—because He creates motives for disbelief in the case of the disbeliever, then commands him with faith. And that is nothing other than the imposition of what cannot be borne. As for the scriptural aspects on which they relied, they are open to interpretation. And what we have mentioned of proofs does not accept interpretation. So the preponderance is on our side.

Commentary
This passage is a response to further objections. Sanusi first states that the opponents' remaining arguments are mere doubts that commanding the impossible is impermissible. He then reiterates that this actually occurs: God creates inclinations toward disbelief in the disbeliever and then commands him to believe—this is indeed imposing what cannot be borne. Next, he addresses scriptural proofs the opponents use, saying they are open to interpretation, whereas the rational proofs he has presented are unequivocal. Therefore, rational argument carries more weight.

The Fifth Proof: God Creates Human Actions
Sanusi states a conditional syllogism proving God creates human actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi argues that God's foreknowledge of human actions implies that He must be their creator or the creator of their causes.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of actions before they happen proves He creates them.

Sanusi presents a compact proof that links divine omniscience to divine causation. The argument is structured as a conditional: if God were not the creator of actions or their causes, He could not know them beforehand. Since the opponents agree that God knows all future actions, it necessarily follows that He is the creator. This lesson walks through the proof step by step, clarifying the logical structure and the key premise that needs to be established.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: a conditional statement, an agreed fact, a conclusion.
The passage starts with 'The Fifth Proof' and ends with the beginning of the explanation of the second premise.
2
Identify the logical components
Find the two premises (if and but clauses) and the conclusion. Understand that the second premise (the conditional) is the one that needs proof.
The agreed premise is that God knows actions before they occur; the conclusion is that He must be their creator.
3
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself why Sanusi considers this proof convincing. What does it show about the relationship between God's knowledge and His creative power?
This proof connects divine omniscience to divine causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الخامس وبالله التوفيق لو لم يكن الله تعالى موجداً لأفعال العباد ، ولا موجداً لما يكون موجباً لها ، لامتنع كونه تعالى عالماً بها قبل وقوعها . إلا أن خصومنا يوافقونا على أنه عالم بها قبل وقوعها. فيلزم القطع بأنه تعالى موجداً لها ، أو موجداً لما يكون موجباً لها . وهو المطلوب أما بيان أنه تعالي عالم بأفعال العباد قبل وقوعها : فقد ذكرنا أنه متفق عليه بيننا وبين خصومنا . إنما الشأن في تقرير المقدمة الثانية . وهو قولنا : لو لم يكن موجداً لها ، ولا موجداً لما يكون موجباً لها ، لامتنع كونه تعالى عالماً بها قبل وقوعها .
The Fifth Proof. And with God is success. If God, exalted, were not the creator of the actions of servants, nor the creator of what would be a cause for them, then it would be impossible for Him, exalted, to be knowing of them before their occurrence. But our opponents agree with us that He is knowing of them before their occurrence. So necessarily it follows that He, exalted, is the creator of them or the creator of what would be a cause for them. And that is what is sought. As for the demonstration that He, exalted, is knowing of the actions of servants before their occurrence, we have mentioned that it is agreed upon between us and our opponents. The only matter is in establishing the second premise, which is our statement: If He were not the creator of them nor the creator of what would be a cause for them, then it would be impossible for Him, exalted, to be knowing of them before their occurrence.

Commentary
This passage is a proof (برهان) for God's creation of human actions. Sanusi states the proof: If God were not the creator of actions or their causes, He could not know them before they happen. Since the opponents agree that God knows actions before they occur, it follows that He must be the creator (either directly or through causes). Then Sanusi notes that the premise that God knows future actions is agreed upon. The challenge is to establish the second premise: that without being a creator, such knowledge is impossible. The text in this window begins the explanation of that second premise but is cut off. The proof is structured as a conditional leading to a necessary conclusion.

Refuting the Second Objection: Conditional Propositions and Divine Knowledge
Refutes the second objection against the proof that God creates human actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the second objection against the proof of divine creation of actions fails due to the necessary implications of conditional propositions regarding God's eternal knowledge.

Core Idea
Conditional propositions force us into one of two actual knowledges, making change impossible.

The opponent's second objection attempts to evade the proof by framing the scenario as a mere conditional statement. However, this objection overlooks that the conditional itself logically requires that one of two specific divine knowledges be actual. Once we recognize that God eternally knows either faith or disbelief, the impossibility of change becomes clear. Sharif al-Jurjani exposes the weakness of this objection by analyzing its own logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to follow the logical argument. Pay attention to how the author structures the refutation.
The passage is dense; take your time to understand each link in the chain.
2
Identify the conditional proposition
Notice that the opponent's argument is framed as an 'if...then' statement. The author points out that this is a conditional proposition (qadiyya shartiyya).
Conditional propositions are the basis of the logical analysis.
3
Trace the necessary implication
Understand why the conditional necessarily implies that one of two divine knowledges (faith or disbelief) must be actual. This is the key move in the refutation.
The author argues that the conditional itself forces this conclusion.
4
Examine the consequences of change
See why the opponent's objection would lead to the impossible scenario of God's knowledge turning into ignorance or changing in eternity.
This shows the absurdity of the objection.
5
Conclude the refutation
The author concludes that the objection is extremely weak and the original proof stands unrefuted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما جوابهم الثاني : فضعيف أيضاً : لأن قولنا : لو كان الداخل في الوجود هو إيمان « زيد ، لعلم الله في الأزل أنه يؤمن . ولو كان الداخل عـدم إيمانه ، لعلم الله في الأزل أنه لا يؤمن . فهذا قضية شرطية . لأن قولنا : لو كان كذا ، لكان كذا : لا شك أنه قضية شرطية. لكنا نقول : إنه لا بد مع حصول هذه القضية الشرطية ، أنه تعالى [ كان (١) ] عالماً في الأزل بأنه يؤمن ، أو كان عالماً بأنه (۲) ] لا يؤمن فثبت : أن أحد هذين العلمين حاصل . وإذا كان ذلك حاصلاً ، امتنع أن يقع نقيض ذلك المعلوم ، وإلا لزم أن يصير علمه جهلا وهذا محال . وأيضاً : إن ذلك العلم ، لما كان علماً في الأزل ثم قدرنا أن المعلوم لم يقع في الأزل ، فحينئذ ينقلب ذلك العلم جهلا في الأزل . وهذا مفضي إلى وقوع التبديل فيها حصل في الزمان الماضي ، وكل ذلك محال . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن هذه الأجوبة في غاية الضعف ، وأن البرهان الذي عولنا عليه ، مما لا يمكن دفعه بوجه من الوجوه
As for their second answer: it is also weak. Because our saying: 'If what enters into existence is Zayd's faith, then God knew in eternity that he would believe. And if what enters is his disbelief, then God knew in eternity that he would not believe.' This is a conditional proposition. Because our saying: 'If it were such, then it would be such' is undoubtedly a conditional proposition. But we say: It is necessary with the occurrence of this conditional proposition that He, exalted, was knowing in eternity that he would believe, or was knowing that he would not believe. So it is established that one of these two knowledges is actual. And when that is actual, it is impossible for the opposite of that known thing to occur, otherwise His knowledge would become ignorance, and that is impossible. Also: that knowledge, since it was knowledge in eternity, then if we suppose that the known did not occur in eternity, then that knowledge would turn into ignorance in eternity. This leads to the occurrence of change in what occurred in past time, and all of that is impossible. So it is established by what we have mentioned that these answers are extremely weak, and that the proof upon which we relied cannot be repudiated in any way.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the second objection raised by opponents against a previous proof (likely concerning divine knowledge of human actions). The author begins by stating that the objection is weak. He then analyzes the logical form of the opponent's argument: it is a conditional proposition (qadiyya shartiyya) of the form 'if ... then ...'. The author argues that given this conditional, one of two divine knowledges must be actual: either God knows eternally that Zayd will believe, or that he will not. Once one of these knowledges is established, it is impossible for the opposite to occur, because that would turn God's knowledge into ignorance (jahl), which is impossible. Furthermore, if eternal knowledge were to later become false, that would mean the knowledge changed from truth to falsehood in eternity itself, implying a change in the past—also impossible. Therefore, the objection is baseless and the original proof stands unrefuted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: The objection treats the conditional proposition as having no actual implication for divine knowledge, as if it were a mere hypothetical.
Correction: The conditional necessarily implies that one of the two possible knowledges is actual; otherwise, the conditional would be meaningless.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The conditional 'if...then' necessarily implies actual eternal knowledge, making any change in that knowledge impossible.

If God’s knowledge cannot change, then every moment of your life was already known in eternity. How does this make you reconsider your attitude toward divine decree? Are you willing to submit to His knowledge even when your desires conflict with it?

Fifth Proof: God Must Create Actions for Foreknowledge
Al-Sanusi presents the fifth proof and its objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the fifth proof from divine foreknowledge establishing that God must be the creator of human actions or their causes.

Core Idea
Divine foreknowledge requires eternal determination; otherwise it would be ignorance.

This proof builds on an agreed premise: God knows all actions before they happen. The author argues that if God were not the creator of actions or their necessitating causes, His knowledge would be mere guesswork. The passage walks through the logical necessity of divine causation for true foreknowledge, and then considers an objection.

Guided Path
1
Read and identify the main claim
Read the proof carefully and note its starting conditional and conclusion. The proof argues that God must be the creator of actions or their causes because He knows them beforehand.
Pay attention to the role of the first premise (agreed foreknowledge).
2
Follow the logical steps for the second premise
The author establishes why, without divine creation, actions would be in equilibrium and any belief in their future existence would be ignorance. Trace the argument from the essences of actions being equally receptive to the need for a determinant.
Understand the notion of 'i'tiqad ghayr mutabiq' (non-corresponding belief).
3
Understand the objection and its rebuttal
Read the objection: God knows eternally because the determinant (servant's power) exists at the future time. Then see the beginning of the answer, which points out a misunderstanding.
The answer is incomplete in this passage; think about what might complete it.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this proof ties divine foreknowledge to divine power and causality. Why does the author insist on an eternal determinant?
This connects to broader issues of qadar (predestination) and divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الخامس
وبالله التوفيق
لو لم يكن الله تعالى موجداً لأفعال العباد ، ولا موجداً لما يكون موجباً لها ، لامتنع كونه تعالى عالماً بها قبل وقوعها . إلا أن خصومنا يوافقونا على أنه عالم بها قبل وقوعها. فيلزم القطع بأنه تعالى موجداً لها ، أو موجداً لما يكون موجباً لها . وهو المطلوب
أما بيان أنه تعالي عالم بأفعال العباد قبل وقوعها : فقد ذكرنا أنه متفق عليه بيننا وبين خصومنا . إنما الشأن في تقرير المقدمة الثانية . وهو قولنا : لو لم يكن موجداً لها ، ولا موجداً لما يكون موجباً لها ، لامتنع كونه تعالى عالماً بها قبل وقوعها .
والذي يدل على صحة هذه المقدمة : إن ماهيات أفعال العباد قابلة للمعدوم ، وقابلة للموجود ، قبولاً على التساوي ، من غير رجحان أصلاً البتة (١) . فلو فرضنا أنه لا يرجح أطراف وجود هذه الأفعال على طرف عدمها ، إلا قدرة العبد . ثم إن قدرة العبد غير موجبة لذلك، بل هي صالحة للفعل والترك ) ولم يكن (۲) أيضاً هذا الرجحان . لا ابتداء ولا بواسطة . لان التقدير أنه لم يوجد في الأزل ما يوجب تلك الأفعال . لا ابتداء ولا بواسطة . وإلا فالجبر لازم . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، كانت أفعال العباد في أنفها قابلة للعدم ، وقابلة للوجود على السوية . ولم يكن هناك البتة ما يقتضي رجحان وجودها على عدمها . فلو اعتقد معتقد فيها : كونها راجحة الوجود على العدم كان ذلك الاعتقاد : اعتقاداً غير مطابق . فكان جهلاً . والجهل على الله محال .
فثبت : أنه تعالى لو لم تتعلق قدرته في الأزل بإيقاع تلك الأفعال في لا يزال ، ولم يتعلق أيضاً بإيقاع ما يوجب وقوع تلك الأفعال في لا يزال ، كان اعتقاد أنها ستقع في لا يزال ، اعتقاداً غير مطابق للمعتقد ، وإنه جهل . وهو على الله محال . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، وجب أن يقال : إن قدرته تعالى في الأزل ، تعلقت بإيقاع هذه الأفعال في لا يزال ، أو بإيقاع ما يوجب هذه الأفعال في لا يزال ، حتى يكون علمه بأنها ستقع في لا يزال : علماً ، ولا يكون جهلاً
فإنه قيل : إنه تعالى يعلم في الأزل أن فعل العبد سيترجح وجوده على عدمه، في الوقت الفلاني ، وفي ذلك الوقت يكون المقتضى لترجع وجوده على عد

علمه حاصلا . فأما لما فرضنا أن المقتضى لذلك الترجح ، هو قدرة العبد
وفرضنا أن قدرة العبد حاصلة في ذلك الوقت . فعلى هذا التقدير ، كان المقتضى لترجحه حاصلاً في ذلك الوقت .
والجواب : إن هذا سوء فهم ، لما ذكرناه . وذلك لأنه تعالى يعلم في الأزل أن وجود الفلاني سيترجح على عدمه في الوقت الفلاني في لا يزال
The Fifth Proof. By God, success. If God, exalted, were not the creator of the actions of servants, nor the creator of what necessitates them, then it would be impossible for Him to be knowing them before their occurrence. However, our opponents agree with us that He is knowing them before their occurrence. So necessarily it follows that He, exalted, is their creator, or the creator of what necessitates them. That is the desired. As for the demonstration that He, exalted, is knowing the actions of servants before their occurrence: we have mentioned that it is agreed upon between us and our opponents. The real matter is in establishing the second premise, which is our saying: If He were not their creator, nor the creator of what necessitates them, it would be impossible for Him to be knowing them before their occurrence. What indicates the correctness of this premise: The essences of the actions of servants are receptive to non-existence and receptive to existence, receptivity equally, with absolutely no preference at all. So if we suppose that nothing gives preference to the side of existence of these actions over the side of their non-existence except the power of the servant, and then the power of the servant is not necessitating thereof, but rather it is capable of both action and inaction, and also this preference did not exist, neither directly nor mediatedly—because the estimation is that nothing existed in eternity that necessitates those actions, neither directly nor mediatedly, otherwise compulsion would be necessary—and if the matter is like that, then the actions of servants in themselves are equally receptive to non-existence and receptive to existence. And there is absolutely nothing that requires the preference of their existence over their non-existence. So if someone believed in them that they are preferable in existence over non-existence, that belief would be a non-corresponding belief, and thus would be ignorance. And ignorance is impossible for God. So it is established: If His power in eternity was not related to bringing about those actions in the temporal future, nor related to bringing about what necessitates the occurrence of those actions in the temporal future, then the belief that they will occur in the temporal future would be a belief non-corresponding to the believed, and it would be ignorance, and it is impossible for God. And if the matter is like that, it must be said: His power, exalted, in eternity is related to bringing about these actions in the temporal future, or to bringing about what necessitates these actions in the temporal future, so that His knowledge that they will occur in the temporal future is knowledge and not ignorance. For it is said: He, exalted, knows in eternity that the action of the servant will have its existence preponderate over its non-existence at a certain time, and at that time the determinant for the preponderance of its existence over non-existence is present. As for our assumption that the determinant for that preponderance is the power of the servant, and we assumed that the power of the servant is present at that time; on this estimation, the determinant for its preponderance is present at that time. The answer: This is a misunderstanding of what we mentioned. That is because He, exalted, knows in eternity that the existence of something will preponderate over its non-existence at a certain time in the temporal future.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof (al-burhan al-khamis) that God must be the creator of human actions or their causes in order for His foreknowledge to be true. The proof begins with a conditional: if God were not the creator of actions nor of their necessitating causes, He could not know them beforehand. Since opponents agree God does know actions beforehand, He must be their creator or the creator of their causes. The author then establishes the second premise: the essences (mahiyyat) of actions are equally receptive to existence and non-existence with no inherent preference. If only the servant's power (qudra) were the determinant, and that power is not necessitating (it can do or not do), and there is no other determinant from God (directly or indirectly—otherwise compulsion would result), then actions would remain in perfect equilibrium. Any belief in their future existence would be a non-corresponding belief (i'tiqad ghayr mutabiq), i.e., ignorance (jahl), which is impossible for God. Therefore, God's eternal power must be related to causing these actions or their causes in the future, so His knowledge is true. An objection is then raised: God knows eternally that the action will be preponderant at a certain time because at that time the determinant (the servant's power) exists. The author answers that this is a misunderstanding; God knows eternally that the existence will preponderate at that future time. The answer cuts off, but the point is that the determinant must be traceable back to God in eternity to avoid ignorance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that God can know future actions based solely on the future existence of the servant's power, without any eternal determination.
Correction: The proof shows that without an eternal determinant (God's power creating or causing the action), God's foreknowledge would be a non-corresponding belief, i.e., ignorance. Therefore, the determinant must be eternal.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the fifth proof?
A) God does not know human actions before they occur.
B) God must be the creator of human actions or their causes to have true foreknowledge.
C) Human actions are random and unknown to God.
D) God only knows actions after they happen.

Key Takeaway
God's foreknowledge necessitates that He be the Creator of all actions or their causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Every action you perform today—your speech, your choices, even this reading—exists only because Allah's eternal power is directly or indirectly creating it. Does this truth make you feel helpless, or does it awaken in you a deeper reliance on Him? What is the first thing tomorrow morning that you can consciously attribute to His creation?
?
The proof shows that without Allah's creative will, your actions would forever hang in perfect equilibrium, unable to tip toward existence. Think back on a good deed you performed. Could it have happened if Allah had not specifically willed it? How does this affect your gratitude—do you still thank yourself, or do you now see His hand in every moment of obedience?
?
You believe Allah knows your future choices. But the proof demands that His knowledge is not passive—it is grounded in His creative activity. How does this reframe your understanding of His omniscience? Are you willing to admit that even your moment of pride in your own free will is only possible by His creation? What would it mean for you to live each day fully aware that your very existence and actions are held in being by His power every instant?

Divine Decree
intermediate

The Necessity of God's Pre-Eternal Will (Part 1)
Proof of God's pre-eternal will for human actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's power must be pre-eternally attached to human actions or their causes to avoid ignorance.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of future acts requires a pre-eternal cause for their existence.

In this passage, the author sets out a foundational argument for divine determinism. He starts with the premise that human acts are equally possible in themselves, and then shows that without God's pre-eternal will, any belief in their future occurrence would be ignorance. This establishes the necessity of God's creative power for all actions.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the premise of equal receptivity
The author begins by stating that the essences of human actions are equally receptive to existence and non-existence. Understand that this means there is no inherent preference for either state.
2
Consider the hypothetical scenario
Suppose only the slave's power could give preference, but that power is not necessitating. The author argues that then there would be no preference at all, neither initially nor through a mediator.
3
See why ignorance results
If there were no cause for preference, any belief that an act will exist would not correspond to reality, making it ignorance. Ignorance is impossible for God.
4
Conclude the need for divine attachment
Therefore, God's power must be pre-eternally attached either to the act itself or to something that necessitates it, ensuring His knowledge is true knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي يدل على صحة هذه المقدمة : إن ماهيات أفعال العباد قابلة للمعدوم ، وقابلة للموجود ، قبولاً على التساوي ، من غير رجحان أصلاً البتة . فلو فرضنا أنه لا يرجح أطراف وجود هذه الأفعال على طرف عدمها، إلا قدرة العبد . ثم إن قدرة العبد غير موجبة لذلك، بل هي صالحة للفعل والترك ولم يكن أيضاً هذا الرجحان . لا ابتداء ولا بواسطة . لان التقدير أنه لم يوجد في الأزل ما يوجب تلك الأفعال . لا ابتداء ولا بواسطة . وإلا فالجبر لازم . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، كانت أفعال العباد في أنفها قابلة للعدم، وقابلة للوجود على السوية . ولم يكن هناك البتة ما يقتضي رجحان وجودها على عدمها . فلو اعتقد معتقد فيها : كونها راجحة الوجود على العدم كان ذلك الاعتقاد : اعتقاداً غير مطابق . فكان جهلاً . والجهل على الله محال .
فثبت : أنه تعالى لو لم تتعلق قدرته في الأزل بإيقاع تلك الأفعال في لا يزال، ولم يتعلق أيضاً بإيقاع ما يوجب وقوع تلك الأفعال في لا يزال، كان اعتقاد أنها ستقع في لا يزال، اعتقاداً غير مطابق للمعتقد، وإنه جهل . وهو على الله محال . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، وجب أن يقال : إن قدرته تعالى في الأزل، تعلقت بإيقاع هذه الأفعال في لا يزال، أو بإيقاع ما يوجب هذه الأفعال في لا يزال، حتى يكون علمه بأنها ستقع في لا يزال : علماً، ولا يكون جهلاً
فإنه قيل : إنه تعالى يعلم في الأزل أن فعل العبد سيترجح وجوده على عدمه، في الوقت الفلاني، وفي ذلك الوقت يكون المقتضى لترجع وجوده على علمه حاصلا . فأما لما فرضنا أن المقتضى لذلك الترجح، هو قدرة العبد وفرضنا أن قدرة العبد حاصلة في ذلك الوقت . فعلى هذا التقدير، كان المقتضى لترجحه حاصلاً في ذلك الوقت .
والجواب : إن هذا سوء فهم، لما ذكرناه . وذلك لأنه تعالى يعلم في الأزل أن وجود الفلاني سيترجح على عدمه في الوقت الفلاني في لا يزال ووقوع الترجيح، وإن كان لا يحصل إلا في ذلك الوقت، إلا أن المفهوم من قولنا : سيترجح: كان حاصلا في الأزل . فهذا المفهوم لما كان حاصلا في الأزل وجب أن يحصل له ما يقتضي حصوله . لكن المقتضي لحصوله، ليس هو ذاته، وإلا لكان واجب الحصول لذاته. ولا قدرة العبد، ولا إرادته، لأن كل ذلك معدوم . فوجب أن يكون المقتضي له : هو قدرة الله وإرادته . وذلك يقتضي أن يكون وقوع أفعال العباد بقدرة الله وإرادته . فإن لم تكن قدرة الله وإرادته، يقتضيان ما هو المفهوم من قولنا : إن الفعل الفلاني سيترجح في الوقت الفلاني، وليس له نقيض آخر . وجب أن لا يحصل هذا المفهوم . وإذا لم يحصل هذا المفهوم، كان اعتقاد حصوله جهلا . فثبت : أن على هذا التقدير أنه تعالى لو لم يكن مـوجـداً لافعال العباد ولا لما يكون موجباً لأفعال العباد، لامتنع كونه تعالى عالماً بوقوعها في الأزل . ولما كان هذا باطلاً، ثبت أن الحق ما ذكرناه، واعلم : أن هذه النكتة إنما استنبطناها من مسألة حكمية . وهي : أن الجزم بوقوع الممكن، لا يمكن إلا بواسطة العلم بعلته وموجبه
وبالله التوفيق
And what indicates the soundness of this premise: that the essences of the acts of slaves are receptive to non-existence and receptive to existence, in equal receptivity, without any preference at all. For if we suppose that there is nothing that gives preference to the sides of existence of these acts over the side of their non-existence except the slave's power, and then the slave's power is not necessitating for that, rather it is suitable for action and refraining, and also there was no such preference, neither initially nor through a mediator, because it is supposed that there did not exist in pre-eternity what necessitates those acts, neither initially nor through a mediator, otherwise compulsion (jabr) would be necessary. And if the matter is thus, then the acts of slaves in their essences are receptive to non-existence and receptive to existence equally, and there is never anything that requires the preference of their existence over their non-existence. So if someone believes concerning them that they are preponderant in existence over non-existence, that belief would be a non-corresponding belief, thus it would be ignorance, and ignorance is impossible for God. So it is established: that if His power in pre-eternity were not attached to bringing about those acts in the future, and also not attached to bringing about what necessitates the occurrence of those acts in the future, then the belief that they will occur in the future would be a belief that does not correspond to the believed, and it would be ignorance, and that is impossible for God. And if the matter is thus, it must be said that His power in pre-eternity is attached to bringing about these acts in the future, or to bringing about what necessitates these acts in the future, so that His knowledge that they will occur in the future is knowledge, not ignorance. Then it is said: He knows in pre-eternity that the act of the slave, its existence will preponderate over its non-existence at a certain time, and at that time the cause (muqtada) for the preponderance of its existence over non-existence is present. So as for when we suppose that the cause for that preponderance is the slave's power, and we suppose that the slave's power is present at that time, then on this supposition, the cause for its preponderance is present at that time. And the answer: This is a misunderstanding of what we have mentioned. That is because He knows in pre-eternity that the existence of a certain thing will preponderate over its non-existence at a certain time in the future, and the occurrence of the preponderance, even if it does not happen except at that time, yet the concept understood from our saying 'it will preponderate' was present in pre-eternity. So since this concept was present in pre-eternity, there must occur for it what necessitates its occurrence. But the necessitator of its occurrence is not its essence, otherwise it would be necessarily existent by itself; nor the slave's power nor his will, because all of that is non-existent. So it must be that the necessitator of it is God's power and will. And that requires that the occurrence of the acts of slaves be by God's power and will. For if God's power and will do not necessitate what is the concept from our saying 'that certain act will preponderate at a certain time' and it has no other contrapositive, then this concept must not occur. And if this concept does not occur, then the belief in its occurrence would be ignorance. So it is established that on this supposition, if He were not the bringer into existence of the acts of slaves, nor of what necessitates the acts of slaves, His being knowledgeable of their occurrence in pre-eternity would be impossible. And since this is false, it is established that the truth is what we have mentioned. And know that this insight is deduced only from a philosophical issue, namely: that certainty of the occurrence of a possible thing is not possible except through knowledge of its cause and necessitator. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is an extended argument for the necessity of God's pre-eternal power and will in the creation of human actions, structured as a proof followed by an objection and its refutation. It begins with a premise: the essences (mahiyyat) of human acts are equally receptive to existence and non-existence, with no inherent preference. The author then hypothesizes that if only the slave's power (qudrat al-'abd) were the source of preference, and that power is not necessitating but merely capable of both action and refraining, then there would be no preference at all—neither initially nor through any cause. Consequently, the acts would remain equally possible. If someone then believed that such an act will exist, that belief would not correspond to reality, making it ignorance (jahl), which is impossible for God. Therefore, God's power must be attached in pre-eternity either to bringing about the act itself or to bringing about something that necessitates the act in the future, so that His knowledge of its future occurrence is true knowledge. An objector then raises a point: God knows pre-eternally that the slave's act will become preponderant at a specific time when the slave's power exists, so the cause of preponderance is the slave's power at that time. The author answers that this is a misunderstanding. The very meaning (mafhum) of 'it will preponderate' is a pre-eternal concept (hāṣil fī al-azal). Since this concept exists pre-eternally, there must be a pre-eternal cause that necessitates it. That cause cannot be the act's essence (otherwise it would be necessary), nor the slave's power or will (both non-existent at the time of the pre-eternal determination). Therefore, the cause must be God's power and will, implying that human acts occur solely by God's power and will. The author concludes by noting that this argument derives from a philosophical principle: certainty (jizm) about the occurrence of a possible thing requires knowledge of its cause and necessitator.

ماهيات
قدرة العبد
جهل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that human actions can occur without any pre-eternal determination, simply by the slave's power at the time.
Correction: The author shows that such a belief would be ignorance for God, because without a pre-eternal cause, the future occurrence has no basis.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if there is no pre-eternal cause for a human action's existence?
A) The action would still occur by the slave's power.
B) God's belief in its occurrence would be ignorance.
C) The action would become necessary.
D) The slave would have no free will.

Key Takeaway
Without God's pre-eternal will, His knowledge of future human actions would be ignorance, which is impossible, so He must be the creator of all actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that every action you take exists only because God willed it in pre-eternity, how does that change the way you approach your daily choices? Are you living as if your actions are independent of His will?
?
The argument shows that God's knowledge is inseparable from His will. Does your heart rest in the certainty that nothing escapes His knowledge and will, or do you sometimes doubt His foreknowledge?
?
If you knew that your belief in God's knowledge of your future acts was only true because He willed those acts, would you be more conscious of His presence in your every moment? How can you remind yourself of this reality today?

Divine Decree
intermediate

The Objection and Its Refutation (Part 2)
Objection and refutation regarding the cause of human actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the slave's power at the time of the act is sufficient for its occurrence, and the author's refutation that the pre-eternal concept requires a pre-eternal cause.

Core Idea
The pre-eternal meaning of 'will occur' demands a pre-eternal cause, not a temporal one.

Having argued that God's power must be attached pre-eternally, the author now addresses a potential objection: that God knows the act will occur when the slave's power exists at that time. The answer delves into the nature of pre-eternal concepts and causation, affirming that only God's power and will can be the pre-eternal necessitator.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector says God knows pre-eternally that the act will preponderate at a certain time when the slave's power exists, so the cause is the slave's power at that time.
2
Identify the pre-eternal concept
The author points out that the meaning (mafhum) of 'will preponderate' is a pre-eternal concept, existing in the mind of God from eternity.
3
Reason for a pre-eternal cause
Since this concept exists pre-eternally, there must be a pre-eternal cause that necessitates it. That cause cannot be the act's essence (otherwise necessary), nor the slave's power (non-existent at that time).
4
Conclude that God must be the cause
Thus, the cause must be God's power and will, meaning all actions occur by His power. If not, God's knowledge would be ignorance, which is false.

Commentary
This passage is an extended argument for the necessity of God's pre-eternal power and will in the creation of human actions, structured as a proof followed by an objection and its refutation. The author then hypothesizes that if only the slave's power (qudrat al-'abd) were the source of preference, and that power is not necessitating but merely capable of both action and refraining, then there would be no preference at all—neither initially nor through any cause. Consequently, the acts would remain equally possible. If someone then believed that such an act will exist, that belief would not correspond to reality, making it ignorance (jahl), which is impossible for God. Therefore, God's power must be attached in pre-eternity either to bringing about the act itself or to bringing about something that necessitates the act in the future, so that His knowledge of its future occurrence is true knowledge. An objector then raises a point: God knows pre-eternally that the slave's act will become preponderant at a specific time when the slave's power exists, so the cause of preponderance is the slave's power at that time. The author answers that this is a misunderstanding. The very meaning (mafhum) of 'it will preponderate' is a pre-eternal concept (hāṣil fī al-azal). Since this concept exists pre-eternally, there must be a pre-eternal cause that necessitates it. That cause cannot be the act's essence (otherwise it would be necessary), nor the slave's power or will (both non-existent at the time of the pre-eternal determination). Therefore, the cause must be God's power and will, implying that human acts occur solely by God's power and will. The author concludes by noting that this argument derives from a philosophical principle: certainty (jizm) about the occurrence of a possible thing requires knowledge of its cause and necessitator.

Key Terms
مفهوم
مقتض
علة
موجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the slave's power at the time of the act is sufficient to cause it, without needing a pre-eternal cause.
Correction: The author shows that the pre-eternal concept of the future occurrence requires a pre-eternal cause, which cannot be the slave's power (non-existent then), so it must be God's power and will.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why can't the slave's power be the cause of the pre-eternal concept that an act will occur?
A) Because the slave's power is not necessitating.
B) Because the slave's power is non-existent in pre-eternity.
C) Because the act's essence is necessary.
D) Because the objection is based on a philosophical principle.

Key Takeaway
The pre-eternal concept of a future action necessitates a pre-eternal cause, which can only be God's power and will, affirming that all actions are from God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every action you commit is ultimately from God, how does that change your view of your own sins and your need for repentance? Will you still take full responsibility for your actions, or does this realization humble you into seeking His mercy more earnestly?
?
Are you willing to accept that your choices are not independent of God's will? How does that affect the way you plan your life and rely on God? Let this truth strip away any illusion of self-sufficiency and drive you to depend on Him entirely.
?
Do you truly believe that God's knowledge of your actions includes the very cause of them, or do you still harbor a sense of pride in your own abilities? Let this lesson expose any hidden arrogance and turn your heart back to Allah as the sole originator of all that occurs.

Proof of Predestination from Truth Determinacy
The author proves that all future events are fixed and predestined using the determinacy of truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that all future events are fixed and predestined based on the impossibility of changing the truth value of past statements.

Core Idea
Future events are fixed because changing them would require changing the past.

The author now presents a sophisticated argument linking the nature of truth to divine predestination. He argues that if a statement about a future event is true, then its non-occurrence would retroactively falsify it, which is impossible. This proves that future events are necessarily determined.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Claim
The author first asserts that, based on the established principle, all future events are predestined without any possibility of increase, decrease, or change. This is the conclusion he aims to prove.
2
Follow the Proof by Contradiction
The proof is a reductio ad absurdum. Assume the event does not occur at its predicted time. Then the original statement 'it will occur' becomes false from the very moment it was uttered, implying a change in the past truth value, which is impossible.
Pay attention to the logic: the non-occurrence would require a past statement to change from true to false.
3
Grasp the Conclusion
Since non-occurrence leads to an impossibility, it is itself impossible. Therefore, occurrence is necessary. This applies to all future events, establishing predestination.
The argument does not rely on divine foreknowledge but purely on the nature of truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : إن هذا يقتضي أن تكون جميع الحوادث المستقبلة : مقدرة على وجه ، يمتنع تطرق الزيادة والنقصان والتغيير إليها . على ما هو مذهبنا في مسألة القضاء والقدر . والدليل عليه : أن الجانب الذي صدق عليه أنه سيقع في الوقت الفلاني . إما أن يكون أن لا يقع في ذلك الوقت ، أو لا عكس . والأول باطل . لأن كل ما كان ممكناً فإنه لا يلزم من فرض وقوعه محال . فليفرض غير واقع . وإذا لم يقع ، صار قولنا : إنه سيقع : كذباً من أول وقت دخول هذا الخبر في الوجود . وكنا قد فرضنا : أنه كان موصوفاً بالصدقية . فإذا لم يحصل ذلك الفعل ، في ذلك الوقت ، لزم من عدم وقوعه في ذلك الوقت ، ارتفاع الصدقية من ذلك الخبر ، من أول دخوله في الوجود . فيلزم أن يكون عدم وقوع ذلك الفعل في ذلك الوقت ، موجباً زوال صفة الصدقية عنه في الزمان الماضي ، فيفضي هذا إلى إيقاع التصرف في الزمان الماضي . وإنه محال . فثبت : أن عدم وقوع ذلك الفعل في ذلك الوقت ، يلزم منه محال . والممكن هو الذي لا يلزم من فرض وقوعه محال . فثبت : أن عدم وقوعه في ذلك الوقت محال . فكان وقوعه فيه واجباً وهو المطلوب .
When this is established, we say: This requires that all future events be predestined in a manner such that increase, decrease, and change are impossible regarding them, according to our doctrine in the issue of predestination (al-qaḍā' wa al-qadar). The proof for it: The side which is truly described as going to occur at a certain time either is such that it will not occur at that time, or there is no opposite. The first is false. Because everything that is possible does not entail an impossibility from supposing its occurrence. So let it be supposed non-occurring. And if it does not occur, our statement 'it will occur' becomes false from the first moment this statement comes into existence. And we had supposed it was described by truthness. So if that act does not happen at that time, then from its non-occurrence at that time follows the removal of truthness from that statement from the first moment of its existence. This leads to effecting change in past time, which is impossible. So it is established that non-occurrence of that act at that time entails an impossibility. And the possible is that which does not entail an impossibility from supposing its occurrence. So it is established that its non-occurrence at that time is impossible. Therefore its occurrence at that time is necessary, which is what was sought.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage presents a proof linking the determinacy of truth to the doctrine of predestination (al-qaḍā' wa al-qadar). The author argues that if a future proposition is truly described as true (i.e., it will occur), then its non-occurrence would lead to a contradiction. Specifically, suppose a future event is predicted to occur at a certain time, and the proposition about it is true from the moment it is uttered. If the event did not occur, then the proposition would become false retroactively from the very beginning of its existence. This would mean changing the past (changing the truth value of a past statement), which is impossible. Therefore, non-occurrence is impossible, and occurrence is necessary. Since this reasoning applies to all future events, they are all fixed and predetermined, with no possibility of change. This establishes the Ash‘arite doctrine of predestination.

Key Terms
حوادث مستقبلة (ḥawādith mustaqbala)
قضاء (qaḍā')
قدر (qadar)
ضرورة (ḍarūra)
امتناع (imtinā‘)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that true statements about the future are merely descriptive and do not necessitate the event; the event could still be contingent.
Correction: The author shows that if a future statement is true, its falsity would require a change in the past, which is impossible. Hence the event must occur necessarily.

Checkpoint
According to the author's proof, if a future event does not occur as predicted, what would happen to the truth value of the past prediction?
A) It would remain true because it was based on knowledge.
B) It would become false from the moment the event failed to occur.
C) It would become false retroactively from the first moment the prediction was made.
D) It would become unknown.

Key Takeaway
The determinacy of truth entails the necessity of future events, establishing the Ash‘arite doctrine of predestination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every future event is already fixed, including your next decision, what actions today are truly meaningful? How does the inevitability of what will happen affect your sense of responsibility for your choices?
?
Consider a moment in your life that you deeply regret. According to this proof, that event was necessary. Can you accept that it could not have been otherwise? What does that acceptance demand of you now?
?
The proof shows that changing the past is impossible. Are you living as if you can change the past by dwelling on it? What would it mean for you to stop trying to revise what is necessarily fixed?
