And it is established that existence is a concept included in everything, and it is established that the powerful one over a thing is powerful over its like: so it follows that the one powerful over bringing into existence some possible things is powerful over bringing into existence all of them.

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument (al-wajh al-thalith) which relies on the Mu'tazilite school's position. The argument is concise: The Mu'tazilite shaykhs hold that the non-existent (ma'dum) is a thing (shay'), and that the powerful agent's only effect is giving existence. Since existence is a single concept that applies to all things uniformly, and since the one able to do a thing is able to do its like, it follows that if the servant could create some possible things, he would be able to create all possible things. The conclusion reinforces the same point as the previous argument: the ability to create any possible entails the ability to create all possibles, which is then refuted by subsequent arguments.

Three Proofs that the Servant Cannot Create Any Possible Thing
Three enumerative proofs with a concluding synthesis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical reasoning behind the three proofs that a human servant cannot possess any creative power over possible things.

Core Idea
If we could create anything, we could create everything—but we can't.

This dense passage from Imam al-Razi lays out three interconnected arguments that dismantle the claim that humans can create any possible thing. The arguments build on each other to show that the very idea of partial creative power is contradictory. Let's trace each proof carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the clear structure: three 'wujuh' (aspects) followed by a conclusion.
2
Identify each proof
The passage presents three distinct proofs. Label them as: 1) opponent concession, 2) inability to create bodies, 3) inability to create life and power in oneself.
3
Understand the logical glue
Realize that the first proof is a quick refutation, the second is a necessary truth, and the third shows absurd consequences. Together they force the conclusion that if the servant could create some, he could create all.
Pay attention to the phrase 'because ability to do a thing along with inability to do its like is impossible.'
4
Connect to the conclusion
The final sentence fuses everything: 'So it is established... that he is not able to bring into existence any of them.' See how the proof chain leads to total negation of creative power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوه : وأما بيان أنه يمتنع كون العبد قادراً على إيجاد كل الممكنات . فمن وجوه : الأول : إن الخصم مساعد عليه . والثاني : وهو إنا نعلم بالضرورة : أنا الآن عاجزون عن خلق الاجسام . فلو قدرنا على إيجاد بعض الممكنات لوجب أن نقدر على إيجاد الأجسام . لأن القدرة على الشيء ، مع العجز عن إيجاد مثله : محال . والثالث : وهو أنه يلزم أن نقدر على خلق القدرة والحياة لأنفسنا . وذلك محال . لأن الخلق لا يصدر إلا عن الحي القادر . ولما كان حياً ، كان قادراً ، فامتنع خلق الحياة والقدرة فيه مرة أخرى ، لامتناع اجتماع المثلين . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن العبد لو قدر على إيجاد بعض الممكنات ، لقدر على إيجاد كلها ، وثبت : أنه لا يقدر على إيجاد كلها . فثبت : أنه لا يقدر على إيجاد شيء منها . وهو المطلوب
Aspects: As for the demonstration that it is impossible for the servant to be powerful over bringing into existence all possible things, there are aspects: First: The opponent concedes it. Second: We know by necessity that we are now incapable of creating bodies. So if we were able to bring into existence some possible things, we would have to be able to bring into existence bodies, because ability to do a thing along with inability to do its like is impossible. Third: It would entail that we are able to create power and life for ourselves. That is impossible, because creation only issues from the living, powerful. And since he is living, he is powerful, so creating life and power in him again is prevented due to the impossibility of the coincidence of two likes. So it is established by what we have mentioned that if the servant were able to bring into existence some possible things, he would be able to bring into existence all of them; and it is established that he is not able to bring into existence all of them. So it is established that he is not able to bring into existence any of them. That is the required result.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs (wujuh) demonstrating the impossibility of the servant having power over all possible things, which then refutes the earlier hypothetical conclusion that if he could create some, he could create all. The first proof: the opponent himself concedes this impossibility. The second proof: we know by necessity that we are currently unable to create bodies (like the sky, earth, etc.). If we could create any possible thing, we would have to be able to create bodies, because the ability to do a thing and inability to do its like is contradictory. Since we cannot create bodies, we cannot create any possible. The third proof: If the servant could create any possible, he would be able to create power and life in himself, which is impossible because only a living, powerful agent can create. Creating life and power in an already living, powerful agent would result in two likes coinciding, which is absurd. Therefore, the servant cannot create any possible thing at all. The conclusion is that the servant has no creative power whatsoever.

Key Terms
عجز (ajz)
خلق (khalq)
أجسام (ajsam)
قدرة (qudra)
حياة (hayat)
حي (hayy)
مثلان (mithlan)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A human can create some things (e.g., actions) but not others.
Correction: The argument shows that if a human could create any possible thing, they would have to be able to create all possible things, including bodies and their own life and power. Since that is impossible, they cannot create anything at all.

Checkpoint

A) The opponent concedes it

C) It would entail creating power in ourselves
D) Existence is a single concept

Key Takeaway
The servant cannot create any possible thing because if he could create any, he would have to create all, including bodies and his own life and power, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The proof says you cannot create life or power for yourself. Where in your daily routine do you unconsciously rely on your own strength, knowledge, or will as if you generated them yourself? What would it look like to consciously attribute every moment of life and every ability to Allah alone?

The Seventh Proof: Inability to Re-Create
Author provides seventh proof: if servant cannot re-create, he cannot create initially.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh proof that the servant cannot create actions, based on the impossibility of re-creation.

Core Idea
If a servant cannot re-create, he cannot create initially.

The seventh proof leverages common ground between the Ash'arites and their opponents: both agree that the servant cannot bring back an action once it ceases. The author argues that this inability logically extends to the initial creation. Let's follow the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Let the structure of the argument sink in before dissecting it.
2
Identify the agreed premise
The proof starts with an agreed point: the servant cannot re-create actions after they cease. Note that this is common ground with the opponents.
3
Understand the analogy
The proof argues: if the servant could create initially, he could re-create, because the essence of the action is the same at both times. Essences do not change with time.
This step is the core of the argument.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since it is agreed that the servant cannot re-create, and initial creation would imply ability to re-create, it follows that the servant cannot create initially.
The conclusion is reached by modus tollens.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان السابع : الخصوم وافقونا على أن العبد لا يقدر على إيجاد أفعاله بعد عدمها . فنقول : لو كان قادراً على الإيجاد ، لكان قادراً على الإعادة . لأن الحاصل عند الإعادة : عين ما كان حاصلا عند الابتداء . وماهية الشيء لا تختلف باختلاف الأوقات . فلو كانت قدرة العبد صالحة لتكوين ذلك الشيء وقت الابتداء ، لكانت صالحة لتكوينه في وقت الإعادة . لكنا توافقنا على أن قدرة العبد غير صالحة للإعادة ، فوجب أن لا تكون صالحة للابتداء
The seventh proof: The opponents agreed with us that the servant is not able to bring into existence his actions after their non-existence. So we say: If he were able to bring into existence, he would be able to re-create. Because what is obtained at re-creation is the very same thing that was obtained at the beginning. And the essence of a thing does not differ with the difference of times. So if the servant's power were valid for forming that thing at the time of beginning, it would be valid for forming it at the time of re-creation. But we have agreed that the servant's power is not valid for re-creation, so it must not be valid for beginning.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof (al-burhan al-sabi') that the servant cannot create any action. The argument is based on a point of agreement between the Ash'ari author and his opponents (likely the Mu'tazila): both agree that the servant cannot re-create an action once it has ceased to exist (i.e., bring it back after non-existence). The proof then argues: if the servant were able to create the action initially, he would also be able to recreate it, because the thing at re-creation is the same essence as at the beginning, and essences do not change with time. Since power is the same, if it sufficed for the initial creation, it should suffice for the re-creation. But both parties agree that the servant's power is not valid for re-creation, so it is also not valid for the initial creation. Therefore, the servant cannot create any actions.

Key Terms
إعادة (i'ada)
إيجاد (ijad)
ابتداء (ibtida')
تكوين (takwin)
ماهية (mahiyya)
قدرة (qudra)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Re-creation is fundamentally different from initial creation because the object has been non-existent in between.
Correction: The argument relies on the essence (mahiyya) being the same regardless of time. The state of non-existence does not alter the essence itself; thus, if a power is capable of producing an essence at one time, it is capable at any time.

Checkpoint
What is the key point of agreement between the author and the opponents in the seventh proof?
A) The servant can create some actions
B) The servant cannot re-create actions after their non-existence
C) Power is the same for creation and re-creation
D) Essences differ with time

Key Takeaway
The seventh proof shows that inability to re-create implies inability to create, due to the sameness of essence across time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly cannot create even a single action of yours, why do you still feel pride in what you accomplish? How would it change your gratitude to Allah if you internalized that even your smallest deed is from Him?
?
The proof shows that if you cannot recreate, you cannot create. Consider a sin you committed: could you bring it back? Then why do you claim ownership over it? How does this humble you?
?
This argument applies to every action you think you control. Are you willing to surrender the illusion of independent power? What would it cost you to admit that your very ability to move is a gift?

The Seventh Proof: Servant's Inability to Create
Al-Razi presents the seventh proof that the servant cannot create anything.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's seventh proof that the human servant cannot originally create any act, based on the logical implication from the inability to recreate.

Core Idea
If you can't recreate an act, you can't create it in the first place.

In this passage, Al-Razi presents a concise logical argument that the human being cannot be the creator of his own actions. The proof relies on a point of agreement between opposing schools: that the servant cannot recreate an act once it ceases to exist. From this admission, Al-Razi draws a powerful implication about original creation. Follow the argument step by step to see how it reaches its conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to understand the overall argument.
Pay attention to the logical structure: premise, implication, reason, conclusion.
2
Identify the agreed premise
Notice that the proof starts with a statement of agreement: both sides agree the servant cannot recreate an act after it ceases to exist.
This is the foundation of the argument.
3
Follow the implication chain
Trace the reasoning: if the servant could originally create, he could recreate (because essence is the same). Since he cannot recreate, he cannot originally create.
The key is the identity of essence across time.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
The passage does not assume recreation is impossible; it uses the opponent's own admission to prove original creation is also impossible.
Avoid the misconception that the proof is circular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان السابع
الخصوم وافقونا على أن العبد لا يقدر على إيجاد أفعاله بعد عدمها فنقول : لو كان قادراً على الإيجاد ، لكان قادراً على الإعادة . لأن الحاصل عند الإعادة : عين ما كان حاصلا عند الابتداء . وماهية الشيء لا تختلف باختلاف الأوقات . فلو كانت قدرة العبد صالحة لتكوين ذلك الشيء وقت الابتداء ، لكانت صالحة لتكوينه في وقت الإعادة. لكنا توافقنا على أن قدرة العبد غير صالحة للإعادة ، فوجب أن لا تكون صالحة للابتداء
The Seventh Proof: The opponents agree with us that the servant is not capable of bringing his acts into existence after their non-existence. So we say: If he were capable of bringing into existence (creation), he would be capable of recreation. Because what is obtained at recreation is the very same thing that was obtained at the beginning. And the essence of a thing does not differ with the difference of times. So if the servant's power were valid for the origination of that thing at the time of beginning, it would be valid for its origination at the time of recreation. But we agree that the servant's power is not valid for recreation, so it must be that it is not valid for beginning.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof that the servant (human being) cannot create any act. The author begins by stating a point of agreement with the opponents: the servant cannot bring his acts into existence after they have ceased to exist (i.e., cannot recreate them). The argument then proceeds: if the servant were capable of original creation (bringing something into existence from nothing), he would also be capable of recreation. The reason given is that the thing recreated is identical in essence to the thing originally created; essence does not change over time. Therefore, the same power that suffices for original creation should suffice for recreation. Since it is agreed that the servant lacks the power for recreation, it follows that he must also lack the power for original creation. This is a logical implication based on the identity of the act's essence across time.

Key Terms
قدرة
إيجاد
إعادة
ابتداء
ماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the proof assumes recreation is impossible for the servant, making it circular or arbitrary.
Correction: The proof does not assume recreation is impossible; it uses the opponent's own admission that recreation is impossible to argue that original creation must also be impossible. The reasoning is valid because of the identity of essence.

Checkpoint
According to the seventh proof, why does the servant's inability to recreate imply inability to originally create?
A) Because the original act is different from the recreated act.
B) Because the essence of the thing is the same in both original creation and recreation.
C) Because the opponents deny recreation is possible for God.
D) Because power is indivisible and cannot be used for two acts.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi shows that the servant cannot originally create acts because if he could, he would also be able to recreate them—which both sides agree he cannot.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have never been able to recreate a single act of yours—not even a word you've spoken. Does this proof force you to admit that even your 'original' actions are not truly under your power? If so, will you today stop claiming ownership of your deeds and instead attribute every movement to Allah, asking Him to make you a vessel of righteousness?
?
When you sin, you often say 'I did it myself.' But if you cannot even recreate a good deed, how can you claim full authorship of a sin? Can you honestly say you created that sin independently? How does this realization change the way you repent—will you now beg Allah not only for forgiveness but also for the power to have done otherwise?
?
This proof shows that your inability to recreate proves your inability to create originally. What act will you perform today—prayer, charity, or even a kind word—that you will consciously acknowledge as being entirely created by Allah through you? Write one specific act and commit to saying before it: 'This is from You, not from me.'

intermediate
8 min
Why the Servant Cannot Recreate His Own Act
Objection to seventh proof and its detailed refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical arguments against the possibility of a servant recreating his own act, and how this refutes the objection to the seventh proof.

Core Idea
Recreation by the servant is impossible because it would require either an overburdened power or shared power, both impossible.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection to the proof that the Creator of the world must be capable of recreation. The objector raises two challenges: first, that recreation of the non-existent is impossible, and second, that even if possible, the power for original creation does not guarantee power for recreation. The author then provides a detailed refutation, showing why the servant—unlike God—cannot recreate his own acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage (up to 'مانعا في الطرف الآخر') to understand the two challenges raised by the objector.
The first challenge is about the impossibility of restoring something that has completely perished. The second is about the non-necessity of ability to recreate from ability to create originally.
2
Identify the two divisions in the answer
Notice that after 'ثم نقول', the author divides the possibility of recreation by the servant into two exclusive options: either by the same power used originally, or by another power.
This is a classic logical division (taqsīm) to show both paths lead to impossibility.
3
Examine the 'same power' argument
Read the argument that the same power cannot be used because it would have to be directed to two objects at the same time, leading to infinite regress.
The key point: a finite power can only be directed to one object at a time.
4
Examine the 'other power' argument and conclusion
Read the argument that a different power cannot be used because two powers cannot produce one act. Then note the conclusion that the servant cannot recreate, and why this does not apply to original creation.
The conclusion reinforces the original proof by showing that the servant's inability is due to specific logical constraints that do not affect God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لا نسلم أن إعادة المعدوم جائزة في الجملة . سواء كان ذلك يمتنع في حق الله تعالى ، أو في حق العبد . وبيانه : أن الذي عدم فقد فني ولم يبق منه لا ذاته ولا حكم من أحكامه . فإذا حصل بعد ذلك شيء آخر ، فهو مغاير للأول ، وامتنع أن يكون عين ذلك الذي عدم . سلمنا : أن الإعادة في الجملة جائزة . لكن لم قلتم : إن القادر على الابتداء ، يجب أن يكون قادراً على الإعادة ؟ وتقريره : إن الابتداء يمتاز عن الإعادة بأمر من الأمور . فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن ما به حصل الامتياز يكون شرطاً في أحد الطرفين أو مانعا في الطرف الآخر ؟ ثم نقول : الفرق بين الإعادة وبين الابتداء . هو أنه لو صحت الإعادة من العبد ، لكان إما أن يقدر على إعادته بتلك القدرة ، التي بها أوجد ذلك الفعل في الابتداء ، أو بغير تلك القدرة . والقسمان باطلان . أما أنه إعادة ذلك الفعل بعين القدرة التي بها أوجده في الابتداء . فذلك لأن القدرة لا بد وأن تتعلق في كل وقت بإيجاد فرد من أفراد ذلك النوع . فلو تعلقت أيضا في وقت من الأوقات بإعادة ما عدم ، لكان قد تعلقت في الوقت الواحد ، في المحل الواحد من الجنس الواحد بأكثر من مقدور واحد ولو جاز ذلك لما كان عدد أولى من عدد . فيلزم جواز تعلقها بما لا نهاية له . وذلك محال . لأنه يلزم أن لا يبقى التفاوت بين القادر وبين الأقدر . فثبت : أن إعادة الفعل بعين القدرة التي حصل بها الإيجاد في الابتداء : محال . وأما أنه لا يمكن إعادته بقدرة أخرى. لأنه لو جاز أن تتعلق قدرتان بإيجاد مقدور واحد ، لجاز قيام كل واحد منهما بقادر آخر . وذلك يفضي إلى حصول مقدور ، بين قادرين. وهو محال . فثبت : أن العبد لو قدر على إعادة فعل نفسه ، لقدر عليه . إما بعين تلك القدرة وإما بقدرة أخرى وثبت أن كل واحد منهما محال فلزم القول : بأن العبد لا يقدر على الاعادة . وهذا العذر غير موجود في القدرة على إيجاد الفعل ابتداء . فثبت : أنه لا يلزم من قدرة العبد على الإيجاد ابتداء ، قدرته على إعادة ذلك الفعل . فهذا هو تقرير هذا الفرق .
If it is said: We do not concede that the recreation of the non-existent is permissible in general, whether that is impossible with respect to God the Exalted or with respect to the servant. Its explanation: that which has ceased to exist has perished and nothing remains of it, neither its essence nor any of its properties. So if something else occurs after that, it is different from the first, and it is impossible that it be the very same thing that ceased to exist. We concede: that recreation in general is permissible. But why do you say that the one capable of original creation must be capable of recreation? Its explication: original creation is distinguished from recreation by some matter. So why is it not permissible to say that that by which distinction occurs is a condition in one of the two sides or an obstacle in the other side? Then we say: The difference between recreation and original creation is that if recreation from the servant were valid, it would either be that he is capable of its recreation by that same power by which he brought that act into existence in the original creation, or by another power. Both divisions are invalid. As for the recreation of that act by the very same power by which he brought it into existence in the original creation, that is because power must in every time be directed to bringing into existence an individual of the individuals of that type. So if it were also directed at some time to recreating what has ceased to exist, it would have been directed in one time, in one substrate of the same type, to more than one object of power; and if that were permissible, then no number would be more appropriate than another number, so it would necessitate the permissibility of its being directed to infinitely many. That is impossible, because it would necessitate that there be no difference between the powerful and the more powerful. So it is established that the recreation of the act by the very power by which the original creation occurred is impossible. As for that it cannot be recreated by another power, because if it were permissible that two powers be directed to bringing into existence one object of power, it would be permissible for each of them to subsist in another powerful being, and that would lead to the occurrence of an object of power between two powerful beings, which is impossible. So it is established that if the servant were capable of recreating his own act, he would be capable of it either by that same power or by another power, and it is established that each of the two is impossible, so it is necessary to say that the servant is not capable of recreation. And this excuse does not exist in the power to bring the act into existence originally. So it is established that it does not follow from the servant's power to bring into existence originally his power to recreate that act. This is the elaboration of this distinction.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the seventh proof and its detailed answer. The objector first raises two points: (1) recreation of the non-existent is not permissible at all, because what has perished is gone entirely; (2) even if recreation is permissible, why must the ability to originally create imply ability to recreate, since there might be a distinguishing condition or obstacle? The author then provides a thorough rebuttal. He concedes recreation is possible in general but then addresses the core of the issue. He argues that if the servant were to recreate his own act, it would have to be either with the same power used originally or with a different power. The same power cannot be used because at any given time a power is directed to a specific individual of a type; if it were also directed to the recreation, it would be directed to two objects at once, leading to infinite regress—a logical impossibility. A different power cannot be used because two powers cannot produce one act; that would make one object of power dependent on two agents, also impossible. Since both possibilities are impossible, recreation by the servant is impossible. However, this does not affect the original creation scenario because the constraints of multiple objects or multiple powers do not apply there. Therefore, the objection fails to undermine the original proof.

Key Terms
إعادة
ابتداء
قدرة
مقدور
امتياز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Recreation of the non-existent is absolutely impossible, for both God and the servant.
Correction: The author concedes that recreation is possible for God, but proves it is impossible for the servant due to logical constraints on finite power.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why cannot the servant recreate his act by the same power used in original creation?
A) Because that power would be too weak for recreation.
B) Because that power would have to be directed to two objects at the same time, leading to infinite regress.
C) Because the original act is of a different type than the recreation.
D) Because God alone can recreate.

Key Takeaway
The servant's power is limited such that he cannot recreate his own act, because it would require either directing one power to multiple objects or sharing an object between two powers, both impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you rely on your own ability to repeat a good deed you once performed, do you assume that power is entirely yours? How does this lesson's proof that you cannot 'recreate' an act challenge that assumption?
?
If every act you produce is a new original creation by God's power, not your own recreation, how should that change your gratitude and humility after each act of obedience?
?
The proof argues that your power cannot handle two objects at once. In what areas of your life have you been acting as if your power is unlimited, trying to direct it to multiple goals? What must you entrust to God instead?

Refuting the Corrupt Analogy and Establishing I'ada
Author refutes the analogy and establishes the permissibility of recreation via necessary concomitants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author declares the analogy corrupt and how he defends the permissibility of recreation (i'ada) through the principle of necessary concomitants of the essence.

Core Idea
The analogy is corrupt because it oscillates; i'ada is permissible as a necessary concomitant.

The author engages with an opponent's analogy that denies the servant's ability to recreate his action. He first exposes the logical flaw in the analogy, showing it either fails to establish a distinction or begs the question. Then he provides a positive argument based on a fundamental kalam principle: once something is possible in one state, it remains possible in all states.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the two main parts: refutation of analogy and positive proof.
2
Identify the corrupt analogy
Find where the author says 'This analogy is corrupt' and understand why it oscillates between two options.
The analogy fails either due to a valid distinction or due to an invalid one.
3
Understand the assumption refutation
The opponent assumes the servant cannot recreate; the author rejects that assumption, causing the proof to collapse.
4
Learn the positive proof for i'ada
The author argues that permissibility of existence is a necessary concomitant of essence, so if i'ada is possible in one state, it is always possible.
This principle is foundational in kalam theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : هذا الذي ذكرتم قياس فاسد . لأن الفرق الذي ذكرناه . إن صح ، فقد بطل الجمع . وإن فسد ، منعنا الحكم في الأصل ، وقلنا : العبد يقدر على الإعادة . فهذا القياس دائر بين ظهور الفارق بين الأصل والفرع. وبين منع الحكم في الأصل ثم نقول : إنكم بنيتم هذه الحجة على أنا سلمنا أن العبد لا يقدر على إعادة فعل نفسه . ولو منعنا ذلك ، وحكمنا بأنه يجوز منه إعادة فعله ، فحينئذ لا يبقى دليلكم البتة والجواب : أما بيان أن الإعادة جائزة فهو أن جواز الوجود من لوازم الماهية ، فلما ثبت جواز إيجاده في بعض الأحوال ، وجب أن يدوم ذلك في كل الأحوال . قوله : ( إنه نفي محض ، فكيف يمكن الحكم عليه بهذا الحكم المخصوص ) ؟ قلنا : قولكم : بأنه لا يصح الحكم عليه : حكم عليه بامتناع الحكم عليه. ولما كان عدمه غير مانع من هذا الحكم ، فلم لا يجوز أن أيضاً من الحكم الذي ذكرناه ؟ قوله : ( الإعادة ممتازة عن الابتداء، بأمر ما فلم لا يجوز أن يكون ما به الامتياز ، شرطاً في أحد الطرفين ، وإلا لكان ذلك الوصف أيضاً مبتدءاً ، ويلزم التسلسل . وإذا كان ذلك الوصف عدماً محضاً ، امتنع دخوله في المقتضى لا يمنع وأما الفرق الذي ذكروه فهو بناء على أصولهم الفاسدة في أنه يمتنع حصول مقدور واحد ، بين قادرين. وفي أن القدرة الواحدة في الوقت الواحد ، في المحل الواحد ، من الجنس الواحد ، لا تتعلق إلا بمقدور واحد . وكل هذه الأصول عندنا فاسدة . قوله : ( لو ثبت فساد هذا الفرق ، منعنا الحكم وقلنا : العبد يقدر على الإعادة قلنا : هذا لا سبيل إليه لأن الأمة مجمعة على أن العبد لا يقدر على إعادة فعل نفسه، فالقول بأنه يقدر على هذه الإعادة يكون خرقاً للإجماع . وبالله التوفيق
Then we say: This analogy you have mentioned is corrupt. Because the difference we mentioned, if it is valid, then the combination is invalid. And if it is invalid, we deny the ruling in the original and say: The servant is able to recreate. So this analogy oscillates between the appearance of a difference between the original and the branch, and between denying the ruling in the original. Then we say: You have built this argument on the assumption that we concede that the servant is not able to recreate his own action. But if we deny that and rule that he is permitted to recreate his action, then your proof remains nothing at all. The answer: As for the demonstration that recreation is permissible, it is that the permissibility of existence is among the necessary concomitants of the essence. So when the permissibility of bringing it into existence in some states is established, it must continue in all states. His statement: "It is a pure negation, so how can one judge it with this specific judgment?" We say: Your statement that it is not valid to judge it is a judgment upon it of the impossibility of judging it. And since its non-existence is not an obstacle to this judgment, why is it not also permitted from the judgment we mentioned? His statement: "Recreation is distinguished from origination by something. So why is it not permissible that that by which the distinction occurs is a condition in one of the two sides? Otherwise that attribute would also be originated, and regress would follow. And if that attribute is a pure negation, its inclusion in the causative factor is impossible." That does not prevent. As for the difference they mentioned, it is built upon their corrupt principles: that it is impossible for a single object of power to occur between two powerful agents; and that a single power at a single time, in a single locus, of a single genus, relates only to a single object of power. All these principles are corrupt according to us. His statement: "If the corruption of this difference is established, then we deny the ruling and say: The servant is able to recreate." We say: There is no way to that because the community is unanimous that the servant is not able to recreate his own action, so saying that he is able to recreate that would be a breach of consensus. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an analogy (qiyas) used to deny the servant's ability to recreate his own action. The author first labels the analogy as corrupt (fāsid). He explains that the analogy depends on either a valid distinction between the original and the branch, in which case the combination is invalid, or an invalid distinction, in which case the ruling in the original case is rejected and the servant is declared able to recreate. The argument thus oscillates. Then the author notes that the opponent built the argument on the assumption that the servant cannot recreate his action; if that assumption is rejected, the proof collapses. To prove recreation is permissible, the author invokes the principle that the permissibility of existence is a necessary concomitant of the essence (jawāz al-wujūd min lawāzim al-māhiyya), so once permissibility is established in one state, it continues in all states. He then addresses an objection: how can a pure negation (nafī maḥḍ) be subject to a positive judgment? The response is that the objection itself judges the negation by declaring it impossible to judge, so the negation is not immune to judgment. Another objection is that recreation is distinguished from origination by something; if that something is a condition in one case, it would lead to infinite regress, and if it is a pure negation, it cannot be a cause. The author replies that this does not prevent the judgment. Next, he dismisses the difference (farq) the opponents rely on as based on corrupt principles: that a single object of power cannot occur between two agents, and that a single power relates only to a single object of power. These principles are rejected. Finally, the opponent argues that even if the difference is corrupt, the servant cannot recreate due to consensus; the author responds that consensus indeed establishes the servant cannot recreate his own action, so claiming otherwise breaches consensus. The passage ends with a prayer.

Key Terms
قياس فاسد
إعادة
جواز الوجود
لوازم الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponent assumes that the servant cannot recreate his action is a conceded premise.
Correction: The author denies that premise; if it is rejected, the opponent's proof collapses.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the basis for the permissibility of recreation (i'ada)?
A) The consensus of the community
B) The principle that permissibility of existence is a necessary concomitant of the essence
C) The rejection of the analogy
D) The ability of the servant

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of recreation rests on the principle that what is possible in one state remains possible in all states, as a necessary concomitant of the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, the author points out that the opponent's argument assumes something without justification. Are you holding any assumptions about your own limitations—in your faith, character, or ability to change—that you have never truly examined? Will you challenge at least one such assumption today with the same critical spirit?
?
The principle here is that if something is possible in one state, it must be possible in all states. Is there an area of your life where you have given up on change, thinking 'that was possible then, but not now'? How does this lesson compel you to reconsider that?
?
The author argues that the permissibility of existence is a necessary concomitant of the essence—it doesn't change. Reflect: Do you treat Allah's mercy and forgiveness as something that can be possible at some times but not others? What would change in your relationship with Him if you truly believed that His mercy is always possible, not contingent on your current state?

Answering Objections and Rejecting Opponent's Principles
Author answers objections and refutes opponent's principles and consensus claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author responds to objections about judging pure negations and the distinction between recreation and origination, and how he rejects the opponent's principles and consensus argument.

Core Idea
Even a pure negation can be judged; the distinction between i'ada and ibtida' does not preclude i'ada's permissibility.

The author now faces two objections: first, that a pure negation cannot be subject to a positive judgment, and second, that recreation is distinguished from origination by something that would lead to regress. He also counters the opponent's underlying principles (one object of power cannot be between two agents, etc.) and addresses the consensus argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to follow the series of objections and responses.
Notice the structure: objection, response; then another objection, response; then dismissal of principles and final consensus point.
2
Analyze the first objection and response
The objector says a pure negation cannot be judged; the author replies that the objection itself is a judgment.
This is a clever refutation by self-reference.
3
Analyze the second objection and response
The objector argues that recreation is distinguished from origination by something; if that something is a condition, it leads to regress; if it is a pure negation, it cannot be a cause. The author says this does not prevent the judgment.
The author does not elaborate further here.
4
Understand the rejection of principles and consensus
The author dismisses the opponent's principles (e.g., one object of power cannot be between two agents) as corrupt. He then acknowledges the consensus that the servant cannot recreate, but notes that this consensus is the ruling, not a proof against permissibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A pure negation cannot be subject to any judgment.
Correction: The author shows that the very claim 'it cannot be judged' is a judgment, so negations are not immune to judgments.

Checkpoint
What is the author's response to the objection that a pure negation cannot be judged?
A) He agrees and gives up the argument
B) He says the objection itself is a judgment
C) He says the negation is not pure
D) He ignores it

Key Takeaway
The author demonstrates that objections to i'ada are invalid, whether based on the nature of negation, the distinction from ibtida', or faulty principles; he ultimately acknowledges the consensus that the servant does not recreate, but that does not disprove permissibility in principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face an objection to a belief you hold, do you ever examine whether your own objection itself contains a hidden assumption? The author points out that objecting to a judgment is itself a judgment. Can you think of a time when your criticism of something revealed more about your own premises than about the thing you criticized? How can you learn to submit your own objections to the same scrutiny?
?
The author dismisses the opponent's distinction between origination and recreation without giving a detailed rebuttal—he simply says 'that does not prevent.' Are there areas in your faith where you have accepted a common distinction as definitive, without asking whether it truly bars the possibility? Where might you be limiting God's power by insisting on human categories?
?
The author acknowledges the consensus that the servant does not recreate his action, yet does not treat that as a proof of impossibility. In your own life, when you encounter a clear ruling or factual limitation, do you automatically assume that it reflects an absolute impossibility? Are there aspects of your relationship with Allah where you have mistaken 'I have never experienced this' for 'this cannot be'? Commit yourself today to distinguish between what is actually impossible and what you have simply not seen.

The Eighth Proof: Servant Cannot Create Motion
Author presents a proof that the servant is not the creator of his action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the eighth proof uses a dilemma and atomic theory of motion to show that the servant cannot be the creator of his action.

Core Idea
The servant cannot create his action because creation requires detailed knowledge of motion's atomic structure.

This lesson walks through the eighth proof from al-Matālib al-ʿĀliya. The proof presents a dilemma: if the servant creates his action, he must intend it either with or without a specific time. Both options lead to impossibility. The argument relies on the nature of motion as composed of indivisible moments and loci. By the end, you'll see why the servant cannot be the creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The proof is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the dilemma structure
Notice that the proof presents two possibilities: either the servant intends the action's existence without specifying a time, or he intends it in a specific time. These are the two horns of the dilemma.
The author argues both are impossible.
3
Analyze the first option
Focus on why intending absolute existence leads to absurdity: the action would either occur at all times or never. Understand why that is impossible.
This refutes the first horn.
4
Analyze the second option
Now examine the second horn: intending the action in a specific time. The author breaks down motion into indivisible occurrences and loci, arguing that intending such motion requires detailed knowledge that most people lack.
This refutes the second horn, establishing the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الثامن لو كان العبد موجداً لفعله . لكان إما أن يقصد إيجاده فقط ، أو يقصد إيجاده في الوقت المعين. والأول باطل . لأنه لو قصد مطلق الإيجاد من غير أن يقيد ذلك الإيجاد بوقت معين ، لم يكن وقوع ذلك الفعل في بعض الأوقات أولى من وقوعه في الوقت الآخر. فيفضي إلى أن يحصل حدوثه في كل الأوقات ، أو إلى أن لا يقع حدوثه في شيء من الأوقات . والكل محال . وأما الثاني : وهو أن يقال : إنه قصد إلى إيقاع الفعل في الوقت المعين . فهذا أيضاً باطل . لأن الحركة من أول المسافة إلى آخرها ، عبارة عن حصولات متوالية في أحياز متعاقبة . وكل واحد من تلك الحصولات غير منقسم . فإذا كان لا ماهية للحركة إلا حصولات غير منقسمة ، متوالية في أحياز متلاصقة ، غير منقسمة ، كان القصد إلى إيجاد تلك الحركة : قصداً إلى إيجاد تلك الحصولات الغير منقسمة في تلك الأحياز الغير منقسمة . والقصد إلى الشيء بدون العلم بماهية المقصود إليه : محال . فوجب أن يكون القاصد إلى تكوين الحركة عالماً بالضرورة بأنه يحدث ويدخل في الوجود حصولات متعاقبة غير منقسمة في أحياز متلاصقة غير منقسمة . ومعلوم أن هذا العلم : مقصود في حق الأكثرين فثبت : أن موجد الحركة ومكونها ، ليس هو العبد
The eighth proof: If the servant were the creator of his action, he would either intend its existence alone, or intend its existence in a specific time. The first is invalid, because if he intended absolute existence without restricting that existence to a specific time, then the occurrence of that action at some times would not be more likely than its occurrence at other times, leading to its occurrence at all times or to its non-occurrence at any time—both are impossible. As for the second, namely that he intended to bring about the action in a specific time, this too is invalid, because motion from the beginning of the distance to its end consists of successive occurrences (ḥuṣūlāt) in consecutive loci (aḥyāz). Each of those occurrences is indivisible. Since the essence of motion is nothing but indivisible occurrences, successive in contiguous indivisible loci, then intending to bring about that motion is intending to bring about those indivisible occurrences in those indivisible loci. Intending something without knowledge of the essence of the intended is impossible. Therefore, the one who intends to generate motion must necessarily know that he brings into existence successive indivisible occurrences in contiguous indivisible loci. It is well known that this knowledge is aimed at by most people? So it is established that the originator and generator of motion is not the servant.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth proof (al-burhān al-thāmin) against the claim that the servant is the creator of his own action. The proof is a dilemma. If the servant were the creator, then either he intends the absolute existence of the action without specifying a time, or he intends its existence in a specific time. The first option leads to absurdity: if no time is specified, then the action is equally likely at any time, resulting either in it occurring at all times or never—both absurd. The second option is also impossible because motion is composed of indivisible occurrences (ḥuṣūlāt) in indivisible loci (aḥyāz). To intend to create such a motion, one must have knowledge of those indivisible parts and their precise loci. But most people lack such detailed knowledge, so they cannot be the creators. Hence, the mover and generator of motion is not the servant. The proof relies on the atomic theory of motion (indivisible parts) and the principle that intention requires knowledge of the intended essence.

وقت معين
حركة
حصولات غير منقسمة
أحياز متلاصقة
علم ضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think that intending an action in a specific time is simple and straightforward.
Correction: The author shows that such intention requires detailed knowledge of the atomic structure of motion, which most people do not possess.

Checkpoint
What is the first possibility in the eighth proof's dilemma?
A) The servant intends his action in a specific time
B) The servant intends only the existence of his action without time specification
C) The servant intends both existence and time together
D) The servant does not intend at all

Key Takeaway
The eighth proof establishes that human beings lack the detailed knowledge of motion's atomic structure required to be the creators of their own actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
When you sin, do you attribute the action solely to yourself, forgetting that even the ability to move is a creation of Allah? How can this proof transform your repentance and reliance on His mercy?

The Ninth Proof: Effect of Power and Its Locus
Author begins a proof on the effect of the servant's power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the ninth proof regarding the effect of the servant's power and the two logical possibilities it examines.

Core Idea
The servant's power either leaves the action from its locus or it does not.

This lesson introduces the ninth proof (al-burhān al-tāsiʿ) on the relationship between human power (qudra) and the actions it supposedly effects. The author begins with a conditional statement that sets up two mutually exclusive possibilities. Understanding this distinction is key to following the proof's subsequent elimination of each option, ultimately showing that human power cannot truly cause actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once, then the literal translation. Note that the proof is introduced as 'the ninth proof' and begins with a conditional 'if... then' structure.
The passage is short but foundational for the argument that follows.
2
Identify the two alternatives
The passage presents two possibilities for how the effect of the servant's power could occur: either by 'leaving it from the locus of that power' or 'not by leaving it from its locus.' Mark these in the text.
These alternatives are exhaustive and mutually exclusive.
3
Reflect on the significance of 'locus'
Consider what 'locus of power' (maḥall al-qudra) means. In kalam, power inheres in the servant, and the action supposedly originates from that power. The proof questions whether the effect occurs by the action leaving that locus.
This concept is key to understanding the Ash'ari position on acquisition (kasb).
4
Prepare for the proof's next steps
The proof will now examine each possibility and show that both lead to impossibility, thereby refuting the claim that human power truly effects actions. This lesson sets the stage for that logical elimination.
You may need to refer to further lessons to see how the proof concludes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان التاسع لو أثرت قدرة العبد في حدوث الفعل . لكان أثرها في حدوث ذلك الفعل ، إما أن يكون بتركه من محل تلك القدرة ، أو لا بتركه من محلها .
The ninth proof: If the servant's power were to effect the occurrence of the action, then its effect on the occurrence of that action would either be by its leaving it from the locus of that power, or not by its leaving it from its locus.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage introduces the ninth proof (al-burhān al-tāsiʿ) regarding the effect of the servant's power (qudra). The proof begins with a conditional statement: if the servant's power were to effect the occurrence of the action, then its effect would be one of two possibilities: either the effect occurs by the power leaving the action from its locus (i.e., the action is detached from the power's locus), or it occurs not by leaving it from its locus. The text then breaks off, but the structure suggests the author will examine each possibility and show that both lead to impossibility, thus proving that the servant's power does not truly effect the action. This is a classic kalam argument about the relationship between power (qudra) and the locus of its effect.

Correction: The passage explicitly presents both alternatives: 'by leaving it' and 'not by leaving it.' Both are examined equally.

The Ninth Proof: The Servant's Power is Not an Agent
This passage presents the ninth proof that the servant's power cannot be the agent causing the action, dividing the possibility into two impossible cases: with or without the substrate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that the servant's power cannot be the agent causing an action, and why both possible scenarios (with or without the substrate) are impossible.

Core Idea
The servant's power cannot be an agent because it is contingent on a receptive substrate.

This passage presents the ninth proof from al-Matalib al-Aliyah that the servant's power is not the agent of the action. The argument divides the possibility into two exhaustive cases: either the power's effect occurs together with its substrate (the bodily locus) or without it. Both are shown to be impossible due to logical contradictions. Understanding this proof is key to the Ash'ari position on human action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two divisions.
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that the author states two possibilities: effect with the substrate or without it.
These two divisions cover all possibilities.
2
Understand why effect with substrate is impossible.
The substrate is merely receptive (possible) while the power is creative (necessary). Making the receiver a part of the creator is contradictory and impossible.
This is a key logical point: possibility contradicts necessity.
3
Understand why effect without substrate is impossible.
If the power were independent in its effect, it would also be independent in its existence, but it exists only in the substrate.
Independence in effect implies independence in existence.
4
See how both divisions lead to the conclusion.
Since both cases are false, the original assumption that the power is effective is false. Hence, the servant's power is not the agent.
This is a proof by exhaustive case analysis.
5
Note the broader implication.
The proof concludes that corporeal powers are not effective in existence at all, a point attributed to the philosophers.
This aligns with the Ash'ari denial of causal efficacy in created agents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان التاسع
وذلك هو المطلوب
لو أثرت قدرة العبد في حدوث الفعل . لكان أثرها في حدوث ذلك الفعل ، إما أن يكون بتركه من محل تلك القدرة ، أو لا بتركه من محلها .
والقسمان باطلان ، فالقول بأن قدرة العبد مؤثرة في حدوث الفعل : محال . إنما قلنا : إنه ممتنع أن يحصل ذلك التأثير بتركه من ذلك المحل لأن ذلك المحل ليس له إلا كونه قابلاً للصفات أعني : أنه لا يمتنع حصول هذه الصفات فيه ، ولا يمتنع لا حصولها أيضاً فيه . فلو جعلنا المحل جزءاً من المؤثر، لكنا قد جعلنا القابل جزءاً من الموجد . وذلك محال . لأن هذه القابلية طبيعتها طبيعة الإمكان الخاص . والموجدية طبيعتها طبيعة الوجوب . والإمكان الخاص ينافي الوجوب . وكون المنافي جزء من المنافي الآخر محال في العقول . وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع أن يكون تأثير القدرة في حدوث ذلك الفعل ، لا شركة من ذلك المحل . وذلك لأن وجود الشيء جزء من كونه موجداً لغيره فلو كانت هذه القدرة غنية في مؤثريتها عن ذلك المحل ، لكانت غنية أيضاً في وجودها عن ذلك المحل. لأنه متى كان المركب غنياً عن شيء ، كان كل واحد من بسيطته أيضاً غنياً عنه . ثبت : أن هذه القدرة لو كانت في مؤثريتها في الفعل غنية عن ذلك المحل ، لكانت في وجودها أيضاً غنية عن ذلك المحل . وذلك محال . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن القدرة لو كانت مؤثرة في وجود الفعل، لكان ذلك التأثير إما أن يكون بشركة من ذلك المحل ، أو لا بشركة من ذلك المخل ، وثبت كون كل واحد من هذين القسمين باطلاً . فثبت : أن القدرة غير مؤثرة في حدوث الفعل وهذا هو دليل الحكماء، على أن القوى الجسمانية غير مؤثرة في الوجود اصلا .
وبالله التوفيق
The ninth proof, and that is what is sought. If the servant's power were to effect the occurrence of the action, then its effect on the occurrence of that action would either be together with its substrate (the locus of that power) or not together with its substrate. Both divisions are false, so the statement that the servant's power is effective in causing the action is impossible. We say: It is impossible for that effect to occur together with its substrate because that substrate only has the property of being receptive to attributes, meaning: it is not impossible for these attributes to occur in it, nor is it impossible for them not to occur in it. If we make the substrate a part of the agent, then we have made the receiver a part of the creator, and that is impossible because the nature of this receptivity is the nature of particular possibility, while the nature of being a creator is the nature of necessity. Particular possibility contradicts necessity, and making a contradictory part of another contradictory is impossible in the intellect. And we say: It is impossible for the power's effect on the occurrence of that action to be without participation of that substrate, because the existence of a thing is part of its being a creator of another. If this power were independent in its effectiveness from that substrate, it would also be independent in its existence from that substrate, because whenever a composite is independent of something, each of its simples is also independent of it. It is established that if this power were independent in its effectiveness on the action from that substrate, it would also be independent in its existence from that substrate, and that is impossible. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned: if power were effective in the existence of the action, then that effect would either be with the participation of that substrate or without the participation of that substrate, and it is established that each of these two divisions is false. So it is established that power is not effective in causing the action, and this is the argument of the philosophers that corporeal powers are not effective in existence at all. And with God is the success.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This proof argues that the servant's power cannot be the agent causing the action. It divides the possibility into two: either the power's effect occurs together with its substrate (the body or locus of the power) or without it. Both are shown impossible. If the effect occurs with the substrate, then the substrate, which is merely receptive, becomes part of the agent, but receptivity (possibility) contradicts creative power (necessity). If the effect occurs without the substrate, then the power would be independent of the substrate in its effect, and thus also independent in its existence, which is false because the power exists only in the substrate. Therefore, the power cannot be effective. The proof concludes that corporeal powers are not effective in existence at all.

Key Terms
قدرة العبد
تأثير
محل
قابل
موجد
إمكان خاص
وجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the servant's power is an independent agent that can cause actions on its own, without needing the substrate (body).
Correction: This proof shows that power cannot be independent because its existence depends on the substrate; if it were independent in effect, it would be independent in existence, which is false.

Checkpoint
Why is it impossible for the servant's power to be effective together with its substrate?
A) Because the substrate is not real
B) Because the substrate is receptive (possible) and the power is creative (necessary), and it is impossible for a contradictory to be part of another
C) Because the substrate cannot be known
D) Because the power does not exist

Key Takeaway
The servant's power cannot be the agent of the action because both possible scenarios (effect with or without its substrate) lead to logical contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If the servant's power is not a real agent, then what is your role in your actions? Let this proof humble you: write down one way you will actively remind yourself that Allah is the true creator of your deeds, starting today.

The Tenth Proof: A Short Argument by Contradiction
A brief proof by contradiction showing the impossibility of the servant creating his own action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a simple argument by contradiction (burhan al-luzum) refutes the claim that the servant creates his own actions.

Core Idea
If we created our actions, we would never experience anything we didn't will — but we do.

This proof is remarkably concise yet powerful. It uses the logical form of consequence (luzum) to show that the claim that servants create their actions leads to a false outcome. Since the outcome is false, the claim itself must be false. Let's walk through this tight argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the concise and tight logical structure.
This proof is only two lines long, but every word carries weight.
2
Identify the two parts: ملزوم and لازم
The ملزوم (antecedent) is the claim that the servant creates his action. The لازم (consequent) is that he would never encounter anything except what he willed. See how they are connected by logical necessity.
Understanding this cause-effect relationship is key.
3
Understand why the لازم is false
Think of examples from your own experience. Do you always get what you will? Or do you sometimes experience things you do not will? The falsity of the consequent is obvious once you reflect on everyday life.
This refutes the claim that the servant is the creator of his actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان العاشر
لو كان فعل العبد يحدث بإيجاد العبد ، لوجب أن لا يجد إلا ما أراده العبد . واللازم باطل ، فالملزوم مثله
The tenth proof: If the servant's action were to occur by the servant's creation, then it would be necessary that he does not encounter except what the servant willed. But the consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This is a very brief proof by contradiction (burhan al-luzum). It states that if the servant were the creator of his own actions, then he would never encounter (experience) any action except what he himself willed. That is, all his actions would conform to his will. But this is false because we often experience actions that we do not will or that happen against our will. Therefore, the premise (that the servant creates his own actions) is false.

Key Terms
إيجاد العبد
لازم
ملزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if the servant creates his action, he must will it, so the consequence is true.
Correction: The proof shows that we sometimes experience actions we do not will, so creation cannot be by us.

Checkpoint
According to the tenth proof, what false consequence follows if the servant created his own action?
A) The action would be eternal
B) The servant would never encounter any action except what he willed
C) The action would be impossible
D) The servant would have no will

Key Takeaway
The servant does not create his own actions because we often experience actions that we do not will, contradicting the necessary consequence of such a claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself frustrated by something you said or did that you did not truly will, do you stop to realize that Allah alone created that action? How does this awareness change your response — from regret to turning back to Him?
?
If you truly internalized that every breath, every movement, every word you speak is created by Allah and not by your own power, how would it change your pride in your achievements and your despair over your failures? Commit today to affirming Allah as the sole Creator of all actions, even the ones you dislike.

Tenth Proof: Actions Not Created by the Servant
Author proves actions are not created by the servant through a conditional argument, with objection and partial answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that human actions cannot be independently created by the servant, and the response to the objection about ignorance leading to erroneous beliefs.

Core Idea
If the servant created his actions, he would always get what he intends; he doesn't, so he doesn't create them.

This lesson presents the tenth proof from al-Matalib al-Aliyah, which argues that human actions cannot be created by the servant because what actually occurs often contradicts the servant's intention. The proof is followed by an objection and its refutation, establishing that the initial ignorance must be created by God.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof structure
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Identify the conditional statement (if servant creates, then only intended actions occur), the explanation of the conditional, and the evidence that disproves it.
The proof is a modus tollens: if P then Q, not Q, therefore not P.
2
Examine the evidence
Look at the three examples: seeking true knowledge but getting ignorance, intending faith but getting disbelief, and the inability to perfectly replicate a line of writing. Notice how each shows the servant does not always get what he intends.
These are empirical observations.
3
Understand the objection
Read the objection: the servant's erroneous belief that his false conviction is knowledge explains why he chose it. See how the objector tries to preserve human choice.
The objection assumes ignorance can be chosen knowingly.
4
Analyze the first answer
The first answer argues that the initial ignorance must itself be explained; if it traces back to another ignorance, infinite regress follows; if chosen from the start, it's impossible. Therefore the first ignorance is created by God.
This establishes God's control over the origin of error.
5
Note the incomplete second answer
The second answer begins by stating that the servant has absolutely no choice, but it cuts off. This part is incomplete in the source.
This may be supplemented from other pages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان العاشر
لو كان فعل العبد يحدث بإيجاد العبد ، لوجب أن لا يجد إلا ما أراده العبد . واللازم باطل ، فالملزوم مثله
بيان الشرطية : أن العبد لما كان قادراً على الأفعال المختلفة ، كان رجحان البعض على الباقي لا يكون إلا بقصده، فوجب أن لا يقع ، وان لا يحدث إلا ما قصده وأراده . وبيان أنه ليس كذلك : أن العبد يقصد تحصيل العلم الحق ، والاعتقاد الصواب ، فلا يحصل له ذلك ، بل يحصل له الجهل والباطل . ويقصد الإيمان ، فيحصل له الكفر . بل نقول : الإنسان إذا كتب سطراً ، فلو أتي بجميع حيل الدنيا ، حتى يكتب سطرا آخر ، يشابه الأول ، في الكيفيات المحسوسة ، لعجز عن ذلك . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أنه لو كان فعله بإيجاده ، لما وقع إلا ما قصد إيقاعه . وثبت : أنه ليس كذلك ، فوجب أن لا يكون فعله بإيجاده . وهذه الدلالة أخذتها من قول أمير المؤمنين علي بن أبي طالب رضي الله عنه : ( عرفت ربي بنقص العزائم ، وفسخ الهمم . )
فإن قيل : إنه إنما حصل له هذا الاعتقاد المخصوص ، لأنه ظن ان هذا الاعتقاد علم وحق وصواب. فلهذا السبب اختاره وحصله ورضي به . فالحاصل : أنه إن علم كونه جهلاً ، لما رضي به ، ولكنه لما اعتقد فيه أنه هو العلم . لا جرم رضي به واختاره .
والجواب : إن هذا باطل من وجهين :
الأول : إنه إنما قصد تحصيل هذا الاعتقاد . لأنه كان قد اعتقد أن هذا الاعتقاد : علم . فلولا تقدم ذلك الجهل ، وإلا لما رضي بهذا الجهل الثاني . فنقول : ذلك الجهل الأول ، كيف حصل ؟ فإن كان ذلك لتقدم جهل آخر ، لزم التسلسل . وإن كان ذلك لأنه اختار الجهل وارتضاه لنفسه ابتداء . فهذا محال . فلم يبق إلا أن يقال : الجهالات تترقى إلى جهل أول ، خلقه الله تعالى في العبد ابتداء . وذلك هو المطلوب
الوجه الثاني : أن من أنصف : علم أنه لا اختيار للعبد البتة في حصول
The tenth proof: If the servant's action occurred by the servant's creation, then nothing would occur except what the servant willed. The consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise false. Explanation of the conditional: Since the servant is capable of various actions, the preference of one over the rest only occurs by his intention. So it is necessary that nothing occurs or happens except what he intended and willed. And the proof that it is not so: the servant intends to obtain true knowledge and correct belief, but he does not obtain that; rather, he obtains ignorance and falsehood. And he intends faith, but disbelief occurs to him. Rather, we say: a person writes a line; if he brings all the devices of the world to write another line similar to the first in sensible qualities, he would be incapable of that. So it is established by what we mentioned: if his action were by his creation, then nothing would occur except what he intended to bring about. And it is established that it is not so. So it is necessary that his action is not by his creation. And this indication I took from the saying of the Commander of the Faithful 'Ali ibn Abi Talib, may God be pleased with him: "I recognized my Lord by the failure of determinations and the dissolution of resolves." If it is said: He only obtained this particular belief because he thought that this belief is knowledge, truth, and correctness. For this reason he chose it, obtained it, and was pleased with it. So the result: if he knew it to be ignorance, he would not be pleased with it, but because he believed it to be knowledge, he was inevitably pleased with it and chose it. The answer: This is false in two respects: First: He only intended to obtain this belief because he had believed that this belief is knowledge. Were it not for the precedence of that ignorance, he would not have been pleased with this second ignorance. We say: how did that first ignorance occur? If it was due to the precedence of another ignorance, an infinite regress follows. And if it was because he chose ignorance and approved of it for himself from the beginning, that is impossible. So nothing remains except to say that ignorances trace back to a first ignorance which God, exalted, created in the servant from the beginning. That is the desired conclusion. Second: Whoever is fair knows that the servant has absolutely no choice in the occurrence of...
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth proof (البرهان العاشر) that human actions are not created by the servant. It begins with a conditional statement: if the servant created his own action, then only what he wills would happen; but the consequent is false, so the antecedent is false. The conditional is explained: since the servant has power over multiple actions, preference for one over another must be by his intention (قصد); so only intended actions would occur. Reality contradicts this: the servant intends true knowledge but gets ignorance, intends faith but gets disbelief. Even in writing, one cannot perfectly replicate a line. These examples show that actions are not under the servant's full control, so they cannot be created by him. The author attributes this argument to 'Ali (رضي الله عنه). An objection then arises: the servant's mistaken belief that his erroneous conviction is knowledge explains why he chose it. The answer refutes this in two ways. The first way argues that the initial ignorance (that led to the choice) must itself be explained: if it traces back to a prior ignorance, infinite regress results; if it was chosen freely, that is impossible. Therefore, the first ignorance must be created by God. The second way (incomplete in the text) begins by stating that the truly fair-minded person knows the servant has absolutely no choice in what occurs.

Key Terms
إيجاد
قصد
شرطية
جهل
تسلسل
اختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this argument denies human action altogether, claiming humans have no role.
Correction: The argument does not deny that humans perform actions; it denies that actions are independently created by human power. Actions are created by God while humans acquire them (kasb), consistent with Ash'ari theology.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the conditional (شرطية) in the tenth proof?
A) The servant is capable of various actions, so preference requires intention, meaning only intended actions would occur.
B) The servant always gets exactly what he intends.
C) The servant cannot write two identical lines because of lack of power.
D) The servant's power is independent of God's creation.

Key Takeaway
The tenth proof shows that since humans do not always get what they intend, their actions cannot be created by them; instead, God is the sole creator, and the initial ignorance that leads to error is also created by God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever desperately intended to do good—to pray sincerely, to speak kindly, to understand a truth—but instead found yourself failing or falling into the opposite? How does this lesson confront your assumption that you are the master of your own actions?
?
Think of a time you were absolutely certain you were right, only to later discover you were mistaken. Where did that first ignorance come from? Can you trace it back to a cause within you, or must you admit that God created it in you? How does that realization humble your pride?
?
If even your failures and errors are ultimately created by God, then what is left for you to claim as your own? What does this lesson demand of you in terms of reliance on God and gratitude for every good that occurs through you?
